THE RED PAGE.
Regarding Mahony’s picture at Sydney
Society of Artists’ show—
Did you notice the edge of the precipice in “To
the Bitter End”—the exact edge? The undermost
centaur has not an inch of brown heath or barren
rock between his hind-leg and eternity. Mahony
has strained the situation till it snaps. Similarly,
another artist made an exceedingly pretty little
moonlight sketch for Barcroft Boake’s poems, and
the moon is introduced as evidence of good faith,
though the quality of the tones and the manner of
reproduction have taken all the moony virtue out
of it—there is moonlight everywhere in the picture
but in the moon. Similarly, a very promising
young Queensland author, having to poison a
woman in a Bulletin story not long ago, poisoned
her in the drawing-room and dragged her right
against her child’s cot in the bedroom to die
—distilling the agony to the last bitter
drop. Similarly, the one charming picture in the
newly-established Brisbane Art Gallery (and it
doesn’t belong to the Gallery ; it is lent by Sam.
Griffith) is called “An Ice-Breaker on the Elbe.”
(Without knowledge, one infers that at Spring’s
advent a powerful bluff-bowed steamer is set to
work to hasten the disintegration of the floating
ice somewhere between Hamburg and Cuxhaven,
and that in the channel she outs follows a line of
released vessels just as the relief squadron followed
through the Mountjoy’s breach in the Londonderry
boom.) Ice-floes lie under a lowering sky, with a
dull-red sun shining over the vessel to shimmer
wonderfully down murky depths of foreground.
Behind, one dimly sees the following fleet. The
atmosphere of the whole is perfect—it looks like
truth, yet truth idealised in a most effective com-
bination of color. But the ice-breaker is mathe-
matically in the middle of the picture, and the sun
glows through the haze mathematically above the
middle of the vessel; and this gives any properly-
constituted mind a feeling of irritation which all
the charm of the work can hardly conquer.
Why? Because you get a glimpse of the man
behind the work, deliberately pulling certain
strings in order to get certain effects ; and the
human intelligence is so built that it hates to
think of itself as a marionette on a wire the
thought dissolves all one’s honest pride of inde-
pendence.
* * « *
[The Brisbane Gallery temporarily occupies an
upper room of the Town Hall; and the local com-
plaint is that about three-fourths of the Govt, suba
sidy goes in management exes., and only the casual
remainder in pictures A dozen tolerable paint-
ings hang on the walls—two landscapes by E. W.
Christmas being about the most satisfactory.
“The Legitimate Drama,” by T. W. Couldery,
contains a nice lot of well-drawn figures ; and most
of them don’t look like models with pokers down
their backs. Godfrey Rivers’ figures in a wool-
Bhed picture do—every one stands rigidly “ just
so,” as if the artist had taken off a photographer’s
camera-cap to them and said, “ Now . . .
steady ! 1!” “An Eviction,” by B. F. Fletcher,has
the same manufactured air—as if every figure had
been studied separately and slid into its place on
the canvas to suit the painter’s arbitrary notion of
what an eviction our/ht to look like. Griffith’s
Icebreaker ” (H. Gayaston, artist) is the only
really valuable item ; with the Christian pair good
seconds.]
W 9 m +
In all these instances, the artists are in error.
They are showing themselves not artists, but
mechanics —stage-carpenters, contriving that the
pantomime fairy shall fly up the precise centre of
the stage ; that the side-scenes shall come forward
precisely the same distance (who hasn’t seen an
anxious carpenter hitching his scenes back or for-
ward after the curtain rises, with the instinct of a
careful mother hitching her little girl’s frock
straight ?). The stage-ideal throughout, indeed,
is in grotesque conformity with the erroneous
notion that order is heaven’s first law. The hou
must always die in a particular patch of lime-
light; slow music must always lead up to the
catastrophe; in Sheridan’s time, the heroine
always went mad in white satin.
Puff. Now Tilburina comes in stark mad in white satin.
Sneer. Why in white satin ?
Puff. O Lord, sir—when a heroine goes mad, she
always goes into white satin.
But this continual conformity with bourgeois
conceptions of fitness is barbarously inartistic.
The outr4 is not necessarily better, but let us wor-
ship the outrd rather than the obvious. The
Scriptural chaos must have been a lot more
attractive than the Scriptural universe de-
picted in some old Bibles —as regularly wooden
as a row of skittles. When in England
a while ago the writer stayed a short time
with an estimable English family which
always, when eating, cleaned its plates. (Cleaning
one’s plate seems to be a well-marked English
trait.) Being an Australian, he never cleaned his
plate; and on the third day the head of the family
kindly took him to task. It irritated the excellent
British citizen to see an uncleaned plate on a
British citizen’s table. So at some personal in-
convenience the writer cleaned his plate until the
very last meal; and then the Imp of the Perverse
insisted on him leaving a spoonful ®f soup, and a
fragment of joint, and a morsel of pudding, and
a heel-tap of ale. He felt instinctively that the
farewell was saddened by those uncleaned plates ;
and, much to his regret, his relations with that
estimable British family have never been quite so
cordial since. And yet one feels in the marrow
of one’s bones that an artist should never dean
his plate.
Mr. Stephens objects to the ‘ brutal truth
primarily beoause it ‘makes us wretched.’ The
inference that the poet has no right to make people
wretched opens up a big question. A good many
honest people believe that the poet should be orna-
mental, not useful; that he should have privileges,
not duties. He should hold himself aloof from
the stress and strain of life, tuning his ears to
catch its harmonies, not its discords; living for
Art’s sake solely, contemplating abstractions. He
should deal only with the rosy-hued and aesthetic.
He should sit at the feet of power, and chant its
praise, and soothe its conscience, happy in his
sycophancy. He should sing of the greatness of
kings and the pride of power—of the goodness of
the deity and the moral ordination of society.
‘ God’s in his Heaven ; all’s right with the world.’
Aud if he should veuture to couch on the ‘ brutal
truth ’ —the tabooed side of life—it must be with
lavender kid gloves on his fingers, using only
the most delicate, distautly-suggestive language,
so as not to derile his sacred calling and hurt the
tender susceptibilities of his readers. To use Mr.
Stephens’s words, ‘he must intensify the present-
ment of truth in the very act of debrutalismg it.’
But there are also a good many honest people
who believe that the poet, after all, has a higher
mission. They believe chat he should come down
among his fellows, and be one of them ; that his
duty co society (which should be above his duty Co
himself) demands that he shall give some share of
the gift that is in him to the making of the world
even ever so little a better place for his having
lived in ic. They believe that he should write of
things as he finds them, aud that it is better to
snarl at a wrong than to wail at it. They believe
that it is almost a crime for a man to close his
eyes to the injustice that confronts him every-
where, or to attempt to gloss it over with equi-
vocal phrases. They believe that the function of
the poet is not solely to please, not solely to dally
with the pleasant side of things ; that he may
seriously employ his art, if he choose, by writing
a song now and then to help along tiie better
times to come. They believe that the right can
look after itself well enough—is being looked after
well enough, and lias been ; and that the victims
of wrong, and injustice, and oppression, want all
the help they can get, artistically or otherwise.
The ‘ brutal truth ’ told by poets of this school
jars on the artist in Mr. Stephens and makes the
man in him wretohed. It is not Art! You had
better pack up your poetic swag ‘ if your faculties
stop short at that.’ There is one condition, how-
ever, under which Mr. Stephens will admit that
the ‘brutal truth’ may be poetically rendered,
and that is in rose-colored verses. The poet who
insists on dealing with the world as he finds it
‘must learn to express the most dreadful senti-
ments in a pleasant way.’
* • * *
Again, a strong and growing note in much of
the verse that one meets with nowadays is simply
the poetic expression of a starved sexuality. It
is very noticeable in verses above the names of
women. They put it pretty plainly, oftentimes,
but none the less poetically, pathetically, or artis-
tically. While it is a wail it is all right, it
appears; but when there is a recognition of the
social injustice behind it, and consequently a snarl
in the wail, then it comes into Mr. Stephens’s
category of the unpoetical.
Yet how can anyone whose mind has acquired
the -scientific habit of looking straight at
the things he sees with naked eyes avoid
writing of these same things as he finds them ?
It is no doubt very comforting to oneself and to
one’s readers to be able to see only the brighter
side of things. But which, after all, is the higher
type of man—the one who cherishes delusions and
shuts his eyes and blinds his readers to half the
facts of life, or the man who dares to face the
whole truth of things as far as his powers can
help him? Isn’t one mentally plucky and the
other a shirker?
Now, you can’t separate the poet from the man
in these matters, any more than you can separate
the poet from his verses.
* * * *
The great mistake that Mr. Stephens makes,
however, is in the belief that the ‘brutal-truth-cum-
anti-cwwi-Marseillaise’ school of poets is purely de-
structive in its writings. If society is to be saved,
scholars in the ‘brutal truth’ school believe it
must have practical ideals to work for; and the
highest of these is the fellowship of man, and its
consequence, as taught by Confucius, that no one
has a right to be happy while his fellows are
miserable. It is just a stage ahead in social evo-
lution that they have an eye on—not ‘ Europe,’
and not ‘ nor-nor-westerly’any more than sou-sou-
westerly. It is only the obstacles to social evolu-
tion to which they are ‘ anti.’ It is only here and
there, at the clearing away of one of these, that
they write ‘ Marseillaise ’ and ‘ Workers, Arise!’
poems.
* * • *
Mr. Stephens is also a social Salvationist, and
these are his views of the poet as saviour
‘There is more relish of salvation, even of social
salvation, in a pretty, fanciful, well-sustained
effort like that of Boake’s, “The Box Tree’s Love,”
than in a thousand sonnets to “Divine Discon-
tent.” The one is at least achievement—of a sort,
and a good sort, too ; the other, however iEolian,
is just wind on thestomach,’ And yet Swinburne
used to be a ‘ brutal truth ’ poet, and an ‘anti
and so was James Thomson, and Shelley, and
William Morris, and many another. It was
Swinburne who penned the line —
Of Christian creeds that spit on Christ.
There is no rosy tint in this—no equivocation, no
quibbling with words, no ‘intensifying the pre-
sentment of truth in the very act of brutalising
it.’ Yet Swinburne is regarded as a great poet.
* « • •
As a matter of faot, the Romantic school is re-
sponsible for a good deal of the ‘ anti-cum-brutal-
truth-cum-Marseillaise ’ poetry that is abroad.
Its one-sided verses are taken seriously by many
generous, high-souled youths and maidens who
set out to face the facts of life, equipped with
rosy-tinted ideals of love and life which they
fondly hope to realise. These believe that love,
as the Romantic school paints it, is an attainable
reality, that life is a pleasant place of dreams,
and that society is organised on a strictly moral
basis, so that the good man shall receive terrestrial
exaltation, and the bad terrestial damnation.
When such an ill-equipped individual clashes
against the realities of life, and his ideals tumble
one after another about him, what wonder if, in
his soul-revolt, he lift a voice of warning to those
who are to follow him? What wonder if, when
he finds everywtiere large-hearted, noble men and
women laboring to make the world a better place to
live in than it is, he should range himself among their
ranks and help to destroy much that deserves
destruction —only that a reconstruction may be
effected? What wonder if he becomes an ‘anti’
in this sense?—for this is how ‘ antis ’ are largely
recruited and made. The Romantic school makes
them and then damns them.
There are many right good and beautiful things
still left on earth, but they are no more exolusi vely
the property of poetry than their antitheses. So
much of a poet’s work is a matter of tempera-
ment, a matter of heredity and environment.
James Thomson could no more have written ‘The
Bab Ballads ’ than \V. S. Gilbert could have
written ‘The City of Dreadful Night.’ And
because James i hotnson, whose ‘ brutal truth ’
makes us wretched, could not ‘ intensify the
presentment of truth in the very act of debrutalis-
ing it,’ he should have ‘ tried some other sphere
than poetry ’! Which is very questionable.
There is so much rebellion, revolution, and
despair in the world, and one poet caters for it;
there is so much hope and joy in the world, and
another sings it. There is a demand for ‘ brutal
truth ’ and one school supplies it; there is a desire
for rosy tints, and another school supplies those.
fSo that every phase of human feeling has its
corresponding poetry or school of poetry. And
for one of these schools to try and oust the other
isn’t fair or brotherly.
AJways remembering Mr. Stephens’s great poetic
achievements, the young Australian poet might
well be justified in saying : ‘ I resent the right cf
the author of “ Convict Once,” or of any one else,
to tell me what I must write about. I have just
as good a right to be in the universe as he has, and
to take my own views of it. I have just as good
a right to select from the wealth of material
everywhere the particular features that suit my
fancy, or my bent, or my temperament—
whatever you like to call it—and sing about
them to my heart’s content. If he prefers to
sing about the Right of things, the pleasant side
of life, the Romantic, I don’t deny him the right
to his preference ; and if I prefer to sing about
the Wrongs of life, the injustice, the oppression,
I deny him the right to deny me my preference.
I have just as much right to my note in the gamut
of human feeling as he has to his, and in thegreat
catholicity of tastes vve can each find our
audience, great or small, and our due share of
appreciation—always provided we deserve it.
There is plenty of room for us all. Let us each
stick to our branch in the tree of poetry, and sing
our best in our own way. But don’t—if you
please, Mr. Stephens—don’t order me off my limb
and decry it as rotten, and don’t describe my
afflatus as ‘wind on the stomach’ because it
doesn’t chime with yours, or your ideas.
— * .
And, on the same subject, ‘P. Luftig ’ —
Mr. Brunton Stephens, on the Red Page
‘ Too many of our young poets sit down with the
deliberate intention of telling the “ brutal truth.”
It is all very well to tell the brutal truth, hut if
your faculties stop short at this, you had better
try some other sphere of usefulness than poetry,’
With the latter portion of which criticism one can,
of course, heartily agree. At the same time it
must be remarked that an individual who can
only tell the brutal truth in brutal language
(essentially a bullock-driver’s qualification) has
certainly no claim to the title of poet, and even
less claim still to be the object of the animadver-
sions of a penman of Mr. Stephens’s calibre. I
am inclined, however, to look upon the tendency
Mr. S. denounces as rather a healthy one. . . .
It is, unfortunately, only too true that poets in
general, from the polished Horace and the courtly
Virgil down to glorious Dryden and the laureate
rhymesters of a later day, have seldom been con-
spicuous for their honest devotion to the dear
goddess Truth. As a rule they have been re-
markably loyal to the loaves and fishes.
Generally speaking, they have basked in
the sun of royalty right gladly ; have fed
only too willingly at the hand of Dives
the magnificent; have even been known, in true
Lazarus-humility of spirit, to eat greedily of the
crumbs that fell from the rich man’s table. We
have had such a surfeit of poetic sycophancy for
countless generations that a little lively rebellion
among even the very diminutive hards of the fag-
end of the 19th century is not altogether a thing
deplorable. (I trust, however, they won’t ‘Awake!
and arise ’ to any considerable extent oii the
strength of this.)
• 9 • •
Mr. Stephens is no friend of the ‘antistic’
school. But the poetry of the lesser hard is not
bad because it is ‘ antistio,’ but simply because the
bard himself is particularly small potatoes. Hein-
rich Heine is essentially ‘ antistio ’; so is Shelley ;
so is Byron ; and there be few loftier names than
these in the poetic calendar. I venture also to add
that their poetic reputation would have been in
no wise diminished had they been ‘pro-istic’
(pardon the clumsy vocable) instead of ‘antistic,’
for Art is Art always, whether the human
habitation she occupies for the moment nub itself
Radical red or blue-blooded Tory. Wherefore
the devotees of the ‘antistic ’ school may go on
their way rejoicing, for the peculiar view they
take of things won’t improve or depreciate the
value of their poetic output to any remarkable
degree. True, the ‘anti-lay’ palls terribly at
times; so does the loyal anthem ; so does the
jubilee ode. Yet both ‘anti-lay’ and loyal
anthem can rise into the region of the Sublime
when Ronget de Lisle intones the one and
Dryden the other.
J • * • *
‘ It is the poet’s mission to make society worth
saving,’ Mr. S. continues. Undoubtedly ! And
surely the songs of a Burns (who was ‘antistic’ to
the backbone), the songs that breath a wholesome
hatred to every form of sham and fiunkeyism,
have done something to make society worth the
saving. Surely the man who fights the Wrong is
doing, in some sort, a battle for the. Right—and
the Beautiful. Surely Hugo (‘antistic’ to t.ie
very core) did a little to redeem society from the
curse of injustice and hideousness that beset
it. The unfortunate side of your jovial,
genial, optimistic bard is that he either
can't see or ivon't see that society is in
need of any Salvation at all. . . . This, however,
is beside the main question, viz.—the merits
and demerits of the ‘ antistic ’ school. The real
truth appears to be that creative poetical power
is entirely independent of pessimistic or opti-
mistic bias; that the artist is the artist always—
whether he be ‘ pro-istic ’ or ‘antistic;’ whether
his peculiar bent make him for the nonce fertile
in mellifluous benediction or gorgeously fecund m
varied execration.
‘ K.’ writes—
In a recent Red Page Mr. Brunton Stephens
discourses on Australian poets and Australian
poetry, kindly but severely. It is hard to get a
definition of what Australian poetry really is. If
it is poetry written by natives of the continent,
then Brunton Stephens and Gordon are out of it.
If it is poetry written upon distinctively Austra-
lian subjeots, then there will not be much Austra-
lian poetry left after you have eliminated the
essential elements common to humanity. Aus-
tralian poetry should really be that part of the
world’s poetry for which Australia is in some way
or other responsible. And it isn’t a very impor-
tant part when you come to sum it up.
When a poet like Brunton Stephens begins to
speak about Australian poetry it is good to listen,
and if it may be, learn. That he is at variance
with some of the younger Australian poets is only
to be expected. Differences of opinion between
the older generation and the younger lie in the
nature of things. Generalised, the dispute be-
tween Brunton Stephens and the young school of
Australian poets is the dispute between the
Romantic and the Realistic schools of Art. Par-
ticularised, it seems that the modern socialistic
idea is pervading Australian poetry. Its mani-
festations are bugbears called ‘ brutal truth ’ and
‘ anti ’ and ‘ Marseillaise ’ —none of which, accord-
ing to Brunton Stephens, ever can be poetry.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A LEGEND OF THE DARGO.
It was on the Upper Dargo, in the spring of eighty-
four,
That Cargoola township boasted a Salvation Army
corps;
Which was needed very badly, for the Upper
Dargo then
Contained a population of most irreligious men.
And the daddy of the sinners, owning neither God
nor boss,
Was h grey old drunken Scotchman of the name
of Sandy Ross.
Now Sandy, as a sinful man, was very hard to
beat. '
His oaths were fresh and fierce and strong—-they
scorched you with their heat.
He was drunk at early sunrise, he was drunk at
sunset, too;
And when drunkest told his biggest lie—he sang
‘We are na fou’! ”
He would steal, or beg, or borrow ; he was always
on the cross;
And the parsons—classing sinners—gave the cake
to Sandy Ross.
But the “ Army ” girls got at him, for their hearts
were m their work,
And the Hallelujah ” lasses have been never
known to shirk
A hopeless case, an uphill fight—salute them as
they pass,
worker of the workers is a “Hallelujah
So they tackled Alexander with the Story of the
Cross,
And a change became apparent in the life of
Sandy Ross.
Now, about this time, it happened that a direful
deed was done;
For the parson’s ducks they vanished—yes, they
vanished one by one ;
And the solitary trooper, for the honor of the
force,
Spent watchful days, and sleepless nights, and
sorely tried his horse ;
Till at length a whisper went abroad—a calumny
most gross,
And the finger of suspicion seemed to point to
Sandy Ross.
But the “ Army ” wouldn’t hear it, and they gave
that yarn the lie,
When they entered Sandy boldly for the “ Coming
by-and-bye.”
Then each night upon the platform, in a broken
voice and low,
He informed his fellow sinners he was “ whiter
than the snow.”
And the.parson’s pretty daughter—the enthusiastic
I loss,
Told her friends, in gladsome accents, “ There’s a
change in Mister Ross ! ”
Then the teacher of the State-school, who possessed
a merry eye,
And had doubts of Sandy’s fitness for a mansion
in the sky,
Wagered gloves that, at next meeting, the con-
verted man would scare
And demoralize the godly with a most prodigious
swear.
But the girls they booked the wagers, and en-
thusiastic Floss
Said she felt just like a sister to the convert
Mister Ross.
The night arrived, the hall was full; men spoke,
and by-and-bye
Came announcement from the chairman: “Brother
Ross will testify ; ”
And Sandy rose and told once more how he
excelled the snow
In whiteness—but Co further in his tale could
Sandy go,
For, heard by all, and seemingly proceeding from
the back,
To the horror of the “ Army ” came a duck’s pro-
testing quack.
The speaker paused, and glared around, then had
another try.
I thank the quack!” “I thank ” —“ quack
quack I” “I thank the Lord that I
Am whiter”—“qu-a-a-ck.” “See here, young
chap ” —-then out the torrent burst,
And Sandy ripped, and tore, and swore—’twas
fearsome how he cursed !
He cursed the teacher—cursed the ducks—he cursed
till all was blue ;
The solitary trooper came, he cursed the trooper
too; *
He took his coat and waistcoat off— he would have
taken more,
But the solitary trooper led him cursing to the
door.
Thus, back upon society, came old-time Sandv
Ross, J
Fearing neither man nor devil, owing neither God
nor boss.
W. LONG.
BALLADE OF SHEARING TIME.
The sweating on the board, the smell of sheep ;
The clicking shears, from sunrise unto night,
Fill all the hours that leaden-footed creep—
The slow, dull hours—“O, would that I were
tight!
That you, or he, or any other might
Walk up and down, and round about the floor
And curse and spit, and listen to the skite,
And understand that shearing is a bore.
The drafting at the race where dust lies deep,
And swirls like gum-smoke in the blazing light:
Where endless strings of jumbucks endless keep ’
The straining eyes of swearing drafters bright
With eager searchings after variants slight
Of “squares,”and “notes,” and marks behind,
before.
Ah, would that you could feel the gay tick bite,
And understand that shearing is a bore.
The must’rings on the mountains that are steep,
Where “ double-fleecers ” line on every height,
And all the bloomin’ world seems but a heap
Of ranges piled on ranges out o’ sight.
The dronings down the roads, dry, dusty, white ;
The blasphemy that rings from shore to shore,
At river-crossings. Seek this last delight—
And understand that shearing is a bore.
ENVOY.
Banks, bosses—you who all the profits reap
Of toil, and sweat, and labor long and sore—
Come up yourselves and work your blessed sheep
And understand that shearing is a bore.
N.S.W.
J. H. M. ABBOTT.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 30, 1897.
The Smith that Failed.
Minister Smith, of the New South Wales
Government, said a straight and honest and
democratic thing when he announced that
his department would resist the application
of the Wentworth Gold Mining Co. for a
suspension of the labor conditions during the
existence of the Lucknow strike. And then
he did a very weak and crooked one, inas-
much as his department didn’t resist the
company’s demand for exemption after all.
Nobody doubts that Minister Smith meant
to carry out his announced intentions, but
the vituperation of the capitalistic press and,
presumably, the opposition of Attorney-
General Want, the strong Tory bigot of a
flabby band, were too much for him, and he
foolishly died away. And therein the Smith
that Failed lost the one great opportunity of
his mild career. He didn’t wreck himself
with the democratic party anything like so
badly as Dibbs did over tire Broken Hill
trouble, inasmuch as Smith evidently meant
to do right and failed, whereas Dibbs
avowedly intended to do wrong and fully
succeeded. But it was a pretty bad wreck
all the same.
* * * *
The principal curse levelled at Minister
Smith by the capitalistic press based
on the fact that he interfered in a mat-
ter that was sub judice. The application
of the Wentworth Co. for a suspension
of the labor conditions was about to
tome on before the Warden, and, while
the case was pending, Smith rushed into
print with a minute to the effect that,
in the department’s opinion, the company
was not entitled to exemption, and that the
application would be opposed. This was
denounced as a base and horrible attempt to
interfere with the ends of justice, and to
influence the mind of the Warden, and to
scare that official from the fair and impartial
exercise of his judicial functions. As a
piece of utterly unscrupulous misrepresenta-
tion this argument takes a high place. The
Mines Department, on behalf of the State,
was the defendant in the case, and the
defendant simply gave notice that he intended
contesting the matter. If he had appeared
to contest it without giving notice of his
intention, there might have been cause for
complaint. Labor conditions are the essen-
tial part of the bargain between the State
and the mining lessee. The Wentworth co.
obtained from the State an immensely valu-
able property on condition that it paid an
infinitesimal rent in money, and made up the
balance of the consideration by employing a
certain number of men. The rent paid in
money is so small that the real consideration
is undoubtedly—as Chief Justice Higin-
botham once laid down in a similar case
before the Victorian courts—the company’s
undertaking to find employment for a certain
number of men. Apparently, from state-
ments that have been made in Parliament, it
didn’t pay any very close attention to the
cash part of the bargain, and it desired to be
excused from paying any attention whatever
to the other part..
* * * *
The Wentworth co., in fact, announced its
intention of appealing to the Court for per-
mission to keep the property and yet to stop
paying the largest part—about 99 per cent. —-
of the I’ent or consideration it had bargained
for. Its landlord, the State, through the
mouth of Minister Smith, announced its in-
tention of resisting the application and re-
quiring payment in full. And the Fat Man
press declared unanimously that the landlord
should have let the matter go, and that his
announced intention of objecting to the de-
fault of his tenant was a gross outrage, and
that he was endeavoring to bring undue in-
fluence to bear on the administration of jus-
tice because he stated publicly the reasons
why he didn’t feel inclined to let his tenant
stop payment without putting his side of the
case also before the Court. In fact, the plea
of the Tory papers was that only the com-
pany’s or tenant’s side of the matter should
have been heard, and that it was an outrage
on Property for the landlord’s side of it to
appear at all. Also, tin re was the bitter wail
aforementioned about the landlord appearing
in the case while it was sub jndice —yet every
defendant appears while his case is sub jndice.
If he doesn’t appear then, there is absolutely
no use in appearing at all.
* *' * *
Apart from the fact that the State has just
as much right as any other defendant to op-
pose its defaulting tenant in the law courts,
there were good reasons why it should have
been heard against this application in parti-
cular. The Wentworth co. demanded that
the State should give it special assistance to
starve out its workmen because they had
gone on strike—which was a gross perver-
sion of the truth. It was the Wentworth
co. which went on strike. There are two
sides to every contract ; the employes agreed
to sell a certain amount of labor for so much
coin, and the co. agreed to sell so much coin
for a certain amount of labor. The co. went
on strike by refusing to give so much coin as
heretofore for a day’s labor and by demand-
ing that other new conditions should be im-
ported into the bargain. Hence it was
distinctly the aggressor, and when it de-
manded State assistance on the ground
that its employes were on strike it said
what was utterly untrue — it, was the only
striker in the case. The Wentworth pro-
prietary declared war against its w r orkers,
and demanded that the State should forego
the largest part of the rent of its lease—the
part that was paid in kind, not in cash—to
provide it with the means for carrying on
the campaign ; also, that it should do this
without any proof that the war was justifi-
able. It merely dumped down the fact that
there was a war, and asked for charity on an
enormous scale on the ground that the other
party hadn’t caved in without a struggle.
* * * *
Assuming that the average wages at Luck-
now' were £2 per week this would mean,
when the leases are fully mannid, a wnekly
expenditure of some £4OO. The amount
looks large, but it is only so because the co.
chooses to grab a stupendous area of land
with a full knowledge of the conditions and
consequences. The State has a very large
direct interest in this amount. It gets Cus-
toms and excise duties out of it. It goes to
swell the incomes of storekeepers and others,
who pay their quota in income-tax. It is
passed on by the storekeepers to city mer-
chants, and pays more income-tax. It keeps
Lucknow in existence, and gives the land
thereabouts a value it would not otherwise
possess, and the Government taxes that
value. It creates postal revenue- -in fact the
State levies tribute on it directly and in-
directly in every possible way ; also it in-
directly saves revenue by keeping down the
number of unemployed and the demands
for State assistance. Therefore it is of far
more importance to the State in actual cash
than the paltry sum which the co. is sup-
posed to pay as the nominal rent of the
ground occupied. By demanding exemption
the Wentworth co. demands that the State
shall give up a lot of revenue to enable it to
carry on its private war aga.ast its employes,
and the State has every reason to inquire
into the justice of the cause which sends
round such a stupendous hat and takes up
a collection so aggressively, and to object
to pay this subsidy if it reckons that
the sturdy British beggar is begging
without good reason. And the employes
have a perfect right to demand an equally
large subsidy for themselves. Probably the
labor which the Wentworth co. agreed to
employ in return for a very valuable conces-
sion yields, directly and indirectly, at least
£3O a week out of its wages to the Trea-
sury at the lowest estimate, and these
joint - stock speculators asked that the
Treasury shall forego this sum in their
private interests. If the men had a similar
amount of brassy assurance —which they
haven’t—they would ask, in the name of
impartial justice, an equal weekly donation
in cash to keep up their end of the scrimmage.
And then the shriek of the aggregate Aus-
tralian Fat Man would rend the heavens and
make the stars sit up in affright.
• * * *
About the loudest note of woe in the
press, however, was to the effect that, if the
co. didn’t get exemption, and wasn’t aided
by a large indirect subsidy to cut down
wages, and wasn’t allowed to default on its
side of the bargain with its landlord, the
State, and if the landlord raised any objec-
tion to the default, or even wanted to have
his side of the case heard at all—capital
would “ leave the country.” But very little
mining capital ever came, or seems likely to
come, to this country ; there are plenty of
British mining companies in Australia with
a large paper capital which was mostly
annexed by first-robbers in London, but
the amount which actually reaches Aus-
tralia isn’t worth a moment’s consideration.
What is much more to the point is that
labor may leave the country—the class of
labor which created Bendigo and Ballarat,
Charters Towers, Gympie, Forest Creek,
and every other past or present goldfield of
any serious account which Australia ever
possessed. When one considers the hundreds
of splendid mines which have been found by
the Australian miner, and setsagainstthemthe
fact that British capital has not often really
found anything, the value of the latter is
infinitesimal. In any case, the business of
the law is to serve out impartial justice, even
if all the British capital which ever reached
this country packed up its carpet-bag and
departed never to return. And the pity is
that Minister Smith lacked the courage to
give impartial justice a fair chance. The
case for the plaintiff company was set forth
at length, while that for the defendant—the
State—was not even represented. The Reid
Government has been going to do many
great things in the interest of Labor, but it
always—or nearly always—funks at the last
moment, and these great things are never
done.
A HARD KNOT.
Dear Bulletin, —After much perpension of
this miserable Suffield business, I had arrived
at the conclusion that, in cases in which the
sex-element comes powerfully into action, there
are special reasons why justice should miscarry.
Where it is Man’s alleged offence against Man,
Woman’s against Woman, or either Man’s or
Woman’s against Property, the procedure-
machine works with none too much precision
and uniformity ; but, where male is arraigned
for offence against female, as female, the ricketty
old concern is so apt to get hopelessly out of
gear that some special “ attachment ” is badly
needed to guard against the barbarous bungles
of which Suffield’s case is but an unusually
obvious example.
• • • at
Suffield, in the first place, was unlucky
enough to be tried before a Judge whose
possibly unconscious tendency to favor the
“ spindle side ” of humanity—however natural
to him, as a man—was an atrocious scandal
upon his administration of reputed justice.
And, when to the effect of this bias was
added his pleasing little custom of con-
veying, not very indirectly, to the jury
that, if it wasn’t either a deplorable idiot oV
a contumacious miscreant, its verdict would
have to be his verdict—the result was • a
foregone conclusion. For the average jury is
guided, not by the evidence of the witnesses,
but by the Judge’s evidence about the evidence
of the witnesses—colored always, little or
much, by the Judge’s personal opinion in the
matter. And, even when that opinion, instead
of being thrust arrogantly down the throat of
the jury, is kept conscientiously as far as pos-
sible in the background, the jury itself—being
mostly “right-thinking person,” and pillar of
church, and Goo-fearing father-of-family—may
generally be backed to deny to an alleged
ravisher the benefit of any doubt. If the Judge
is sufficiently arrogant it can be backed to
convict him, as in Suffield’s case, in the
teeth of one of the clearest alibis ever estab-
lished, which brings me to my “Eureka!”—
or what I took to be such, until I turned it
over to look at the other side.
* * * *
A bi-sexual jury, I thought, should go far to-
wards equalising matters. Trust six women
to counterbalance by a perhaps-inevitable sex-
leaning towards one side, the equally natural
inclination of six men towards the other. “It
takes a woman to understand a woman’s possi-
bilities,” and—given jurywomen of the right
stamp —such a personal sex-attitude, for in-
stance, as ex-Judge Windeyer’s, would have
vastly less weight with the petticoated arbiters
of an accused man’s fate than with the
trousered.
* « * »
And this “ right stamp ” we can arrive at
only by eliminating—lst, the woman who
knows nothing of the seamy side of life; 2nd,
the woman who knows too much of it; and,
3rd, the woman whose second-hand and dis-
torted knowledge thereof is more dangerously
misleading than absolute ignorance. The
mechanically virtuous person, whose “ correct”
environment has kept her almost as ignorant of
her own sex as of the other, would be as ill-
fitted for the duty as the notoriously imvirtuous
person who would probably be prepared to
believe any evil of either. And if we further
exclude not only the envenomed virgin who
revenges her unacceptability to the male sex
by ascribing to it, as a whole, the worst
qualities of its worst part, but also
the uneasy old tabby whose delight it
? 8 . to continually sniffing round such
high - flavored morsels as the age-of-con-
sent question—we have left only the kind of
woman to whom marriage and maternity, in
bringing full sex-enlightenment, have brought
also a wholesome, non-hysterical attitude
towards sex-relations.
* * * «
But here it struck me that this is just the
stamp of woman whom the man who can
properly appreciate her would almost as soon
see behind the spikes, herself, as see her com-
pelled to seek for the truth amongst the deplor-
able filth which has so often to be raked over
in sex-cases. If wife is to husband, then, as
impossible in this connection as daughter to
father or sister to brother, it only remains to
be acknowledged that, after all, there is a
point at which woman’s “sphere” brings up
hard against the essential nature of things. A
point which most of her doesn’t approach, and
doesn t want to ; which some of her would like
to pass, if she could ; and which a little of her
actually does pass, at the inevitable expense of
ceasing—as belonging to neither sex—to be of
any interest or consequence to either !— Stkix.
PLAIN ENGLISH,
On Behalf of the Widow.
The N.S.W. Government proposes to increase
the duties on the estate of the late lamented
with a view to bringing them more in line with
those of Queensland, Vic., and S. A. At present,
N.S.W. charges a maximum of 5 per cent, on
the largest estates, and all estates below £2OO
are exempt. In Victoria all estates below
.£lOOO are exempt, but the maximum on the
largest estates is 10 per cent. In Queensland
estates under .£2OO are exempt, and the maxi-
mum is also 10 per cent., but whereas the 10
per cent, is only levied in Victoria on.an estate
worth ,£lOO,OOO or upwards, in Queensland it
is levied on estates worth £20,000 or upwards.
In S.A., if the inheritor is the widower, widow,
or descendant of the deceased, the full 10 per
cent, rate is only charged when the estate is
worth £200,000 or upwards ; any distant rela-
tive or a stranger has to pay 10 per cent, when
the property is worth .£20,000 or upwards.
Therefore when the fat man dies and leaves,
say, ,£30,000 to a complete stranger, the
State takes, in N.S.W., 4 per cent. ; in Vic-
toria 6 per cent. ; Queensland 10 per cent. ;
S.A. 10 per cent. ; while Maoriland comes out
on top with 13 per cent. On the other hand,
if he left £lO,OOO, the inheritor being no rela
tive by blood or marriage, the State would
take, in N.S.W., 2 per cent. ; Victoria 4 per
cent. ; Queensland 8 per cent. ; S.A. 8 per
cent. ; Maoriland 10 per cent. With a much
smaller rate N.S.W. raises nearly as much
revenue as Victoria, the difference being that
N.S.W. raises the money mostly by taxing the
small property which is barely sufficient to keep
the widow out of the Benevolent Asylum, while
Victoria makes its revenue mostly by “ slop-
ping it on ”to the very big estate. Queensland
follows both policies at once. The Bulletin
reckons that there should be heavier taxation
on the large estate, but also that the small one
should receive further exemption; when the
old man pegs out and leaves the old woman
minus her sole source of income, and with only
£2so—in other words, with only a four-roomed
wooden cottage and a little furniture —between
her and starvation, it is a mean, sinful, grovel-
ling, undemocratic business to lay a special tax
on her merely for the loss of her bread-winner.
Even if all estates up to £lOOO went free,
that would only mean that the old woman was
left untaxed the equivalent of a six-roomed
brick cottage and the furniture, and about
enough money to keep her from actual want for
two or perhaps three years, and the widow in
that situation—especially if she is too old to
go out washing—isn’t so bloatedly affluent as
to call for special taxation. In this paper’s
opinion the policy of N.S.W. and Queensland,
by which succession duty is charged to the
widow and the small orphan on everything over
<£2oo, is an outrage on humanity, and even the
exemption limit of .£5OO fixed by S.A. law is
much too narrow. Premier Reid’s proposal to
increase the taxation on large estates is highly
commendable, but if it isn’t accompanied by
liberal concessions in favor of small properties
the Labor-party, as the only reliable democratic
force in the Assembly, should press the matter
for all it is worth. The merciful law of Maori-
land goes even further than that of Victoria,
for it exempts the widow altogether, and there
s a great deal to be said in favor of this idea.
The Galvanising of Gillies.
Dear Bulletin, —The blunder of the Victorian
elections, which was worse than a crime, was
the Age’s persistent attack on Duncan Gillies,
which had the effect of regalvanising that poli-
tical corpse into a new life and sending him
into Parliament the hero of the hour. It is
only a few weeks back since the rosy-cheeked
Duncan returned from England, and com-
menced haunting the Opera House and other
places as he used to do in the days of his very
own boom. At that time not the most Tory of
Conservatives took the little man seriously,
and even the Argus did not think fit to
chronicle his arrival by a five-line par. The
mention of his name was merely the cause for a
laugh, or a pitying word from the more tender-
hearted, and the mere suggestion of his
attempting to re-enter politics would have
been regarded as an extravagant joke. When
he did announce himself for Toorak, and even
addressed a meeting, the blundering Syme,
instead of chronicling the fact that “ a candi-
date named I). Gillijes was given a patient
hearing at Toorak last night,” with a few
mock-kindly words about past services and grey
hairs, burst into an hysterical frenzy, and with
fiery adjectives blazing at each other’s tails
shrieked at the disgraced boom author.
Syme deliberately went and poured boil-
ing water the sleeping dog and
effectually woke him up. The community, see-
ing Gillies attacked, considered that he must be
somebody to be worthy of so much abuse, and
began to take Duncan seriously. Then the
Age, still not satisfied, wired in afresh, and


worked up a heated controversy with the
Argus over the reports of a Gillies meeting,
and consequently the people who had forgotten
Gillies couldn’t help remembering him again,
and the “old war-horse of the party” neces-
sarily emerged from his obscurity. What is the
end ? Heaven only knows, with a dangerous
man like Gillies in politics again. Cute,
clever, courageous and unscrupulous, who can
say how long it is before the Premier of ’BB has
wormed or forced himself into the Turner
Govt., or has ousted Turner from his position?
The return of Duncan Gillies means much
more to Victoria, much more to Australia, than
appears on the Toorak notice-board. It means
that a parasite has fallen upon the saplings of
Democracy just giving promise of becoming
sturdy gums—probably the most vigorous thing
in the way of political parasites to be found in
Australian politics.—X.Z.
The Casual Counsel.
In the S. A. Assembly, after Nixon had been
sentenced to 18 months imprisonment for
stabbing his wife in State Bank Chairman
Addison’s office :
Mr. Poynton : “ Who is defraying the cost of the
Queen’s counsel who appeared for the prosecution in con-
nection with the case?” (Hear, hear.)
Mr. Kingston : “ I don’t know, except that the Govern-
ment are not.” (Cheers.)
Surely a very bad principle, and not worthy of
cheers. Whoever it was that paid him, the
Q.C., at any rate, took particular pains to
whitewash Addison ; and if Addison didn’t
pay him—well, the Q.C. did more than most
lawyers do for clients who fee them hand-
somely. For the papers had not reported his
closing speech ; whereupon he wrote, quoting
what he had said, and it was all a defence of
Addison from the attack of Nixon’s counsel.
Also, nobody but Addison was likely to fee
him. Now, Addison wanted to get Nixon out
of the road, and offered him money to go —
he admits that. Further, he admits that
he neither hates nor has a contempt for Nixon,
because his feelings are more intense than those
weak words imply. The public will possibly
conclude that it was this man with such amiable
intentions who paid the Q.C., or in whose in-
terests the Q.G. was paid ; and appearances are
in favor of such a view. But the Criminal
Courts are surely not constituted either to
gratify feelings of this sort or to allow them to
be gratified. The Crown takes up a prosecu-
tion, not as a matter between the stabber and
the stabbed, the robber and the robbed, but as
one between the people and the accused. The
process is not one which allows any place for
personal malice; if personal malice exists
the proceeding becomes naturally a persecution,
and is not a prosecution at all. The poor man
who gets into the prisoner’s dock (an unfair and
odious institution in itself) is handicapped
enough by the Crown fee-ing only an ad-
vocate to prosecute, leaving him to shift
as to his defence as best he may; but
if on top of this the Crown accepts
without question the services of other advo-
cates paid by private individuals who, for some
reason or other, may be anxious to get the
accused out of the way, or to pay off an old
score against him, then the handicap on the
poor man becomes pretty well intolerable. The
Adelaide ease does not seem singular, either. A
similar thing has been done in N. S. W., especially
during labor troubles, when, the employers
being eager to “rub it in” to “ agitators,”
have paid higher-priced and more influential
and zealous advocates than the Grown has
retained.
♦ :
Sydney Evening News, in its leading a±cicle
of last Friday, brushed away all Lyne’s un-
pleasant criticisms of Reid’s budget like a fly
off a bald head :
How far Mr. Lyne’s criticism of abstruse points of
government-account keeping is likely to be fair may be
judged by the extraordinary things he endeavored to make
figures prove in connection with matters more within
ordinary knowledge. He tried to make out that Victoria
was, in proportion, much more prosperous than New
South Wales, entirely ignoring the awkward fact that
people are leaving Victoria for N.S. Wales, and that the
decline in the population of Melbourne has been, within
the last few years, most marked, whereas the population
of Sydney has increased.
That settles it, of course. Everybody knows,
through much reiteration in the shoddy press,
that the people from all the adjacent provinces
keep pouring into N.S.W. to enjoy the bless-
ings of Free-raid, and that Sydney is constantly
expanding. Yet how they do know it has
never been explained. The N.S.W.. official
Government publication lumps together the
arrivals from Queensland, S.A., and Victoria
in one item, and does the same with departures
to those three provinces. For the last six years
(1891-96) recorded departures from N.S.W. for
the three adjacent Protectionist provinces were
321,883, and arrivals in N.S.W. from the three
adjacent provinces were 312,464. This is over-
land-traffic only, the sea-traffic returns giving
no particulars as to countries of origin or desti-
nation, but, presumably, it is a fair guide, and
it shows N.S.W. has lost nearly 9000 people
to the adjacent provinces in six years. Two of
them, Vic. and S.A., have also lost population,
but they lost it to Westralia, not to N.S.W.
And, as regards the increase of Sydney, even
that has exceptions, as witness the Govt. returns:
1894, population 423,600; 1896, population
410,000; decrease in two years, 13,600. Yet
the constant growth and prosperity of Sydney,
and the great influx of population from the
adjacent provinces, are two articles of faith
with the average cornstalk person, and paper,
and politician. They are so self-evident that
he never dreams of asking anybody to prove
them.
Premier Seddon’s announcement that on the
Royal Commission to inquire into theMaoriland
Police Administration there shall be no one
connected with Prohibition, the liquor-traffic
or politics, makes a new, bold, good departure.
Too long it has been the custom of Governments,
in casting around for members of a commission,
to see only political, social, and clique circles.
The daily bores who fill the papers with cronk
crime-statistics, the ring-bosses who smell of
the evils the inquiry is concerned about,
and frayed and other politicians whose judg-
ments at the tail of the investigation can be
foretold before the inquiry opens, constitute
the general field out of which Australian Go-
vernments are accustomed to pick the favorites.
Commissions so appointed are worse than
worthless. Men nominated to a commission
should be as free from taint of bias or pre-
possession as the juror empanelled to try a
criminal or civil action. And men thus free
are available in all communities if Governments
would but look beyond the field where the axe-
grinders are at work.
In Melbourne Evening Herald, Alexander
Sutherland is writing some papers under the
heading, “Men who have made Australia.”
This is one of the trails left by the Jubilee
drunk. Sutherland poses Phillip as a
humane and just and benevolent deity,
to whom we must accord all praise and
honor as the founder of Australian coloni-
sation. But the mists of tradition have not
yet closed over the acts of our first Governors.
There are such things as records. Nay, there
are those living who have spoken with contem-
poraries of Phillip. And it is known, thereby,
that during that humane Governor’s control of
the first settlement women, stripped to the
waist, were harnessed to the carts that carried
cobblestones and bricks and timber for the
buildings of voung Sydney and Parramatta.
The dull-eyed, diseased hags from seaport
stews of England, and the younger and more
venial sinners (they were all thrown into the
cauldron alike in those days) toiled in this
shameful, shameless fashion under the lash of
an overseer and the orders and approval of
Governor Phillip. But what then ? It is the
fashion of historians to cover up the dungheaps,
just as Jack Robertson, yielding to the pres-
sure of colonial sassiety, destroyed its family
tree when he burned the passenger-list of the
First Fleet.
From July to September, of this year, the
number of miners in Tasmania was : Whites,
3839; Chinese, 285 ; a total decrease on the
figures for July-September, 1896, of 350 —230
whites and 120 Chinese. Since leaving the tin-
fields, whither have those 120 Chows wandered ?
They are certainly not growing cabbages in
Tasmania, so the chances are all in favor of
them having drifted across Bass’ Straits. In
addition to these 120 Mongolians displaced in
the tin-mines, another hundred or two reached
Tasmania during the year—ostensibly to work
at the aforesaid tin-mines. Where are they
now ? Tasmania is as much the dirty back-door
for the admission of undesirables to the main-
land as ever it was, and well the boss Chinamen
know how to take advantage of the pliability of
Fysh.
“ Public School Teacher” to The Bulletin :
The Irish National Scripture Lesson books (unexpurgat-d)
are used in the N.S.W. public schools, and it was usual,
until lately, for the scholars to read the books, with the
result that the teacher would generally contrive to be
busy at his desk or with another class while big girls
(often young women) read full-flavored passages aloud.
In one case, a nine-year-old girl, having enquired what
was meant by “ Abraham circumcised his son Isaac,”
wasn’t satisfied with the information that circumcis’on
was a Jewish rite, and so confused the teacher that he
had to beat a retreat. “ I don’t believe he knows,”
said the girl ; “ I’ll find out for myself.” And
doubtless she searched the Scriptures, and found
many things that she would understand later in life.
Many teacher's on this account avoid administration of the
“ pure ” Word by giving the Scripture lessons orally, and
garbling them into comparative decency. Public schools
have occasionally been styled “hotbeds of immorality”
because religion was not given a prominent place, but the
truth is that a great deal of the immoral knowledge
acquired at school is from the Scripture lessons. Even
from the ultra-religious point of view, in short, Scripture
lessons are a dead failure. Also, since truthfulness,
honesty, industry, obedience, and so on are inculcated
in the general routine of every well-managed school, what
is the use of warning children against sins of which they
have no knowledge, or worrying them about creeds and
other things, of which their elders don’t really know any
more than they do ? If Scripture must be taught, let
properly qualified theologians undertake the cure of their
own particular lambs. The difficulty, though, would be
to sort ’em out, for it is amusing to observe how the
children change their religion according to the parson in
attendance.
“ Homestead Lessee,” to The Bulletin :
In your issue of 28th Aug. there is an article on home-
stead leases and resumed areas, in which you seem to favor
the attachment of said areas to leaseholds. As a home-
stead lessee, I, in common with my class, don’t agree, for
this reason : If attaching the resumed areas to the lease-
hold gives the squatter a more secure tenure, there is no
need to get the areas attached. The argument that
resuming half of the large pastoral holdings stopped im-
provement will only hold water with those who do not
know. Out back here there are miles of country which
are not on the resumed areas, and which are not improved,
or anything like it; still the lessees of these lands will say
that they can’t improve them, as their tenure is not
secure. About 60,000 acres would be an average sheep-
paddock out in the Western district. If cut up into four
or five paddocks, it would carry about 6000 sheep con-
tinuously ; but in some paddocks the body of sheep soon
tread the feed down ; in its present unimproved condi-
tion, with an average of one watering place to each pad-
dock, about 4000 sheep would be heavy stocking. There
is no guarantee that more improvements will he gone on
with when the areas are attached, nor that the attached
areas will yield a better rental—as the lessee has the
right of appeal against the rent if he thinks it is too
much—and he always thinks it is too much. The beastly
Cocky protests for all he is worth against attachment, as
small pastoral holdings give working men and others with
small capital their only chance to start in life out here,
for the rainfall is too uncertain for farming, and irrigation
on a large scale will only come with population ; so by all
means keen the land open for homestead leases until the
land needs to be re-divided. Station-holders will not kill
more vermin with attached areas, or they would be more
industrious on the leaseholds. The present Ministry
seem to favor farms or large holdings. Well, farms will
not do out here, until the rainfall improves. But, as a
living can be made off 10,240 acres of the best class of
western land—and that is about the least one can raise a
family on—let the land stay as it is.
[What The Bulletin said was this : that in
the interests of the legitimate selector the
prowler who drops upon a squatter’s tank and
holds it until a pinch is over, but without in-
tention of selecting it, ought to have his game
spoiled ; and, therefore, that, where land is not
likely to be at once needed for settlement, it is
in the general interest that such land should
be thrown into leasehold, with a provision for
its resumption six months after any application
is made for it, and without reference then to
the Minister. This last is the all-important
safeguard of the bona-fide selector. — Ed. B.]
Queensland Land Bill makes 18 the minimum
age for selectors ; Groom and others fight for
16, and rely on N.S.W. experience. The
N.S.W. experience is that the selector under
18 or 19 is inostlj r more or less of a dummy ;
but as he is generally more or less the dummy
of his father, the evil is comparatively small,
especially as, in most cases, the son, if he
proves to be any good for the work at all
presently goes into bona fide possession “ on
his own.” The N.S.W. law doesn’t prohibit a
father giving his son money wherewith to take
up land and make a start in life, while hitherto
Queensland has not permitted this to be done.
Certainly the permission does open a door to
fraud ; but, providing the door is watched,
not enough harm is done to set off the good.
In this respect, as in most others in con-
nection with the land law, administration
counts for almost more than the law itself.
What the N.S.W. law does not allow is that
the son, having got the money from the father,
shall give a mortgage or otherwise secure the
land to the “old man ”; it is on this rock that
more than one over-cautious selector, blessed
with a houseful of boys, has gone down. And
it is through ignorance of the other provision
that many another selector has struggled to
find employment for his family on the limited
home selection, though it would have been far
better for them all if he had set up the lads on
neighboring country, where he could still have
kept his eye on them and given them his advice,
and at the start an occasional job.

Writes “ Spartan ” :
Dear Bulletin, —The “ yellow agony ” is severely felt
at Texas, on the Dumaresq, the finest tobacco-growing
district in Leperland. Some IS years ago the settlers on
this river found that they could grow very high-class
tobacco-leaf on its adjacent alluvial flats ; but, not satisfied
with a fair thing, they leased their land to the Chinamen
on the share system, the land-owner getting a quarter of
crop as rent. Result was that the Chows swamped the
district, and in a couple of years grew so much tobacco
that supply exceeded demand, and prices, of course, fell.
Meanwhile, many people, attracted by the success of the
industry, settled in Texas district mostly without
means, but hoping to get a start with the first crop,
and the state of affairs there at present is conse-
quently deplorable. Last year’s tobacco crop was
three times what was required by the manufacturers,
who are, of course, therefore fixing their own price. Many
selectors with large, unsaleable crops of leaf on hand are
on the verge of starvation, and scores of families in the
district do not know where next week’s rations are com-
ing from. Government will do nothing, and I should not
be surprised to see the men of the Texas district make an
anti-Chinese law of their own. “God helps those who
help themselves.”
Nelson (Queensland’s Hugh, not Emma’s
Horatio) must have read his constitutional
history through cronk eyeglasses. He says :
The British Constitution is not founded on abstract
rights, but is based on the principle that each branch of
the Legislature shall have the power of preventing- any
particular class from obtaining an undue preponderance
of influence and power, so that the interests of all classes
alike will have an opportunity of being protected, con-
served, and developed.
Take the last clause first—about all British
classes alike having opportunity of being cod-
dled—and conpare it with the fact that none of
the lower classes possess any share in the
franchise. Then test the value of the “ prin-
ciple that each branch of the Legislature shall
have the power of preventing any particular
class from obtaining an undue preponderance ”
by examining, in the light of history, that
alleged principle’s workings. For centuries
until within the memory of persons still living
a “particular class” a very particular
class —the so-called nobles, wielded an immis-
takeable predominance. Anyhow, they mono-
polised every office of honor and emolument in
Church, State, Army and Navy. They laid a
tax on the industry of the people, levied it for
their own uses and called it rent. Besides
plundering the people, they purloined much
church property and pilfered many a perk of
the Crown. Nothing came amiss to them. And
they did their villainy in the name of Statute
Law. To-day another “ particular class,”
the Plutocrats, exercise the predominance by
means of the other branch of the British Legis-
lature. Under their rule, to the Plutocrat
goes the profit, the interest, the rent —the
whole swag. To the Plutocrat the inventor is
bondsman—the producer drudge. Below the
drudges are the statutory paupers and the
Submerged Tenth. All these, the exploited
producers and artisans, the drudges, the
paupers, the submerged—many millions of
them—daily curse the “principle” by the
operation whereof their interests are hugh-
nelsonically “ protected, conserved, and de-
veloped.”

Premier Nelson (Q.): “There is little use
creating a Federal Senate and then frittering
away its power by providing for deadlocks and
things of that kind.” Nobody could give the
case away much more wooden-headedly than
that. All the ideas for settling deadlocks con-
sist in referring the matter, in some form or
other, to the popular will. The people may
decide in favor of either House, therefore the
provision for deadlocks “fritters away” the
power of cne House just as much or as little as
the other ; or, in other words, it strengthens
each House against the other and doesn’t
“ fritter ” at all. Nelson assumes that the
Senate will naturally be an ox-headed institu-
tion which the people will, as a matter of
course, decide against every time, which is not
at all what is intended. Further, he assumes
that if the Senate hasn’t power to create dead-
locks it will be of very little use—which is also
very rough. He doesn’t contemplate a Senate
which will be a valuable constructive body, and
possess the confidence of the people, and lead
public opinion ; he only looks forward to a
large, square dab of mullock with a mission to
block things, and if it isn’t allowed to block
things then what use will it be ?

The other day, after a Lucknow man had been
found guilty of receiving stolen gold, a large
policeman was allowed to lumber into the
witness-box and allege, apparently as an aggra-
vating circumstance, that gold-stealing had
been going on at the Lucknow mines for five
years. The law was badly shocked to hear
that the evil was so great and so long-estab-
lished, and it expressed its opinion of the
enormity of the case by a sentence of three
years. It didn’t even ask the policeman how
he knew his alleged knowledge. Nobody, it
would appear, was ever convicted of stealing
ore from Lucknow before. Nobody was ever
found with any ore, which was proved to be
stolen, in his possession. The management
simply says that gold has been habitually
stolen, apparently on no real legal evi-
dence apart from the fact that it thinks
the returns should Jie larger than they are,
and assumes that the difference between the
gold which iB there and the gold which
it thinks should be there has been stolen.
And the police, apparently, know just what
the management tells them it thinks it knows ;
and Chief Justice Darley knows what the
policeman tells him on the evidence of what the
mine management fancies it is aware of, and
comes down like a ton of bricks on his prisoner
in consequence. The policeman didn’t pretend
to assert that he knew the prisoner to have been
concerned in these alleged robberies, which
were alleged to have gone on for five years, so
it is difficult to see why he was allowed to drag
them in at all. And The Bulletin badly wants
to know what kind of a police force it is that is
fully aware of a crime going on upon a large
scale right under its nose, and yet takes five
years to secure a conviction ?
O’Sullivan, M.L.A., and others contend that
the election of the Federal Senate by provinces
entirely as one electorate means necessarily the
election of rich or greatly-boomed men, “ who
will be strongly conservative.” Australia
affords but one example of such voting, and
rich men did not then figure strongly in the
returns. Politicians in the front rank, or, from
the O’Sullivan standpoint, “greatly-boomed
men,” possess natural advantages and deserve to
possess them, as against the sleepless activity
of the local town-pump member. O’Sullivan
himself used to boast that Gladstone couldn’t
beat him in Queanbeyan. That’s just what
United Australia should endeavor to make
possible.
“ Mars ” to The Bulletin :
Failing the polite attentions of the Civil Service Board,
N.S.W. M’s.L.A., with a desire for economy, might keep
an eye on the military estimates when they appear. The
Assembly, when it insists upon a reduction in the “ war”
vote, generally takes the advice offered to make the reduc-
tion in a lump sum, the items being apportioned by the
authorities. It is significant that the rank-and-file stand
all the retrenchment, which rarely touches the gilded
warriors at Vic. Barracks. A visitor at this establish-
ment, or a reader of the army-list, is paralysed at; the
number of majors, colonels, &c., there is to such a small
force. In view of the fact that a lot of these highly-paid
officers seem to have nothing to do except practice unjust
economy at the expense of the partially-paid non-coms, and
privates, some enquiry ought to be made as to their use-
fulness. As redundant colonels I might mention the
A.A.G. and the A.Q.M.G., whose duties in each case could
be performed by a warrant-officer —except that portion
which consists in attending social “ functions.” Another
colonel is boss of the riding-school; his duties could
surely also be performed by a smart non.-com.— only the
latter might not be such a distinguished ornament at the
Hunt Club meets. There are 22 officers connected with
the Permanent Artillery—about 500 men ; 14 could not
only do the work necessary, but they could still have time
for polo, &c. A lot of the gentlemen on the “ staff ” get
allowances for horses—used on an average two hours per
annum for military purposes. It’s a scandal that so much
money is spent at the head of the military department,
while the main body of the force is being disorganised by
cheeseparing retrenchment.
There is another side to every question, how-
ever. In this case it is as follows : When war
comes this way, as it must some day or other,
it may be necessary to rush 20,000 or 50,000
raw recruits into the ranks of our lit Lie army
if there is to be anything like a decent defence.
And if N.S.W. hasn’t good officers to lick them
into shape there will be trouble. If the pre-
sent lot are really good officers, then it is w ell
worth paying their salaries, so that they will
be on hand when the emergency comes. The
only question is—are they ?
Postage on N.S.W. newspapers is the direct
reply to the Government statement that the
province can’t afford to surrender State school
children’s weekly threepennies. But even
though this measure of educational justice
were secured, as it now seems likely tq be,
without any conditions, still postage on
New South Wales newspapers should be in-
sisted on. Freetrade should demand it, as the
tax is the price of services rendered by the
State to a particular class of traders, and Pro-
tectionists should demand it for that reason
and for the additional reason that it is a plank
in all existing Protectionist systems. The little
start which the late Chief-Justice Martin,
when a politician, gave to Protection, set out
postage on newspapers as one of his first prin-
ciples. And he carried it, too, as part of his
Protectionist scheme in 1864. The country
then was not ready for Protection as a policy,
but even on the rejection of the other parts of
the scheme postage on newspapers was re-
tained. Lang, urged on by the Freetrade press
•—then, as now, not genuine, but self-seeking
in this department of Freetrade ethics—moved
to abolish the postage, but failed. In 1869 J.
F. Burns carried a motion directing the Go-
vernment to bring in a bill for the abolition of
the impost, and the Parkes Government of
1873 took the postage off. It is well-known
that, with the exception of Dr. Lang, the prime
movers in freeing newspapers from postage
lived on the crumbs of notoriety and
fame which the powerful and widely- circulating
Freetrade press of Sydney city dropped
day by day in their path. Hence the Freetrade
press was absolute master of the situation, and
not only itself indulged the instinct of self-
seeking advantage, but imposed the preserva-
tion of that advantage on its political tools.
Freetrade politicians subsidised out of the public
coffers a Freetrade press for the journalistic
service that press, widely disseminated, was
able to yield in stemming the tide of the op-
posing political system. N.S.W. Leg. Assem-
bly is now in a better position to treat this
question independently than any of the Parlia-
ments that have preceded it. A call for a
straight - out vote on postage or no post-
age would have in its favor, if prin-
ciples be followed, a solid Protectionist
vote, a solid Labor vote, and, at least, two-
thirds of the Freetrade vote. For the reasons
given all these votes should go to the right.
This is the time for a combined demand to be
made, and a list of voters for and against placed
before the country.
The other week, Bananaland Parliament
made a bigger ass of itself than usual. A bill was
introduced giving the Minister for Education
power to omit certain subjects from the school
curriculum, and introduce others (unspecified) at
pleasure. It will scarcely be believed that the
bill was passed with very little difficulty. The
ostensible object was to allow the Education Act
to be amended in the direction of introducing
superior education into the State -schools, but it
leaves the Act open to be tampered with * y every
fad-ridden Minister m turn. And they've all had
fads so far.


Thomas A. Edison, the great inventor, has
just produced the “ HOME ” phonograph, which,
as the name implies, is intended to supply the de-
mand for an instrument for private use having all
the vital points of the expensive exhibition phono-
graph combined with a low price. This new
“ talking machine,” costing only £l2, will soon be
found in every house in the country. The manager
of the Edison Electric Parlor (162 Pitt-st., Sydney)
reports a strong demand.*
