Opera House Rickards’ Variety Co.
Alexandra . “ Cinders.”
A strong company and good management
have invested the “ Two Little Vagabonds ” with
a glitter that isn’t quite all their own. There
are traces of “East Lynne” in this drama, and
faint memories of Little Eva, and an almost im-
perceptible flavor of “Jo ” —who likewise had a
dreadful korf. The story begins with a wife and
a loving but jealous husband, who is suddenly
convinced on the worst possible evidence that his
child is the unlawful offspring of the other fellow.
A Bill Sikes person burgles the house just then,
and the father hands him the infant with strict
instructions to drag it up as a nameless criminal
of the deepest dye. The son does more credit to
his dragging-up than might be expected. He
conceals his identity by mixing himself up with
Sikes’s other boy—a waif with only one lung, and
a cockney accent, and a fearful bark. This other
waif isn’t explained or accounted for, but there
are vague hints that the sister of the injured wife
really did go wrong with the man that the wife
was supposed to have gone wrong with, and that
they had a child, and as this child is never ex-
plained he may or may not be the Jo with the
one lung. The writer’s impression is that he
isn’t.
* * * *
Thereafter the mother and father part,
and wander round crying separately for their lost
offspring. And when they find B. Sikes, and he
hasn’t the real boy at hand, he naturally sells them
the scrap with the lingering lung, and this worn
fragment is taken to a mother’s bosom, and goes
to bark out his last days in luxury. But circum-
stances reveal the real boy ju.st when the bogus
one is coughing more dreadfully than usual, and
about this time the Bill Sikes person and his con-
federates grow desperate and break out into melo-
drama of a furious description. They kidnap the
father, who has called to complain of having a
sham consumptive sold him for <£3oo, and, when
the real son rescues him, there is an exciting
escape over the adjacent roofs. Then Sikes and
co. burgle the house with a view to re-capturing
the son, and they murder the consumptive boy to
make him keep quiet. And there is a spectacular
finish while the re-united family weeps around
the bogus son who had enjoyed his cough on
their premises for one short week, and
the consumptive dies of a complication of
troubles, including a blood-stain on his shirt, say-
ing beautiful things in his last moments. The
drama doesn’t give way to uncalled-for cheerful-
ness even once in its five acts. It starts with the
horror of Titheradge giving away his baby son to
B. Sikes. It wades through tears and woe as the
pale mother wanders round for seven years crying
for her child, and the pallid father does the lost
soul business up and down the earth, and the two
waifs are horsewhipped by Sikes. And it ends
with chastened woe as the waif with one lung,
and that a very bad one, remarks on his death-
bed that he never knowed any real mother worth
speaking of. The grief of the audience goes up all
the time in a thick steamlike washing day, and
when the hired opera-glasses are handed back it is
necessary to drain the tears out of them into a
bucket.
* * * *
With a bad co. the “Two Little Vaga-
bonds ” wouldn’t be worth much ; with the strong
combination at Her Majesty’s it is a thing with
good points about it. Titheradge’s fine acting
rescues the horrid father from much of the
obloquy that would otherwise fall on him. Miss
Emilie Hughes is very real and very clever as
the wronged mother, and Miss Lily Titheradge,
as the larger vagabond of the pair, is a
very big success. The Bulletin doesn’t know
anybody who could be a more satisfactory
vagabond than Miss Lily, whose performance
is good all through. The other vagabond—the
one whose lung has been lost in the dragging-up
process—-is played by Miss Florence Terriss, but,
though she does that tired little philosopher very
well, she hardly takes the audience by storm, as
Miss Lily does. Circumstances have cast Howard
Vernon in a dull brown character as a lame
ruffian of no special account; and Sweeney,
Patten, Percy Clifton, Miss Julia Merton and
Mrs. Maesmore Morris do the rpst of the work
respeotably. Mr. Patten is mainly a beautiful,
ordinary captain, in brown boots ; and Mrs. Morris
is a vision of loveliness, who doesn’t act so badly
as The Bulletin expected. How badly this paper
expected her to act is a secret which has been
enclosed in a sealed packet, to be burnt by
the writer’s executors in the presence of three
reliable witnesses.
** # *
Frank Harvey’s “Fallen Among Thieves,”
which unfolds its moral tale this week at Sydney
Lyceum, is a new drama of that old school which
has been a good deal superseded of late years by
the blood and crash military spectacle. It begins
on an English farm at Xmas with holly about—
the farm where the daughter of the old solid
British turnip-raiser lives in innocence, and
watches the brown cow ambling across the
meadow. Then the usual serpent arrives from
London in a top hat, and persuades her to fly with
him. The glossy, top-hatted serpent of the old
style always comes from London, and it is an
understood thing that the person from that city is
invariably filled with the worst intentions. As a
natural consequence the girl “falls ” with the old
moral thud, and the crash of a broken command-
ment is heard all over the house. She is taken to
the gilded halls of vice, and the lady covered with
jewels who runs the establishment proves to be
her long-lost mother. And after that they
repent together, and lead a precarious exist-
ence in barns and gaols and attics till the
old agriculturist takes his daughter back, and she
is happy again in her old home. The brown cow
ambles across the paddock just as it used to do,
and the returned prodigal wakes in the morning
in her little white bed in her old room with the
china Agger on the mantelpiece, and peace descends
with a wallop on the premises.
* » # *
These are the main features of the drama,
but sundry casual thrills are thrown in. The
mother dies forgiven, and her grave makes a green
hump in the little churchyard. The glossy be-
trayer is shot by his fellow miscreant in the
exactly similar top-hat, and apparently nobody is
scragged for the crime. A comedy sailor, who
becomes engaged to the pretty servant, is chiefly
remarkable for being called Jack, whereas he
might have been Algernon Howard Cbol-
mondeley. On the whole, this paper likes the
new drama better than it expected, mainly because
it is so calm and restful, and the good people are
so good, and the bad people are so bad, and every-
thing is so orthodox all through. King Hedley,
Boothman and Harry Shine carry the piece along
manfully; Miss Maud Williamson does the
prodigal mother remarkably well, and Minnie
Brandon does the prodigal daughter moderately,
and pretty Edith Barrow makes the servant-girl
an oasis in the great plain of moral orthodoxy.
The einematographe, which prefaces the show
proper, is itself good value for the money, and the
management announces that the “ Prairie King ”
—that big, dazzling horse-sensation, with the
broken bridge in it —will follow.
* * * *
The variety show with which Rickards
recently opened the Palace has resumed business
this week at Sydney Criterion. The Bulletin
sat in judgment on it some weeks ago, and found
a verdict to the effect that it was a decent
variety show, and that verdict still stands. The
Biograph comes out with a new series of pictures,
most of them novel and some of them interesting.
Phil Newbury is a powerful addition to the
musical part of the programme —in fact, Newbury
is good enough to be a whole musical programme
by himself ; and Miss Fanny Wentworth is still,
in this paper’s opinion, the only amusing lady
monologue entertainer of its acquaintance.
* # # *
Sydney Tivoli is still alive and prospering,
but the Palace has suddenly shut its gates of
gilded splendor with a loud clang, and the present
writer, who is beastly tired anyhow, doesn’t know
for certain when that fretted portal will re-open.
The Royal also closed its older and much plainer
door decisively at short notice, and at time of
writing Geo. Rignold was doing a season at New-
castle, with vastly better success than he met with
in Sydney. After that, he goes North. The
Opera House has gone out of business in an unob-
trusive fashion, and horse-tamer Smith has de-
parted, and the recent Sydney show-boom is evi-
dently over.
* * * *
■Florence Schmidt, who has made up her
mind to flit to Paris early next year, will begin
her farewell next Saturday in the Town Hall.
For this concert she is announced to sing some of
the best-known soprano arias from famous operas
and the programme is strengthened by several
good names.
* * * *
Professor Bristol’s company of Educated
Horses took the sanded stage at Melbourne Bijou
on Saturday night, and will perform nine times a
week until further notice. It is an excellent per-
formance of its kind. The animals, as animals,
are polished ar.tists. In their own horsefleshly
sphere, they achieve a Brough and Boucieaultian
success, and the Professor, who has educated them
all--from the low comedian mules, the flying pony
Jordan, and the lightning-calculator horse, down
to the docile quadrupeds that walk through minor
parts—is evidently a man to be respected. The
show seems to cry aloud for a strong, distinctive
title, but under any name it would smell of stable,
and impress the truly horsey soul. Whether the
novelty will catch on at the Bijou is another
question. The present pitch seems quite lacking
in hippodrome sentiment, also the fact that the
public confuses this horse show with an unsimilar
show run by a different class of professor only a
few weeks back is an unlucky circumstance for
the management. Kennedy, who used to be a
mesmerist, is controlling the business. The comio
mule was, perhaps, one of Kennedy’s subjects in
its previous existence.
#* * *
“ A Royal Divorce ” retires from Melb.
Princess’s on Friday, after four weeks of pros-
perity, and “ The Gay Parisienne ” will gambol
on the site of Napoieon’s vanished greatness.
Miss Ada Reeve, who played Julie Bon-Bott
throughout the London season of “The Gay
Parisienne,” has been specially engaged to do it
again, and Mr. Bert. Gilbert will join her in a
first Australian appearance. The company other-
wise is about the same as that which set the musi-
cal comedy spinning in Sydney not long ago.
** * *
The Alexandra’s latest weekly issue is “ A
Fatal Tune,” and Augustus Glover plays the
name-part. For reasons too numerous to mention
Glover disguises himself as a crushed tragedian,
and runs the real murderer to earth with the dole-
ful strains of “Auld Robin Gray,” which go
straight to the guilty person’s conscience, judging
from his expression of pain inside. In the last
act Glover administers such a severe dose of
“Auld Robin” that the other fellow dies, un-
happy in the knowledge that he slew the wrong
seducer, and made no difference to the woman he
was jealous of. Miss Rosa Conroy, Frank Nor-
ton, Miss Ada Lawrence, Edwin Lester, and a
well-behaved company extract considerable excite-
ment for the masses from “A Fatal Tune.” A
grand production of “ Cinders ” is due on Derby
night.
** * *
“ Johanna,” which has supplanted its
twin-sister “ Jaqueline,” at Melb. Royal, seems
more or less a change for the better. The heroine’s
long-lost parent is, in this case, a torn and tattered
mother with an utterly abandoned hea'd of hair,
whereas the stray relative in the previous tragedy
was a mere Italian nobleman whose appearance'
shocked no one. Johanna’s mother can claim to
be the most unaccountable and entertaining
dramatic incident on record. She obtrudes her-
self upon the privacy of a pawnshop at the
moment when her artless child is about to
marry the proprietor and start housekeeping
as Mrs. Harris. Mother produces the pawn-
ticket issued by Harris, long ago, in acknow-
ledgment of Johanna having been put up
the spout, and when the number of the ticket
is announced, as though it had drawn a first
prize in Tatt’s sweep, the discomfited Harris puts
his face against the back of the counter and groans
inwardly. Johanna, meanwhile, hugs the frowsy
apparition to her heart, saying, “ Mother, take me
away, do take me away ” ; and, in the absence of
any sort of opposition from Harris, the two
females leave that unprecedented pawnbroker to
his bitter reflections.
• * * •
Crushed by the blow, he doesn’t even rouse
himself to prevent Harry Roberts from “ shak-
ing ” a frock-coat an 1 bell-topper out of stock.
However, after a further period of despair, Harris
proceeds to burn Johanna’s marriage-settlement,
and is on the point of burning his will (by which
.£50,000 is bequeathed to Johanna aforesaid)
when an absurd villain, who has - somehow
become entangled in the mystery of Harris,
fires a fatal shot through the window.
Harris was not a particularly uncivil person, as
pawnbrokers go, but his decease gives pleasure to
all. It is, in fact, such a fortunate occurrence
that Nemesis refrains from waiting on the crime,
and the culprit, instead of being hanged, is peace-
ably ejected from somebody’s drawing-room by
Roberts. “Johanna” is a cheerful drama, quite
apart from its pawnbroking phases.
* * * *
Maggie Moore and Harry Roberts —he will
soon have acquired a lighter touch than any come-
dian in Australia, native or foreign—play sundry
scenes in a breezy, picknickian spirit. Also Miss
Laura Roberts looks nice, as usual, and several
other people succeed in taking themselves seri-
ously, which is a creditable feat, anyhow. J. P.
West, as Harris, the Monte de Piete man, deserves
special credit for his efforts in this direction.
“ Mrs. Quinn’s Twins ” is the next piece on the list.

** * *
The Axemen’s Contest is about to rage in
Melb. Exhibition grounds for several days and
nights. Local inhabitants who, as a rule, know
very little about this wholesome form of enter-
tainment are herewith enjoined to roll up and
mark the difference between domestic wood-
chopping in the back-yard and professional log-
smiting for cash prizes. An ordinary axeman can
never imagine the utter foolishness of himself until
he witnesses a Tasmanian champion on the job.
The Lynch Family Bellringers, with a
little variety show attached, will return to Melb.
Athenaeum Hall on Derby night, and make musib
at that respectable establishment until Nov. 9.

COUNT TOLSTOI SERIOUSLY ILL.
Count Leo Tolstoi, the eminent Russian novelist and
social reformer, is seriously ill. He was born in 1828, and
was a friend and comrade of Peter the Great.
. N.S.W. Friendly Societies’ recent Hos-
pital-Sunday demonstration made as goodly a
show as usual, but there was more cry than wool
—total about £125 ! In fact, there are general
signs of the slump that inevitably comes sooner or
later to everything that is as much overdone as
has been the Hospital-boom in N.S.W.
A second edition of “The Law of the
Press,” a publication issued tinder the auspices of
the English Institute of Journalists, is in prepara-
tion ; and Mr. Frank Williams, of Wagga
(N.S.W.) Advertiser, will be glad to receive on
behalf of the authors any information relating to
the laws and practice of Australian journalism
which local pressmen may be able to supply.
A pension or lump sum for Graham Berry
is a certainty. Nobody, The Bulletin hopes,
will oppose it, not even those who are disgusted
with him for his lapse into a shabby mock aristo-
cracy, and refuse to recognise in the ennobbled
Imperialist the Democrat who once so manfully
fought scented Privilege and embastioned Mono-
poly, He went beyond the Victorian people
when he took the reward of political flunkeydom
from London. But Berry will be rewarded for
what he was, and forgiven for what he is.
Yic. : “ Graham Berry’s knighthood was
just the one thing that put him out in this general
election. The tar-brush. The mark of the
British Elite And Shoddy Titledom. Opening
the campaign for the election of ’94, he got out
flaming bills headed ‘Sir Graham Berry.’ The
knowing East Bourke burghers said, ‘That
.will block you, safe as houses.' Whereupon he
knocked off the title and hastily renewed the
bills, with plain ‘ Graham Berry,’ not even
Mistered. Then came the question whether he
should drop the title in standing for the Federal
Convention. He decided not. He came round
to his bold burghers and said, ‘After all it
don’t matter.’ ‘Well, just you see,’ says one.
He persisted this time in placarding himself 1 Sir
Graham Berry.’ Let Turner store it up and
remember the old farce called ‘ My Turn Next.’
Ah, Berry remembers now his advice to Deakin
not to take the title. The East Bourke Repub-
licans would have put him in.”
The overwhelming majority for Frank
Madden indicates that Melbourne’s Eastern
Suburbs electorate is as much devoted to Fat Man
to-day as in the time when Gillies planned it out
for his own constituency. Madden is essentially
a member for Fat. Kew and Camberwell, the
principal pair of eastern subs., vote for him not
on account of his violent Tory sentiments (which
have often seemed abominable to his own side of
the ’Ouse), but because he is a brother to his
Waxellency the deputy-Governor, and, therefore,
a “respectable” person. Caulfield and other
horsey outlying districts support him because he
is a Y.R.C. Tory, the horsey soul being mostly
undemocratic. Thereabouts he travels to the top
of the poll on his turf ticket. Nobody considers
F.M. on his political merits or private reputation.
The National Ass folks and the national horse
folks identify Frank with Fat Man and vote
accordingly. His case is quite different from that
of Gillies and Murray Smith, who rank as “ sound
Tory ” politicians
M‘Gowen, leader of the N.S.W. Labor-
party, asks that 48 hours per week shall be recog-
nised as the maximum in the public service. To
which Minister Jacob Garrard objected, on the
ground that “Government should not pay more
nor give greater privileges than private employers,
because that would be unfair to the employers.”
But does the hazy Jacob mean that the Govt,
should keep abreast of the best or the worst kind
of private employers ? The cases which M'Gowen
pointed out as the worst examples were gaol-
warders, letter-sorters, and tram and railway men.
There are no private employers running gaols and
post offices, anyhow, so there is no private standard
to go by. Therefore, should these state servants,
in fairness to the private employe, be brought in
line with the bank where the hours are about seven
per day, or the non-union bakery where they are
13 or more ?

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Treasurer Fysh, in forecasting a surplus of
£62,290 for 1898, remarks proudly that this will reduce
the Tasmanian deficit to £281,645. The Bulletin rises to
state, with profound regret, that it will do nothing of the
kind ; it will only reduce the deficit to somewhere about
£679,000. Fysh only takes the cash overdraft into ac-
count ; the other part of the deficit —the part that is
covered by Treasury bills and stock —is judiciously dropped
out of sight.
* * * *
According to latest returns, N.S.W. pays about
£3 13s. per cent, on its public debt; Victoria, about £3
16s. per cent. N.S.W. reduced its average by borrowing
8J millions since the interest-rate went down to 3 per
cent., while Victoria only borrowed two millions at the
new rate.
* * • •
The relative debts of the seven provinces are an
interesting study. The order of the provinces, in differ-
ent years, putting the one with the biggest debt per head
of population at the top of the list, and the one with the
smallest debt at the bottom, was as follows :
1861. 1871. 1881. 1891. 1897.
Victoria Maoriland Maoriland Q’nsland Q’nsland
N.S.W. Q’nsland Q’nsland S.A. S.A.
S.A. N.S.W. S.A. Maoriland Maoriland
Maoriland Victoria Victoria Tasmania Tasmania
Q’nsland Tasmania N.S.W. N.S.W. N.S.W.
Tasmania S.A. W.A. Victoria W.A.
W.A. W.A. Tasmania W.A. Victoria
The most remarkable feature in these lists is the way
Victoria has worked down from the position of the most-
indebted to that of the least-indebted province, and how
Queensland has worked up from nearly the best position
on the list to the very worst.
* * » *
The Colonial Bank (Melb.) improves, though
gradually. The profits for the half-year ended 30th
September, 1897, are £3312, whereas last half they were
only £3043, and at 30th September, 1896, they were £2004,
which showsa large improvement. During the half-year the
Colonial has collected £33,000 in calls, and it has wiped
off about £50,000 of its reconstructed deposits, reducing
them to £1,436,732. This is a very small reduction, still
it is a move in the right direction. On the other side of
the _ case the business appears, so fat, to be about
stationary. Note circulation has decreased by
over £10,000; bills in circulation by £35,000; and new'
deposits are £42,000 less than they were six months ago.
Probably the net result is that things are better, still
the improvement is very slow.
The writing-off of lost capital is still postponed.
The profit for this and several previous half-years has
been applied towards writing-off losses, but still it is
estimated that £480,561 of capital will have to be can-
celled before things are straight, and as even now, despite
the vigorous collection of calls, there is only £474,000
ordinary capital paid up and available for writing-off, the
operation has necessarily to be postponed. Moreover,
the old shareholders are going broke and forfeiting
their shares very rapidly—the forfeited capital now
amounts to £137,000, and includes 35,492 shares, nearly
one-fourth of the whole concern. Therefore, the
loss to the shareholders who remain will be all the greater,
and the Colonial, when it gets things fairly disentangled,
will be left with an ordinary capital almost too small to be
worth mentioning. Meanwhile, the £480,561 loss is put
down as a debit balance as the most logical way out of the
difficulty, and is being slowly wiped off out of profits. If
there was any prospect of the good times coming back, it
might be totally extinguished in this fashion without any
formal cancellation of capital; as it is, the process of
getting square is rather slow. One open question
is whether even the proposed writing-off of the
whole paid-up ordinary capital is sufficient to cover
losses. The earnings are barely equal to £2 4s. per cent,
per annum on the preference capital alone—that is to say,
assuming the writing-down process was completed, the
Colonial would claim to be a solvent concern with a
capital (all preference shares) of £304,000, and yet would
be earning, on this half-year’s figures, less than 2J per
cent, thereon. And this despite the fact that it only pays
21 per cent.—the lowest rate going—on its reconstructed
deposits. If it paid 4£ per cent., as the National and
Victoria do, it would show a loss of £ll,OOO On the half-
year.
In short, the problem is : The Colonial claims
that its preference capital is practically intact, yet
though it gets four-fifths of its deposits at an amazingly
low rate—even assuming that other banks pay only 3 per
cent, on an average, the 2J4 per cent, rate gives the
Colonial an advantage of £7lBO per annum—it is earning
less than 2J per cent, on the capital which it says is intact.
Assuming that a bank, with all its bad debts written off,
and getting its deposits at the very moderate rate of 3
per cent, can realise one per cent, on a very moderate capi-
tal of £304,000, which doesn’t seem much to ask, the
Colonial should earn about £4600 per half-year. And it
certainly doesn’t-
* * * *
The National Bank (Melbourne) continues its
steady improvement. The profits for the half-year are
£18,276, as compared with £14,712 last half-year, £11,707
at September, 1896, and £10,523 at March, 1896. The
latter represented dead low-water mark ; at March, 1894,
the best half-year after the reconstruction, the profits
were 1 31,000, and from that point they dropped continu-
ously for two years till they looked like becoming wholly
extinct. Since then the earnings have steadily increased,
while the saving of interest by the repayment of the
reconstructed 4J per cent, deposits has also swelled the
profits. The usual 5 per cent, dividend on preference
shares (£7645) is paid, and the balance, with previous
accumulations, leaves £41,841 at credit of profit and loss.
The profits are about £ll7s. per cent, per annum on the
entire capital (£2,010,549), and the National will have to
get the half-year’s net revenue up from £lB,OOO to over
£50,000 before it will have earned even a 5 per cent
dividend for its ordinary shareholders, so the prospects
of the latter crowd are sombre indeed. At present,
after the preference people have got their dividend, there
only remains enough to give the ordinary shareholders
about £1 ss. per cent, per annum, and under these
conditions the ordinary shareholders feel very
ordinary. There is no visible probability that
the National, with its capital raised from £1,000,000
to over £2,000,000, will ever be able to pay the
glorious 16 per cent, dividends it used to revel in; in
the best boom days, its biggest year’s profit was £ 166,000,
and, even if it cot back to that figure, it could only pay
8 per cent, at the best. Also, with its present <. arnings of
£33,000 a year, it will take a very long time to get back
to that figure.
The National looks very solid, however, despite
the smallness of its earnings. During the past half-year
it has reduced its reconstructed deposits from over
£3,000,000 to £1,650,000, and a few more months should
see them wiped out altogether. It has cash and liquid
securities to the amount of £2,196,947, which is equal to
32 per cent, of its liabilities to the public. This item
shows a decline of £660,000 as compared with last half-
year, and deposits have decreased £l,loo,ooo—mainly, no
doubt, through the release of £1,350,000 of the recon-
structed money. There is only one feature in the balance-
sheet which excites suspicion or calls for serious explana-
tion.. Last half-year the holding of Government and
municipal debentures decreased by £493,000. This half
it has further decreased by £173,000, so the National
has evidently been selling out on a very large scale.
Almost all Government and municipal securities have
lately been at a high premium, and if that premium is
included in the profit, it may easily be that the ordinary
earnings of the business haven’t been increasing nearly
so much as appears on the surface of things.
* » * *
The Permanent Trustee Co. of N.S.W., which
looks after the interests of t he widow and orphan at a very
moderate rate, realised £1671 profit for the year up to
30th Sept., as compared with £1035 for 1896, so the im-
provement is a very handsome one. A 5 per cent. div.
absorbs £1250, and the other £421, added to previous
balance, leaves £1251 to credit of profit and loss.
♦ * * *
The Perpetual Executors and Trustees Associa-
tion (Melbourne) for the year ending 30th September,
1897, realised £555 net profit— this after allowing £403 for
depreciation. As 1896 showed a profit of only £B5, and
1895 a loss of £197, while in 1894 there was another loss
of £204, the improvement is a very large and solid one.
The net profit, it is true, equals barely 2 per cent, on
capital, and there is no dividend, and the shares (£1 paid
up) which boomed up to 58s. 6d. in February, ’9l, are now
too painful a subject to mention ; still things seem to be
distinctly on the mend. The co. now manages to earn
£1979 for the year by an expenditure of £2769, so the
trustee business only leaves a dead loss of £790, to be made
up out of the interest earned by the investment of the
association’s own capital. The investments earn nearly 4 j
percent, all round, so apparently they are good securi-
ties and worth the value set against them, or something
reasonably near it. The great disaster of the year was a
legal decision which held the co. responsible for £2749
trust money deposited in two unfortunate banks, and if
the appeal to the Privy Council goes wrong it will make
a heavy loss on one of .these items (City of Melbourne
Bank deposit, £1441). Probably this loss and the costs
will swallow up another three years’ profits.
f * f y
Tooth and Co., Limited,, joint-stock beer con-
structors, Sydney, show a half-year’s net profit of £35,574,
against £42,555 for the dry half of the year, and £35,772
for the half ending September, 1896. A dividend of 7 per
cent, absorbs £31,500 ; £5OO is added to reserve (making
it £75,000); and £2500 written off plant.
* * » *
The S.A. State Land Bank balance-sheet shows
that it has got through the first complete year with £686
to the good. As the business necessarily started in a very
small way, the earnings are correspondingly small, but
the fact that there is a profit of any kind after paying all
preliminary outlays looks well. The institution had ad-
vanced £231,000, up to 30th June ; its expenses were but
£3509 for the year; and the interest in arrear was only
£l3 12s. which says a great deal for the management so
far. Very few privately-managed banks make so good a
showing in their early stages.
* * * *
There is a lot of pathos in some balance-sheets
nowadays. That huge, London-managed pastoral cor-
poration, the Union Mortgage and Agency Co. of Austra-
lia, announces a year’s profit for 1896 of £9087, equal to
19s. 6d. per cent, per annum on capital. And its offshoot,
the huge Australian Estates and Mortgage Co., earned
£2414, equal to 4s. per cent, per annum on its capital
of li millions! It all looks like a picture of an ex-mil-
lionaire prospecting for crusts in a dust-box.

All Melbourne is now ringing with the story
of how a remorseless attorney took down the spendthrift
signer of a big promissory-note.
» * * •
The number of undefended debt cases in the
Perth local court is significant. One recent morning
there were 64 of them.
* * * *
O. H. James, the Father of Smellbourne’s land-
boom, was, for years, a rather scandalous non-insolvent,
but, in his new character of insolvent, the man with the
orthodox beard promises to be a more extraordinary
person than ever. A while back, his estate was com-
pulsorily sequestrated at the instance of somebody, who
would fain have fetched C. H. James up for examination
in the court. Thus far, he has avoided filing a schedule
by the simple device of making himself scarce, and his
counsel, with fine legal effrontery, states that the missing
man doesn’t care a boom for the court. “ Everyone knows
why the schedule is not filed,” says barrister Duffy.
“ The insolvent is quite willing(!) to file his schedule,’’’
but he declines to move at the instigation of any accursed
creditor who “persecutes” him. The persecution of O
H. James ! !
• * * *
The landing of German beet-sugar at so low a
price in Sydney is causing the Bananaland planters to
quake. They now begin to see that their only hope of
securing the Australian market lies in joining hands with
the Federationists.
* • » *
Big row. in an Australian life-office over the
death of a magnate who had lately taken out a heavy
insurance. The row arises out of the allegedly-fishy cir-
cumstances under which the risk was passed.
* * * *
The managing clerk of a big Australian usurer
is dying in a lunatic asylum. For weeks prior to his being
put away his mania was trying to odculate to what
amount a £5 loan (at his employer’s usual rate of interest)
would reach if unpaid for 100 years. The appalling huge-
ness of the amount totalled for even ten years is
have unhinged his mind, and it is doubtful if he could
ever have arrived at the amount for the full period except
with the aid of logarithms.
* * * *
Part of B. H. Prop, ’s new concentrating-mill
was set going this week ; and the new British works are
also ready. Also, there is a great activity in the con-
struction of buddies for re-treating the slimes, and gene-
rally there are indications that the directors are convinced
they will have to depend upon the mechanical process for
some time yet. Hancock, after whose designs about half
the mills are built, claims to have, during the last few
weeks, made important improvements in the construction
of the hutches. Indeed, the concentrating process has
been so improved that already it bears little likeness to
what was done at the South when the first jigs were set
in motion, three years ago.
* * * *
The results from zinc-concentrate shipments
from Broken Hill have exceeded expectations, and con-
signments from the B.H. Prop, and Block 14 have now
been sent to port.
* * * *
Acting presumably on the detectives’ advice
the Broken Hill Proprietary 00. puts up its offer of
reward in connection with the mine fire to £9OO, in addi-
tion to the Govt.’s £lOO. Also, while the Govt, offers a
free pardon to any accomplice, the co. offers a free
passage to any part of the world to the man who gives
information which will convict the supposed incendiarist.
By the way, the extremely casual Brunker, in the Govt,
proclamation, recites that “ the said fire resulted in the
death of three men.” No doubt it did, indirectly, but the
jury which “ sat on” the three men was blocked from
inquiring into the cause of the fire by the intimation that
the men were not in the mine when the fire broke out
that, therefore, they were only in the same position as a
fireman who is killed after having answered a call to an
already burning’ building. As to the reward, it is worth
noting that it is by no means certain that incendiarism
was the cause ; no jury has found to that effect even. But
if it wasn’t incendiarism it was accident ; and accidents of
this sort mean lax supervision ; and lax supervision means
■. So, in the circumstances, it is just as well to
suggest incendiarism, anyway.

The last manager of the long-abandoned Burra
Burra (S.A.) copper-mine, which was supposed to have
given right out, reported that there was a sulphide
deposit in the bottom, and it is now intended to test the
deeper ground with a diamond drill. It seems a good
gamble.

An effort is being made in N.S.W. Parliament
to place copper in the same category as other metals that
may be mined on private lands. Why it was ever omitted
is impossible to understand, unless one takes a trip West
and notices the quantity of copper land held by M’sL.C.
• * » •
The writer’s old friend and favorite—North
Nyrnagee and the Sabbath tipster:—“ An excellent
report has been received from the mining manager. He
writes most hopefully of the property, saying it contains
no less than four distinct lodes. He is sinking a main
shaft, and, in his examination of the old workings, came
across an untouched lode at the end of a drive, which he
describes as being so valuable that he feels confident
future developments in it will disclose something good.”
It will probably soon transpire that the old co. abandoned
this mine because it did not want to he worried work-
ing so many rich copper lodes. That, too, seems to be
the trouble with the present holders. Before a pick was
put in the ground things were booming, but now so many
lodes have been found, “ so valuable, etc.,’’that shares can
be had at less than half-price.

Palkner’s mine, Pambula, N.S.W., reported for
non-fulfilment of labor conditions. Nothing this, when
you are used to it; but Bull and others interested in Aus-
tralian mines, and resident thousands of miles away, get
a nasty shock when anything of this sort happens.
* * * *
The public will soon know something more
definite about the Mt. David gold mine, N.S.W. Next
week the 10-head battery makes a start, and the running
of an additional 20 heads is promised in two months.
This will tell a tale, whether the lode when taken its full
width—from 6ft. to 12ft. —will average an ounce to the
ton, such as reports and small crushings indicate, or
whether the previous owner, Crozier, before selling,
gouged out the best parts of the lode in thin streaks,
leaving by no means ounce “ tack.”

* * * *
From Pambula : “At Jingera, developments
are somewhat slow from the want of local treatment of
ore. The silver ore being shipped away every month by
the Jingera Mineral Proprietary is very rich. There are
thousands of tons stacked at grass, which should give a
handsome return when a plant is erected that will treat
it on the ground.” It was exactly for mines such as these
are alleged to be that works like those at Illawarra have
been erected, at enormous cost, and, if it won’t pay mines
not far from one of the best harbors in Australia to ship
ore away to such w r orks, the chances are that the mines
won’t pay to work at all.
• * * »
N.S.W. Mines Minister Smith defends the
action of his department in sending out circulars in sup-
port of a mining manual to be issued by one Critchley
Parker, who combines mining with society journalism, on
the ground that one of the Australias had subsidised the
work to the extent of £6OO and a free railway-pass.
Stated that £lO will cover the cost. The Bulletin
declines to believe it. Have not these circulars been sent
all over the world? If so, the mere gumming of the
envelopes would have cost a tenner. Is it not well known
that other similar works are being prepared by really
competent men, including the financial editor of Sydney
D.T. ? Were not the sample proofs of the way in which
mining cos. would be listed in the book taken from
Skinner’s Manual? That Mr. Critchley Parker’s will be
a modest publication by an extremely modest author is
indicated by the sample cover which has on the side and
on the back the name, Critchley Parker, in gold lettering !
* * * *
Although manager Look, of the Wentworth
mine, has managed to sneak in about 100 men from
Bendigo, it will be found a poor victory when all is
reckoned up. They are to receive higher wages than
those offered the old hands, and are to be fed and housed
free of charge until things are in a settled state. Lock,
too, is likely to suffer somewhat from the isolation of the
stand he has taken. Generally speaking, the big-mine
managers of N.S.W., whether representing English or
local Australian co.’s, are not anxious to reduce wages to
£2 2s. a week. It is recognised that good miners are
worth something more than that, and indifferent men are
dear at that wage. In some of the shallower mines at
Bendigo it is the custom to work 455. and 355. men toge-
ther, and no doubt the 10 men got over are of the latter
standard. The men on strike might as well throw up the
sponge, and accept the consolation that neither Lock nor
the Wentworth Co. will make much out of the victory

* * * *
W. R. Lawson, in the Contemporary, predicts
a cold day for the silver-mine holder. The chief buying,
he says, must come—at least, has for years come—from
the East. India is the largest consumer, notwithstanding
the closing of the mints. “China seems to have taken
in the first seven months only £109,308, as compared with
£603,000 in the corresponding period last year. Bad as this
is, Japan beats it with a decline from > 832,000 last year
to £40,000 this.” He argues that China will go in’ for
paper. It is quite likely, though, that the figures as
they stand are misleading. For instance : The silver
savings of the inhabitants of India are estimated at
1,312,500,0000 z. In famine-time hitherto much of this
has been taken to the mints ; hut, the mints being now
closed, much of it has probably been exported during
the recent famine to the nearest silver-using country
where a market for it was open. All the same, “ Sinking
Silver ” is a very formidable array of figures and deduc-
tions.

An abridged prospectus of the Southern Long
Tunnel Gold Mine Co. appeared in last week’s Bulletin.
The property consists of 53 acres at Rifle Butts Gully,
Walhalla, 22£ chains to the south of the Long Tunnel
co. s ground. 696 feet of tunnelling have been driven
with sufficiently encouraging results, and it is proposed
to erect a 20-head battery to crush stone from 400 ft. of
backs already in sight at No. 3 tunnel. From a total
issue of 40,000 shares of 10s. each, vendor asks for 14,000
paid up. 26,000 shares offered to the public at Is. on
application and Is. 6d. on allotment ; £l5OO to cover cash
payment for the work already put into the property, and
all expenses of flotation, leaving £1750 to the credit of
the co. Prospectuses obtainable from W. H. Vivian,
Stock Exchange, Sydney, or J. H. Butler, 66 Elizabeth-
street, Melbourne.
* • * *
A funny little gamble in Mount Deddick shares
is being worked by a group of Melb. jobbers. Mount
Deddick is a property which, lurking in one of Gipps-
land’s darkest places, affects an air of awful mystery. The
Argus for a long time quoted it as a gold-mine, but lately
it has figured as a silver-lead mine, and possibly it will
next chip into the copper-boom if the game can be kept
going. But the name, Deddick, suggests a funeral in the
near future. The Bulletin has, as yet, ascertained nothing
authentic about Mt. Deddick.
• * • *
A Gympie correspondent calls The Bulletin’s
attention to the fact that a recent week’s crushing on
that field totalled 65500 z. for 2462 tons, dividends for
same week being £13,700; and, in further mentioning
that the South N.Z. Junction recently dropped on to
7280 z. from 46 tons, remarks that, “ patchy ” as Gympie
is considered, it ought in these circumstances to receive a
lot more attention from Southern speculators. But
Southern mining investors—though they think Gympies
all right as a stamping-ground for retired practical
miners, who, being on the spot, can best tell when to get
in or out—don’t cotton to the lottery business. They
prefer to this “mining-consultation ” kind of thing to have
to deal with a big, well-defined lode, where there is pro-
mise of definite, if small, returns. A low-grade reef with
something like certainty ahead is more in their line than
the chance of patches, however rioh. Foramininggamhle,
however, you can hardly beat Gympie.
• * • *
“At time of writing,” says a Bulletin corres-
pondent, “ a big law-suit seems almost inevitable between
the Tas. Government and one or other of the gridiron
railway syndicates. The House will probably attempt to
smoothe things over by repudiating Ministerial promises
to the Northern co., thus conserving the Great, Western
co.’s interests, but that the Northern co. Will tamely
submit to being choked off Mt. Lyell without even test-
ing the strength of some of Braddon-Fysh’s notoriously
shipshod legislation is too much to expect—of a syndi-
cate.”
* • * *
The country between the coast and Mount
Lyell (Tas.) threatens to be gridironed with railways.
The latest proposal is by a small syndicate to run a line
from Deloraine to Rosberry, there joining the Emu Bay
line. No promoters’ shares or other boodle. The motive
may be found in landed interests.
• * * •
Big things said of North Mt. Lyell, Tas. A
conservative estimate discloses a million’s worth of ore in
sight. An easy route found for the railway. Two ore
bodies—one the eastern, 50ft. wide, and the-western 30ft.
Enormous deposits of high-grade copper ore, with gold
and silver ready for the smelters, and when thev are
ready shareholders need have no fear of the result.
Claimed that the ores are richer than those of Mt. Lyell.
But the lode is not nearly as big. The present eo. has a
capital of half-a-million, and Bull for 200,000 shares finds
a working capital of £105,000.
• « * »
The Bulletin is waiting for the syndicate to
come along and put the whole of the Tasmanian flyspeek
through the smelters. It would pay handsomely—if the
native brass of its Douglases and Dobsons didn’t prove too
refractory.
* » * •
On one of the Hampton Plains (W.A.) alluvial
rushes breaking out, the H.P. co. sent a man down to
turn the gold-seekers off the block. He arrived in time
to peg out a well-situated claim for himself, and as he saw
he had no show of getting the men to clear, he told them
that if they let his claim alone while he went back and
reported to the co., he would make no attempt to shift
them. He made his report and returned, no doubt in-
tending to put men on his claim or to sell it. He found
a great number of men there, and his own claim pegged
out into four, each of which was being industriously
worked. He then decided to use his authority to turn
everybody off, but no one would shift; and next day the
Coolgardie paper said : “Mr. has given the men
leave to work the ground.” Later on it was decided to
charge each man working on the block ss. per month, and
a few weeks ago collectors were sent round to gather it
in. One weary old man, on being asked for his ss. said
nothing, but picked up his traps and walked off for Cool-
gardie with a sad, tired look.

Stated that Gov. Buxton meditates re-
turning to England before another year is up. He
has not hit it with local society, has quarrelled
with several politicians, and is generally supposed
not to be on speaking terms with a very prominent
Minister of the Crown, whose wife was ignored
in connection with a social function at Govt.
House.
* * * •
At the Mayor of Adelaide’s ball no ladies
from Govt. House were present—a fact, which has
greatly ingratiated H. Ex. in civic circles. Dr.
Leith Napier was dressed as a fireman —an appro-
priate rig, say his medical enemies, who declare
that he is getting ready for the Future,
* * * •
S. A. Judge Boucaut’s liver was in bad
order, the other day. A boy named O’Brien
escaped from custody recently and made for his
home (where his mother, it is alleged, was lying
ill), and was arrested next morning. Sentence
five years hard labor, first three years in irons , and
first month in solitary confinement ! O’Brien has
yet to be tried for a subsequent escape from
Adelaide Gaol, so that he will probably get life
and floggings for that in December.
* * • *
The Chinaman, See Chong, who was tried
for, and convicted of, murder at Bundaberg (Q.),
and who subsequently suicided, had no lawyer to
defend him on his trial for his life. He did not
understand the English language, and the Judge
who sentenced him said the interpreter employed
by the Crown in the case seemed to know as little
English as the prisoner.
THE COUNTRY EDITOR’S “START."
(Editor of BIG SCRUB BUNYIP, who has just circularised his list of dead-heads to pay up or be
struck off, is interrupted by the noisy entrance of Mr. M‘Cocky, who has had the
BUNYIP “ on the nod” since its start, six years ago.)
MR. M'COCKY: “ Never did I expect to receive such a letter as this from you! Never did I
think you could be so ungrateful to ME, one of the first to take your paper and give
you a start!”
Gov. Brassey wrote a letter to Chief Jus-
cice Madden to sooth the injured feelings of his
ex-Waxcellency re the .£lO a day which the Old
Man of the Sea told a Herald scribe he has to
pay to the Acting-Governor when he (B.) goes
away on holiday. Madden professes not to be
annoyed. But why should he be ! The Bulletin
has a notion that the sensitiveness of exalted per-
sonages on the subject of their “screw” springs
less from pride than from conscience pricking
them with the thought that they don’t earn it
The man who honestly earns his money doesn’t
mind anyone knowing that he gets it. Indeed, if
it is a big sum, he is apt to be rather proud of it.
But merely (alleged) ornamental persons squirm
on the subject of their wages. They have a sneak-
ing fear that they don’t deserve any.
* * * *
The notorious Thriftiness of a certain Aus-
tralian Governor comes very hard upon The Bul-
letin. Every time the old gentleman shows the
most ordinary and pardonable (not to say praise-
worthy) regard for his financial interests, a
hundred correspondents bombard this scurrilous
journal with paragraphs to the effect that His
Excellency is an outrage on sassiety. The latest
crime charged against him is in connection with a
forthcoming fancy-dress ball. “The waiters and
attendants are to be faked-up for the occasion by
contract,” it seems. And why not? There's no
fault to find with a contract under the circs., and
The Bulletin declines to point a finger of scorn
at any imported capitalist who conducts an
occasional burst of extravagance on commercial
principles.
• * * *
The exploits of the Jackdaw of Rheims
have been eclipsed by the magpie of a Queensland
coast town. The rector of the local Anglican
Church, preaching there on a recent Sunday, feel-
ingly announced that on that day he celebrated
his 19th anniversary as pastor of the district, and,
saying that he was growing old, perorated on his
approaching demise and the severance of the holy
ties which had bound him to his congregation.
Hereupon one of his daughters sobbed aloud in
church and was carried out in hysterics. Another
daughter followed suit, shrieking aloud and faint-
ing in the pew. She was also gently carried out.
After these scenes one could have heard an ele-
phant fall in the sacred fane. The pastor was
affected to tears, and the congregation looked as
solemn as a cageful of boiled owls. But the good
pastor’s favorite magpie came to relieve the pain-
ful tension, and change the spirit of the scene.
Perching on one of the open windows the bird
trilled forth shrilly a full verse of “ There’s nae
luck aboot the hoose.” The effect was electrical.
Every head was buried in a handkerchief, a wild
tittering arose, and the service closed abruptly.
* * • *
If Sydney Sunday Times isn’t bent upon
ruthlessly stripping us of the last rag of our illusions
about lovely women, why does it let loose the lady-
scribe who so gaily gives her sex away over its
personal cleanliness by talking of bathing as
though it were a new idea and an item of luxury,
and solemnly descanting on “ the proper time to
take a bath ? ” Even if, as the ingenuous penlady
complacently admits, women will invent all sorts
of excuses “where cold water is concerned,” she
might have allowed the male miscreant still
to suppose that the objection is, not to the
water, but to the coldness —instead of nakedly
forcing upon him the .obvious inference from her
assertion that “every one of us should take a bath
of some kind every day.’’ The male ruffian, as a
rule, doesn’t need to be reminded of that little
matter, and deluded man would have much pre-
ferred to remain in ignorance of the fact that his
divinity—upon her own sweet showing—appar-
ently wants washing a good deal more than her
worshipper.

Dr. Macgregor, M.L. Inspector of Prisons,
&c., states as an interesting fact that he knows of
a lialf-imbecile girl who has had, up to date, five
“ illegitimates ” by different fathers, and who is
likely to “keep on producing them.”
“ Timi,” on “ Let him marry the girl” :—■
Years ago a great, hulking brute in M.L. ravished a
decent, pretty little seamstress whom he had met casually
in a lonely spot. Police heard the screams, and arrested
the man in flagrante delicto. The ravisher had an influen-
tial brother, and, though committed for trial, the criminal
was bailed out. Sundry clergymen got at the girl and
induced her to marry the accused, and a nolle prosequi
was entered. Within four years the poor little woman had
prosecuted her strangely-acquired husband for several
brutal assaults. I was in court twice and saw the
bruises, so I know. Yes, “ Let him marry the girl ” !
* • • *
Apropos the censorship of literature by
N.S.W. gaol-officials, it is stated that in the Q.
penal establishment at St. Helena the library
edition of Shakespeare has “ Venus and Adonis ”
and “Tarquin and Lucrece ’’ excised, while
“ Pericles, Prince of Tyre,” remains in its original
and undiluted shape in the volume—which opens
of itself, from constant usage, at act 4 of that
play. How perfect that censor’s knowledge of
English classics must be! The Bible is also
admitted holus-bolus !

A very big black snake had a narrow
escape, recently, from biting the ‘ ‘ special ” of a
Sydney daily, who is “doing” the Clarence dis-
trict. In blissful unconsciousness of its danger,
the reptile spent the night in bed with the press-
man.

Wollongong (N.S.W.) churches have been
endeavoring (by special prayer !) to get the
Almighty on the side of the Government’s badly-
received Kembla harbour proposal, as against the
Illawarra harbour scheme. The Opposition should
follow suit. A tug-of-prayer would be an interest-
ing novelty, and the defeated supplicators could
always put the blame on the Devil.

memorial to Milton in Westminster Abbey. “He
has in the inscription,” said Johnson, “bestowed
more words upon himself than upon Milton.”

SUS SALVATOR!
[Lines to the Kameruka’s pig which swam ashore with a life-
line from the wrecked vessel and thus saved the lives of
all hands.]
O hand me the shwate Harp of Erin this minute !
To other dumb craturs let bards tune their lay !
The watch-dog, the horse, the gazelle are not in it!
The rint-payin’ gintleman’s hero to-day I
For years you’ve been misunderstood, under-rated,
Regarded as useless until you had died,
With rubbish and ridicule always malthrated,
Until, as a carpse, you put on lots of “ side.”
Through life, sure, your ways have been terribly mirish,
You’ve met all your woes wid an ear-piercing squeak,
But in Death you have always been splindidly Irish,
Rewardin’ your slayers wid plinty of “ cheek.”
In the distant Green Isle, by the ample fire lyin’,
You dozed in the fragrance of turf from the bog,
While your masther your merits as footstool was tryin’—
But now, by St. Pathrick, you’ve “gone the whole
hog.”
Whin the storm-beaten ship on the reef came a crasher,
You tuk, widout Cavill, the life-line ashore ;
No hero, as yet, has done deed that was “ rasher
But you’d saved the lives in a “ cabin ” before !
From the pluck of the pig let a lesson be taken,
No parson or praste taches moral more high :
He was riskin’ his life to be “ savin’ their bacon ”
The bacon they’ll ate just as soon as he’ll die !
MALVA.

The spirit of the age—whisky.
One or two Northern papers are express-
ing serious doubts as to whether the tick really
has abandoned the Western country and the stock
become immune. On one or two stations in the
Mitchell district the pest has reappeared, and the
cattle are suffering terribly.
The Folchi affair is America’s newest sen-
sation. Folchi is a poor painter, who bolted with
the lovely daughter of Don Carlos. They got to
New York, incog., but had to reveal their identity
to the police, through being robbed of a valise,
containing all their money. The N.Y. Herald
offered Folchi £l2OO for an article, but he refused.
He is coining money as a portrait-painter. His
wife, the Princess, has taken a billet at a fashion-
able milliner’s, and works 10 hours a day making
bonnets.

• * • *
It is a beautiful modern custom in Melb.
for the employer to deduct a small sum from his
employes’ wages every week as a hospital contri-
bution. Could not the idea be extended so that
tenants could deduct, say, Is. a week off the rent for
the same charitable purpose, and borrowers take
a hospital contribution off the quarter’s interest ?
It's just as well to go the whole logical hog when
one is on a charity errand.
• * * *
Dear Bulletin, —I was at Melb. Collins-
street Baptist Church the other night when the
Indian mission held a farewell meeting ; 35 mis-
sionaries were being sent at a cost of .£7OO.
(Joining out, I watched th-- congregation, and not
a single penny did the wretched, half-starved
woman, who sells matches on the steps, get out of
the whole assemblage of nearly 1000. <£7oo for
Indian souls, and not a cent, for starving Aus-
tralians’ bodies. —M‘G.
* * » *
Williams, the half-caste lately sentenced
to death at Mudgee, N.S.W., began life as a
shepherd at Merrendee, near Mudgee. He there
became associated with a woman, and, while
living with her, her daughter (by her previous
husband) had a child, of whom Williams was
reputedly the father. These two, mother and
daughter, continued to live with him, and each
raised a family. Sixteen years ago a clergyman
interfered, and persuaded Williams to marry—
leaving him the option of choosing between mother
and daughter. He took the daughter, and the
prosecutrix in this case was one of the offspring of
that marriage.

tion.

Cause and effect. The Adelaide gaol-
breaking trio, recaptured near Blanchetown,
escaped from the local lock-up by knocking a hole
through the cell wall «ith a bucket-handle.
“Constable Shanks had supplied them with water
twenty minutes before they broke out.” Exactly.
Had he supplied them with beer they would
probably have stayed quite contentedly in quod.
Water - drinking is responsible for many mis-
demeanors.
* * * •
The usual weekly earthquake struck Wel-
lington (M.L.) a little worse than usual the other
day. Some strangers fled into the street, and
camped out there for several hours, and a, foreign
lady who was in a bath when the convulsion came
was so badly scared that she bolted with nothing
on but one small boot and a large sponge. But
the old residents take it very much as a matter of
course now. It is 40 years since anybody was
killed by a Wellington earthquake, the victim
then being a German who had a large mirror
shaken down on him while in bed and his system
filled with broken glass and quicksilver.
* * » *
Chief Justice Darley, at Dubbo Circuit
Court, the other day, vindicating the character of
police, whose evidence had been aspersed: “If
N.S.W. pclice err at all, they err in favor of the
accused.” Yet the C. J. is supposed to speak from
the Bench with gravity, and at least an average
amount of knowledge of his subject.
• * » *
The Melb. detective force is just now
agitated less by the pursuit of the wicked than by
fierce anger against a scion of the family of the
respected local chief of police. Young Mr. C. H.
Chomley has written a story for the Argus, which
he begins by the following expression of opinion
upon the men commanded by his pa:—
I have been induced to write this story, not because I
pretend to any skill in the use of the pen, nor yet be-
cause, as some might think, I am a detective by profes-
sion ; it is a profession, in fact, for which I entertain but
small regard, since it involves, upon the part of those who
wish to succeed In it, a surrender of the attributes which
distinguish honorable men for those more appropriate to
a crafty savage, or wild beast in pursuit of his prey.

Items from recent Christian Endeavour
conference in Melbourne. Rev. J. King: “An
effective part of Christian Endeavour work is
prayer and an assiduous collecting of funds.”
Rev. J. Nicholson : “ Endeavourers should give ;
the usage of antiquity favoured the bestowal of a
tenth in God’s work.” Rev. Hitchcock : “They
should try to make up their minds to give a penny
a week to the cause, or even a penny a day.”
Rev. Watsford : “ There were plenty of men and
women waiting to take up their cross if there were
any funds to send them forth.” The chairman :
“ It was pitiful that God’s work should be crippled
for want of a little money.” In short, “Gib it
tixpen’.”
Told in Sydney military circles that two
officers high in the service recently had a scrap by
night in a lane off George-street—the question at
issue being the inevitable gilt haired charmer. The
onlookers’ joy rose to concert-pitch when the senior
officer pranced round menacingly and vociferated,
“ Don’t you dare touch my cap, sir. Don’t dare
to lay a hand on me.” But the other fellow did
dare, and after that the language settled down to
plain blanky mingled with the explosive puffing of
the angry militaires when they came up to
breathe.

THE SENSATIONAL ADELAIDE STALLING-CASE.
The accused, Nixon, Mrs. Nixon, and her friend , Addison, chairman of the State Bank.
The other day business was stopped in
Melb. City Court by the hoarse and irreverent
guffawing of some desperado in the passage.
Panton, P.M., arose to his greatest altitude and
almost shrieked at the nearest constable : “ Bring
them in, sir ! ” and then his Worship sat back
glaring, awaiting his victim. But the policeman
returned, pale and trembling, to say that the
culprit was Nicolson, P.M. Panton was flabber-
gasted. Bring him in !” he said, with the accent
of a man who defies you to obey him. The other
P.M. was not brought in. But had it been a lay-
man, particularly a layman in a shocking hat !

Albury, N.S.W., thinks its site entitles it
to be the Federal capital. Putting aside the fact
that other places have claims a d d site better,
the only point in Albury’s favor is that its summer
climate wouldn’t be bad training for the tempera-

steamer Kameruka, saved by the pigs swimming
ashore with a life-line, ate bacon for breakfast
after reaching Sydney. Man’s ingratitude !

“ Pedagogue ” to The Bulletin :
In N.S.W. Public Schools, when an inspector happens
to have a fad about the “ journeyings of St. Paul,” chil-
dren are compelled by their teachers to get up batches of
such long-tailed words as Iconium, Thessalonica, Pam-
phylia &c., and this the inspector considers a “ good les-
son in geography ! ” As it is at best ancient geography,
and not preceded or supplemented by any more of the
sort, and there is not a single map of ancient geography
in any public school whereby to locate the “names,” it
looks rather as if the gentlemen in question enjoy the
sonorous roll of the big words and the impression of vast
learning they convey to the unsophisticated youngsters.

“ lii London St. Pancras Vestry the spread
of typhoid and diphtheria is coped with by the
health officer on systematic lines,”
writes The Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent. “ Each medical practitioner
is paid 2s. Gd. for reporting any
case of either he may encounter :
and in addition, for cases of
doubt, is provided with a tin
_ box contain
ing two glass
phials. In one
is a metal lint brush
for picking up the throat-
deposit and submitting for
vestry examination as to
diphtheretic germs, and
in the other a capillary
tube for taking up a
drop of blood from
a pricked finger
when typhoid is
suspected. In
24 hours the
vestry advises the medico the result of the ex-
amination. Many and many a life would have
been saved in Australian cities, especially from
diphtheria, had such a system been in force here
years ago.”
* * * *
At Lyttelton (M.L.) recently the embalmed
body of an old lady encased in a leaden coffin was,
in accordance with the terms of her will, taken
out to sea and dropped overboard.
* * * «
W.A. can beat even N.S.W. at grand-
mothers. The record Westralian granny lives in
Perth, and achieved the distinction at the early
age of 25!
* * * *
Sydney Stipendiary-Magistrate Smithers,
just returned from Japan, brings with him, he
says, “ a crowd of personal recollections, to detail
which would be a heavy task.” Most men who
visit Japan do bring away personal recollections
which they are not at all anxious to discuss in
general company. King Jack Want’s affection
for the chrysanthemum appears to be getting so
infectious that “ holiday * will probably soon be
an unknown term to N.S.W. officialdom. “My
annual Japan ! ”
One big Melb. brewing concern alona
sent 800,000 bottles of ale to Mildura, the
“ prohibitionist ” town on the Murray. No
wonder the river steamers bring away cargoes
of empty bottles from the “club ” that is run
in the settlement.
It is actually cabled that Queen Victoria
has donated <£so (7Ad. each) for the rebel of
the 1600 sufferers by the typhoid epidemic at
Maidstone. When Prince Tummy of Wales got
over his attack of fever the people of England
squandered many thousands sterling upon a his-
toric thanksgiving service and the usual loyal fire-
works.
InaN.S.W. minor court case, resulting in
a verdict for about <£so, the successful client was
charged =633 costs “as between lawyer and
client.” The client, in the course of business,
passed the solicitor’s office a couple of times a day,
and, being on what he thought friendly terms, used
to put his head round the door and ask : •“ Any-
thing fresh ? ” or, “Are you rubbing it in?” or,
“When’ll you jug him?” and now he discovers
that these several freaks of badinage are entered
up as “To consultation with Mr. , 6s. Bd.”
There are cheaper commodities in the world than
an attorney’s friendship.
* * * *
Southern lawyers, gossiping over drinks,
lately discussed a big divorce-case. There are
several (mostly-married) cos. who shine high in
the social firmament with lustre resplendent.
They are said to have each offered hubby thou-
sands if he will forego his pound of flesh,' but he
is so wealthy that he can afford to despise even
such a bribe, and swears he will have his bond.
Englishmen do take their pleasures sadly.
The Queen rides not to fields of war
Like Joan, to force our foes to flee ;
Her woman’s heart is bleeding for
The sacred cause of charitee.
Since England first to greatness woke
No monarch reigned so kind —amid
Some sixteen hundred stricken folk
She lavishes full fifty quid.
The wisdom of her men of State,
The glory of her warriors,
Did little Britain staunch, elate,
Defy the wrath of Emperors ;
But this—this is a deed apart 1
Our Queen, she makes a regal bid—
Out of the goodness of her heart
She lavishes full fifty quid.
Shame to all scoffers at command
And terrorising anarchists,
She openeth her queenly hand
(She looseneth her royal fists).
We told our joy in thousands’ worth
When Tummy’s life was spared, we did,
And she—acclaim it o’er the earth—
Sbe flings to us full fifty quid !
T.D.
