GREAT ARE SOME
PHYSICIANS.
BUT FAR GREATER ARE GOULD’S
TINY TONIC LIVER PILLS,
FOR THEY CURE AFTER PHYSICIANS
FAIL.
So say the people ? The great surprise of the present
day, and one who is most to be pitied is the man with a
weak stomach and a sluggish liver, and who can’t eat
this, and can’t drink that, is miserable and feels disagree-
able, broods over nothing, and can’t see enjoyment in
anything; sympathy is a nuisance, and friends a bore ;
one day a big appetite, and next day sorry and a great disin-
clination for food. Such is exactly how thousands of
people feel in this colony, and you are likely one of the
thousands. Up to a few years ago stomach and liver
troubles were almost unknown ; but to-day it is the ex-
ception to find one who has a good, strong digestion. In-
numerable cures have been tried and tried by sufferers
without avail, that is up to a short time ago ; but now,
owing to the efforts of some physicians in the United
States, a cure has been discovered which not only tones
the digestion and stir* up the liver, but the blood is
cleared of taint and face pimples, heart palpitation, con-
stipation, piles, &c., speedily become a thing of the past.
This preparation is put up in the form of small pills for
convenience sake, and are called Gould’s Tiny Tonic Liver
Pills.
You will very likely say, Oh ! this is another patent
medicine. This is true to a certain extent; but not a
patent medicine as the term is usually used. Gould’s
Tiny Tonic Liver Pills are a pharmaceutical preparation
the formula of which has been improved on, until now a
preparation has been gotten which will bring joy and
happiness to many a home in Australia. Price, 2s. 6d. •
six bottles, 14s. 3d., from chemists and storekeepers or
from the Australian Branch of the E. A. Gould Sons and
00., 19 Macquarie Place, Sydney.
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, October 26, 1897.
My Dear Myee—-
A real live prinoe, although a dusky one, is a
Sydney lion just now. He is Kadavu Levu, from
Fiji, tall, muscular and handsome, according to
white notions —except, perhaps, for his nose. He
was schooled in Sydney, and speaks English well.
Dressed in blue serge and brown boots, he looks a
swell, and, as he is a cricketer, he is constantly
taken for the not-yet-arrived Ranji. At Rush-
cutters Bay Oval, one day last week, his
play was closely followed by interested onlookers,
who took him for the other prince.
Ada Reeve, with her frank and boyish-looking
hubby, Gilbert (described as “the coming Fred.
Leslie ”), has been in town for the past week, ex-
hibiting some pretty frocks and Bond-street hats.
The latest things in millinery are bright and
jaunty, also shrunken in size, compared with the
style before last; but they are sensational, perpen-
dicularly instead of horizontally, as the “ ribbings ”
run up into high bows-and-ends, which it is the
triumph of the hat-artist to get more lofty and
more rampant at every effort. Ada (the original
Gay Parisienne) and her husband are so much
alike that they might pass for brother and sister.
The youthful pair, who came to Sydney to rehearse
with the “ Parisienne ” orchestra and co., created
a most favorable artistic and personal impression
at Her Majesty’s.
How’s the following for a descriptive title?
“Honorary representative for N.S. Wales of the
Associated Board of the Royal Academy of Music
and the Royal College of Music.” It beats
“ President of the Brick Lane Branch of the
United Grand Junction Ebenezer Temperance
Association ”by half a line of type. Having taken a
long, deep breath I go on to remark that it belongs
to Mr. H. Levy, who, in his capacity as above,
dealt out per post vast quarter-acre allotments of
pasteboard adorned with an embossed lion and
unicorn and much copper-plate wordiness, to a
select crowd which met Mr. Lee Williams and Mr.
S. Aitken, officials of the above many-worded
musical schools, at the Oddfellows’ Temple, last
Wednesday evening. Those two gentlemen
have arrived to hold exams, throughout Aus-
tralia and Maoriland. The party was an
extensive dress-and-diamond parade, as the invi-
tation-list took in the Nicest both socially and
musically. Between the two there was a percep-
tible difference in the gowning. The Potts-
pointers wore their most elaborate confections, and
the St. Cecilias were artistically negligent. The
fashionables were dressed and the musicians were
clothed.
The giving-out of medals and awards in connec-
tion with the above exams, happened at University
Hall on Friday afternoon, and was a most tire-
some function. All the speechifying was on that
wearisome altitude called “a high level.” Gov.
Hampden, who did the handing-over, remarked
that he didn’t know a note of music ; Chancellor
MacLaurin said ditto; and the speakers all
referred to a federated musical association
throughout the great empire, and how it would
strengthen the bonds, &c. And the only joke that
sent a quiver through the crowded hall was the
good old chestnut about bagpipe music ; the loud
tiring-off of that hoary-headed Joe Miller about
bagpipes proving as effectual as swords in routing
the enemy. The distribution was evidently hur-
riedly got up, as some of the certificates were
neither dated nor signed by examiner and local
representative. Miss Elbe Butler was adjudged
most worthy of the gold medal for piano playing.
The Boys’ Brigade had its annual outing in
Govt. House grounds last Wednesday. The boys
met at the offices in Elizabeth-street, and “ pro-
ceshed ” along through the city, scrambling for
coppers thrown by bookies who were at Tatter-
sail’s, and yelling familiar street ditties. At the
gate His Ex. received his small guests and speechi-
fied briefly, eliciting shrill cheers of thanks for the
Brigade’s hon. treasurer, Mr. J. R. Fairfax, who
had helped to fix up that afternoon’s picnic.
After that the youngsters fell to at various games,
unawed by a throng of fashionables, assembled by
invitation to help the party along. The Austra-
lian street urchin has but one title for all grown
men; they are all “Mister” to him. But the
language wasn’t particularly unbearable except
from one unknown young picnicker, whose vitriolic
remarks caused him to be summarily dropped out-
side the railings.
Since not even Presbyterianism’s worst enemy
can accuse it of a predilection for being “ bossed,”
there are likely, I hear, to be high-jinks in
Woollahra Presbyterian congregation over the
managing committee’s recent ukase that pews
must be upholstered, if at all, in the “ style and
color ” affected by a certain prominent and well-
gilt member of the congregation.
Considering that Saturday was an off-day, there
was a very fair attendance at Randwick races.
The grand-stand seemed to be mostly occupied by
folk from behind the footlights. A manager’s
wife wore one of the numerous envy-stirring crea-
tions acquired by her on her recent visit ’Ome ;
with her was a girl with hair draped full over
each temple, and airy embroidered muslin. A
black gown encrusted with silver sequins glittered
like the spangled heavens. The starter’s wife had
a black muslin gathered with tangled white. G-.
Lauri was original in a long frock-coat and in-
congruous tile of soft felt; Fannie Wentworth
was in white with indigo hat; J. C. Williamson
was on the lawn, also other well-known faces.
Sassiety was sparsely represented.
Canvas-town at Stanmore is in full swing.
The Seventh Day Adventists are holding their
annual mild religious razzle after the manner
of the houseless patriarchs. On Saturday
which the sect keeps holy as the Sabbath, there
were special shivoos in the shape of extra meet-
ings in the large assembly tent. The dwellings
are neatly ranged in streets, and the streets are
named. Visitors, of whom there were swarms on
Sunday, gazed curiously into the shops and
houses of the camp, and were invited in by
families with extra-swagger habitations, while
sisters who had no reason to be proud of the
adornment of their temporary homes, let down
the hessian portals against intruding eyes. No-
body found out exactly why the Adventists had
gone into camp or what their tenets are, except
that, by regarding Saturday as a close day for toil,
they wisely insert two holidays into every week.
The wheel-race, at the S.C.G. on Saturday
afternoon, attracted a large crowd. In the throng
on the stand was Mrs. Platt-Betts, wife of the
record-breaker, in an early-Victorian bonnet.
There were many examples of the scoopy head-
gear on the fairly-dressed occupants of the ladies’
stand, some very pretty, but the faces underneath
didn’t all follow suit. The paint on some of the
women’s faces was inches thick. With unskilful
daubers the recipe was to make an obtrusive dab
of rouge on each cheek-bone, and then dust over
with flour.
The present employer of R. D. Fitzgerald (the
Adelaide coachman who has just become heir to a
substantial legacy, and the succession of the
baronetcy of De Ros) is Mr. J. H. Howe, the
Federal delegate ; during the Convention the for-
tunate Jehu was in Sydney with Mrs. and Miss
Howe. The legatee has had a career —the usual
adventures of one of the legion that never was
“listed.” But he is not the only gone-under aris-
tocrat suddenly brought to the surface. The late
Earl of Limerick was getting timber for the Chil-
tern (Vic.) mines when his wife became a countess
and his son Lord Glentworth ; they screwed and
pinched in cheap Jersey till the estates were free.
Spiers and Pond’s former waitress, Mrs. Au-
gustus Perceval, now Countess of Egmont, made
a lucky dip in the matrimonial lottery bag when
she married “Gussy.” Tiresome folk are bound
to use her name to point the moral that barmaids
and waitresses always make sensationally rich
matches. In point of fact, as the new earl was a
common working person when she joined fortunes
with him, it was a very fair deal. But now, by a
surprising turn of the wheel, the ex-sailor, ex-
fireman, ex-hotelkeeper, becomes owner of one of
the “ stately homes of England,” with ever so
much a year, two or three titles to choose from,
and the hereditary right to make laws. Born in
Maoriland over 40 years ago, he is the first Aus-
tralasian contribution to the House of Lords.
The Two Little Vagabonds began their career
on Saturday to a large and sniffing audience.
You want to take your very largest handkerchief
to this drama, which reduces every women to a
moist and flabby state. One girl on Saturday,
after she had damped her own cambric, had to
borrow one from a friend to mop up her tears.
Compared with the “Two Vags.,” onions aren’t
in it as tear-raisers. Between sobs, I noted that
even the gowns were watered.- Mrs. Morris looked
gracefully lank in black moire. Also, Miss E.
Hughes can wear that sombre hue better than any
other color. Wally’s limbs are well-nourished for a
vag’s., and suggest great bicycling possibilities.
Lily Titheradge (Dick) takes off half-a-dozen years
with her skirts and looks a pretty little Arab of
ten. In the audience Dick the first was sitting,
and many pros, were present; a stage-box held a
lately-waned star and her party, while most of
the rest of her co. decorated the stalls front row.
The Society of Artists has been drawing the
public to its show by throwing in music and light
refreshments. A bright evening gathering was
undertaken by Mrs. J. Caro and daughters, when
the tiny stage was utilised for the first time in a
musical scene, “ The Blind Beggars.” On Friday,
in the Opera House, the new and original farce,
“ Nancy’s Nuptials,” by W. Beattie, will be played
for the benefit of the society. In this playlet,
which is in rehearsal under a professional eye,
there are several skits on some of the local painting
and musical dons.
Yon can now have jewellery made while you
wait. Mr. C. Stockle, with a reputation as a
“ lightning gold-wire artist,” gave an exhibition
of his art to some invited guests, at the Empire
Hotel last Wednesday. With a coil of glistening
wire and some small pincers he rapidly curled up
all sorts of fanciful devices—brooches, hair-pins
and bangles. Each lady-guest had her own name
presented to her written in wire inside of three
minutes. The artist’s most elaborate efforts are
plush-backed portraits of the Queen and the
Premier, which latter is a faithful likeness, eye-
flass and all, and every hair in the moustache
ristles accurately.
Bombala possesses a quadrille club which de-
serves record for an original idea carried out at the
wind-up hop of the season. A beauty-com-
petition was held and settled by referendum
among gentlemen, who midway in the festivity
plumped in favor of a belle arrayed in pale
blue and yellow satin peppered with spangles.
To her that hath shall be given ; a brooch marked
the decision in the damsel’s favor. It would have
been more consolatory to have given the trinket
to the lady at the foot of the poll; only she pro-
bably wouldn’t have claimed the badge. And
what a lively time the partners of every girl save
one in the room must have had for the rest of the
evening!
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Disconsolate parties will be numerous on the
night of the forthcoming Brassey costume ball.
Already tales are told of elaborate dresses ordered
by expectant guests who haven’t yet received the
necessary pasteboard. Two friends, when the
shivoo was first announced, arranged to go as the
rival Queens, Mary and Elizabeth, and selected
four damsels each as train-bearers. The robes are
all ready, but, alas, neither of their Majesties has
been invited, and some of the train-bearers are
also left out in the cold.
“ The Brasseys’ fancy-costume ball threatens to
be a garish hen-convention, with only here and
there a man to face the music. The more retiring
sex doesn’t take kindly to picturesque disguises,
especially when it is expected to compete against
professional mummers, who live by making objects
of themselves in public. Also, Cup-week is con-
sidered to be a most unsuitable time for the
pageant. No far-sighted man cares to book him-
self for a gay-and-festive character on the off-
chance that he will succeed in backing the winner.
And, if they were all to go as the Melancholy
Dane, the effect would be depressing.
“ All the Govt. House lackeys at the coming
fancy-ball are to wear old French dress, so as to
provide a correct milieu for Louis Seize Brassey
and his Marie Antoinette. Like most latter-
day entertainments, the vice - regal festival
promises to be purely spectacular. Lancers
are already being arranged with an eye
to scenic effect and members of ‘Versailles,’
‘Stuart,’ ‘Juliet’ and ‘ School for Scandal’ sets
already oross swords with co-dancers re clashing
colors and cuts. Questions such as ‘ Who is to be
Romeo?’ cause trouble too, and everyone pri-
vately accuses her dearest friend of having selected
the one role on earth most utterly unsuited to her
style. To begin with, ‘Mine Host’ himself!
What is the resemblance between Louis the
Martyr and Baron Brassey ! Sounds like a
conundrum which most people would promptly
‘give up,’though a historical pedant might point
out that both of these heroes loved his fad (mak-
ing crazy locks in one case, running his yacht into
tight corners in the other) better than sticking to
his own paid-for trade.
“Soft-hearted spectators, who only sniffed and
felt chocky when the beasts breakfasted off the
newly-engaged couple in the ‘ Sign of the Cross,’
regularly snort and blubber over the wrongs of
poor Josephine. As a consequence of such weep-
ful productions, the atmosphere of the Princess’s
begins to feel damp and depressed, full of histri-
onic ‘ farewells to the dying and mournings for the
dead.’ Altogether, the ‘ Gay Parisienne ’ will be
as welcome to theatre habitues as a sunny day in
bronchitis-season.
“ Maggie Moore, at the Theatre Royal, as street-
singer Jac., is plumpest when hungriest. Later
on, lifted into the lap of luxury, she * fines down ’
fast and furiously, with improving fortunes ; but
just after her adoption into upper circles, before
the thinning process has had time to start, she
goes ruralising in a trailing pink wrapper (of all
garments in the world) and a huge cooking apron,
and in a lonely blackberry thicket might easily be
mistaken by a terrified urchin for the ‘ fat lady ’
escaped from some circus.
“‘The Daughters of the Court’ gave their
annual High Court entertainment last week in the
hall attached to a Melbourne tabernacle, Mrs. Dr.
Bevan figuring as Lady Superior or Chief Dame
of honor. This gilt-edged institution, which was
probably intended to develop into a sort of saintly
Primrose League, has a beautiful motto and a
feeble pulse. The daughters undertook, in the
first instance, to ‘ Do a good deed every day, and
speak ill of none,’ but, judging from Mrs. Be van’s
icy reception of their obeisances, they must have
broken the pledge all round. The idea of bring-
ing a lot of women together in order that they
might cease from passing the usual remarks upon
one another was not a very brilliant inspiration,
anyhow.
“ Coroner Youl, quite unknown to his large
assortment of children and grandchildren, left a
widow to mourn his loss. He married the lady
in Sydney some 15 years ago, without spread-
ing the fact abroad, and when he returned to
Melbourne he didn’t care to spring a second Mrs.
Youl upon his admiring relatives. The lady, for
her part, seems to have been quite happy in the
secret marriage arrangement. She enjoyed a
small villa residence in a quiet neighbourhood,
and the jolly old coroner was a welcome visitor.
When at last he died, with a smile on his face, he
was thinking, perhaps, of the surprise in store for
his wife’s stepchildren. Not an unpleasant sur-
prise when they come to consider the matter
critically. But a staggerer, all the same.
“ The ‘ Cottage Cookery ’ yarns, by Rita, which
popped up periodically in Melb. Herald, are now
issuing in shilling book-form from Geo. Robertson
and Co. It is a meaty little volume, full of dis-
cursive wisdom and solemn reflections on woman’s
inhumanity to her dyspeptic husband; the
fact that the first edition sold out in four days
strikes criticism dumb. ‘ Cottage Cookery ’ seems
to be the kind of volume that Dora Copperfield
might have regarded without dread. Perhaps the
secret of success in Rita’s doctrines is a liberal
infusion of things generally known and too often
overlooked, as, for instance, ‘ No religion teaches
that it is necessary to bring the same joint to have
the same blessing asked over it two or three times.’
A much-married man thinks very much in this
strain when he mutters ‘ grace ’ over the cold
mutton. The sight of his silent sentiment in cold
print on a bookstall persuades him to invest a
‘ bob ’ in one more appeal to Maria’s better nature.
Maria may, perhaps, be influenced by the card-
board exterior of this shilling shocker, which is a
bright cabbage-green. Every bad cook adores
cabbage.
“ The evils of golfing have not been fully recog-
nised yet, but are coming gradually to declare
themselves. Quite lately a Melb. lady golf en-
thusiast noticed that her right hip had become
much larger than her left, so much so that when
she wore a tightly-fitting “tailor’’gown the de-
formity was clearly noticeable to all. Several
over-golfers thereupon discovered that the same
calamity was descending upon them, the swing of
the club while playing producing a marked en-
largement of the right hip-joint. Many fashion-
able dressmakers have consequently received orders
to insert a layer of padding over the left hip of
their golfing customers’ race-gowns to equalise
the size of each side of the figure. Golf seems to
be the only hidden power except the ocean—
which can make a woman careless as to her per-
sonal appearance. A group of female golfers,
after a wet afternoon’s play, whereat they have
been wet to the skin six or seven times, is a
deplorable sight. Such a group was on Caulfield
railway-station lately. The colors in their hat-
bands had all run—and made dreary little rivulets
over their straightened ‘fringes.’ Their wet,
flappy skirts clung round their muddy boots, and
their talk was all of ‘ holes ’ and unskilful ‘ mark-
ing.’ and other mysteries which they discussed
with much enthusiasm. They were all damp but
dauntless.
“ The sleekest and most ‘ popular ’ shepherd of
a very big Australian city is a snob of the very
first water. Visiting at the residence of a pluto-
cratic disciple to pay his oily respects to the dear
ladies, or meeting them in the public street, the
good man ignores the presence of any inferior
female who, in the character of governess, hired
companion, or nurse, stands around. Such people
have no existence as far as this representative
soul-saver is concerned. They neither rent pews,
nor drop half-sovereigns in the plate. Their con-
dition is menial and they interest him not.
“Following in the footsteps of the clever little
German-Yankee who makes thousands a year by
painting fashionable London beauties—and other-
wise—in miniature, a Melb. girl, Miss Norris, has
successfully studied the art and set up a studio in
the city. Though but a few months have elapsed
since she made her start, and as yet she and the
new ‘ ivory craze ’ are little known here, orders
begin to come in to her very freely, and she cer-
tainly produces exquisite little pictures. She has
lately a very dainty pair of portraits of
Miss Elliot Page and ‘ Mommer ’—the latter has
® e ® n beautifully set in an entourage of turquoise
and brilliants, by the way. Mrs. Potter and
Ivyrle Bellow, as Romeo and Juliet, make a dainty
brace of pictures, which the originals admired
very much, and generously offered to accept—as a
gift. Julius Superbus in toga and peplum is
another successful miniature, the likeness in this,
as in the others, being very cleverly brought out.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.
Our Bleeding Country!
Sydney Sunday Times makes the following
calculation of the cost of the tremendous arbi-
tration case, M'Sharry v. N.S.W. Railway
Commissioners :
Mr. Barton (arbitrator), £27 10s. a day, five days
per week for 14 months ... £8 250
Mr. Bruce Smith (for Govt.), 20gs. per day and
sge. for a junior ... ... ... ‘ ... 8,250
Mr. O’Connor (for M'Sharry) 25gs. a day ... 8,260
Mr. Delohery, junior to Mr.' O’Connor, at 7gs. a
day ••• ... ... ... ... 2,100
Conferences (at least two a week), at 5 guineas—
Mr. Smith ... ... ... .. g2O
Mr. O’Connor ... ... j" 620
Ditto for juniors ... ... ... ... 500
Total £28,590
Then a low estimate for attorneys, &c., is £5OO, and
£2OOO for shorthand-writing- and typewriting, &c., making
a grand total of £35,590. If it cost that for 14 months, on
the same ratio three months longer will add £7555 to the
total, or £43,145. Special trains and other incidentals
are not included in these calculations, so that altogether
±50,000, or one-third of the amount at issue, will pro-
bably be involved in expenses.
The fees M‘Sharry pays are his own business ;
but those the Govt, pays are grossly extrava-
gant considering time and ability involved and
work done. A District Court Judge—sav
Judge Murray—at £l5OO a year could surely
have managed the whole business admir-
ably. The same paper calculates that the
disgraceful N.S.W. Public Works Committee
(Messrs. T. T. Ewing, chairman; H. Clarke, C.
A. Lee, J. L. Fegan, T. H. Hassall, G. Black,
F. A. Wright and F. Farnell, representing the
Assembly, and Messrs. F.&T. Humpliery, J.
Hoskins, C. J. Roberts, W. J. Trickett and D.
O’Connor, representing the Council) costs the
country not less than £7OOO a year.
There are two “ sectional committees ”
travelling the country at £3 3s. per day
and expenses (besides Parliamentary salary),
and the remainder usually sit four days
a week in Sydney at £3 3s. per sitting. Who
would be “unemployed” in the face of these
figures ? And just let any level-headed man of
affairs who knows “ the run ” of Sydney cast
his eye down the list of the Public Works Com-
mittee personnel and say what would be the
value of such men’s time, and how far their
abilities would be relied on in any commercial
capacity !

Cancer mortality has increased in England
during the last 50 years from 1 in 129 of the total
mortality to lin 28. In Paris cancer is estimated
to cause 1 in every 20 deaths.
In J. Morrissey, the new member for
Rodney, Vic. Parliament has secured a person-
ality. Morrissey is an orator locally reputed to
be able to talk all the venom out of a mountain-
cat and argue a bull-pup off a bone. His head is
the most striking head in the new Parliament —a
very round skull set upon a tall, battered pillar of
a neck, and a face like that of a Roman emperor
on an old, time-worn, bronze coin. He promises
to be a joy for ever to the caricaturists.
The'unfortunate Lucknow strike is practi-
cally ended, and the victory is with the manage-
ment. Every person of reasonable foresight
reckoned that it was almost bound to end that
way, because the management almost always wins.
There were a good many reasons for the result.
The first one was the steady influx of the multi-
tudinous “scab,” from Bendigo and elsewhere;
labor is never well-enough organised to prevent
a steady influx from somewhere in such cases, and
this was one of the dangers that loomed ahead
from the first. The long and mysterious delay of
the Government in issuing the Wentworth lease
left the big co. wholly exempt from labor condi-
tions for the greater part of the time the strike
lasted, which was also an important advantage.
The back-down of Minister Smith, who promised to
oppose the demand for exemption, but didn’t do
it, helped the co. a good deal, and the partial ex-
emption which it secured—possibly thanks to the
caving-in of Minister Smith—practically settled
the matter. The pathetic elements of the tail-end
of the conflict are the efforts of the union leaders
to keep up hope when there is no hope left, and
the undisguised joy of the Sydney daily press at
the collapse. In this case, the allegedly demo-
cratic Daily Telegraph and the Tory Star are
on the same side. The D-T.’s democratic senti-
ments are good theory, but they never stand the
strain of actual practice. Possibly, however, for
a concern bossed by a magnate of the old sweating
Sydney Bus Co., it is as democratic as could be
expected.

Personal Items.
Curious coincidences. The first white
child born in Botany Bay was named Rope. One
of his grandchildren was afterwards hanged.
Frank Smith, late M.P. for Balmain
(N .S.W.), who “ went down ” in the building-
society smash, is editing a paper in the Rhodes
interest in South Africa.
Belmore, of 30 years ago, is the only
N.S.W. Governor who left a bequest to Sydney
University. He bequeathed =£3oo towards a
department of agricultural chemistry.
The danger of the hardened business man
getting out of harness. John Bridge, after devot-
ing a life-time to building up the big wool
emporium known by his name, takes his first
holiday—and his last.
Interpreter (to Hebrew punster-politician
Zox): “ Are you in favor of a tax on pork, old
Jew-boy?” Zox: “No, dear friend; but lam
strongly in favor of a heavy tax on hogs who grunt
at public meetings.’’
Graham Berry received the news of his
defeat at Brunswick as though it crushed him.
He reeled when told the numbers, and muttered
“ After 40 years ! ” as he left the hall and entered
his waggonette to be driven home with one of his
family, who shed copious tears.
Ex-Premier John Colton, who introduced
Kingston into S.A. Ministerial life, and who was
rewarded with a knighthood five years after when
his protege became Premier, is in a hopeless state
of feebleness. He was 74 years old in Sept.

(N •S.W.) criminal case, a witness for the accused
used the phrase, “ The news reached me,” pro-
nouncing news “nooze.” “Come, come, my
friend,” said the gentle Judge, “ you anticipate.”
In view of all the little stories told about
him, Gov. Lamington’s refusal to “ whustle on
Sunday ” to the Sydney reporters causes no end
of amusement in Brisbane. But both the stories
and the refusal are thoroughly Scotch (vide Bobby
Burns).
Ed. Bulletin, —Duncan Gillies is not a
Northumbrian, and, I believe, never saw Northum-
berland. He is a Glasgow man, and was educated
at the High School, in that smokeful city. His
father was a decent, dour market-gardener, a few
miles out of the city.—Glesca.
Arthur Streeton, now settled in Chelsea,
the stronghold of studioism, does not seem to care
for London and the Londoners, and probably
regrets the deflection of his original intention to
luxuriate in the lotus-eating life of the Greek
Isles—which would recall Mosman’s Bay every
fine day.

asked Aspinall : “Is that so ? ” Aspinall: “ No,
not half enough, but it would have been all right
if you called one Patmore. ”

It is a little - known incident of Ben.
Tillett’s career that he was once subscribed ,£450
—for the purpose of a health trip. The cash was
paid over, but Tillett’s trip seemed to hang fire,
and at last it seemed so “strong” that the com-
mittee began to inquire whether he had “ blewed
it ”in riotous living in the metropolis. The result
was to show that the £450 had been paid by Tillett
into the funds of the Dockers’ Union.
Bear Bulletin, —Will you let me tell that
Dr. Evans story, I think more effectively, “as it
was told to me.” To illustrate his particular
point by a historical parallel, Dr. Evans had
begun a sentence, “ I remember, Mr. Speaker, in
the days of Queen Anne,” whereupon a member
interjected : “ Aha ! Doctor ! we have learned
your age at last! ” “ Mr. Speaker,” continued the
Doctor, with withering emphasis, “ a scholar, sir,
is the contemporary of every age.”— Historicus.
John Harper, the newly-appointed traffic
manager of N.S.W. railways, was a country jour-
nalist some 30 years ago. He worked with th»
versatile Garnet Walch on the Cumberland
Times of that day, when Parramatta cherished the
noble ambition of leading literary Australia.
Harper now draws £9OO a year; but it is ques-
tionable whether £9OO a year with the knowledge
that a man is a good traffic manager brings as
much joy as a third of that income with the fancy
that he can write poetry.
Many of Premier Seddon’s friends say
that he will take the Agency-General. None
know better than the herculean Richard that Wel-
lington political life killed Atkinson and Ballance,
as it must kill every man who fairly breasts the
collar in the short awful three months’ session in
which Maoriland does its legislating for the year.
Why, look at the press-gallery death-roll of the
past few years—Searl, Vesey Hamilton, Crago,
Dix, and Bond —to say nothing of the many good
men it knocked out.
Ex-Chief-Justice Lilley was once trying
a libel-case in Brisbane in which “Bobby ” Byrne,
of Figaro, was defendant. Lilley’s son was coun-
sel for “ Bobby,” and, noting the coincidence, a
witness for plaintiff passed the following lines
around :
Lilley was the father,
Lilley was the son,
Lilley would much rather
That “ Bobby ” Byrne won.
But Bobby lost.
Adelaide society is in a flutter over the
unexpected luck of a local coachman employed by
Conventionist Howe, M.L.C. The lucky young
man—R. G. D. Fitzgerald by name—received a
cable advising him that he is now heir to the
Barony of De Ros, with Irish estates worth £l5OO
a year. Local society has hitherto only viewed
this young man in the light of a common coach
man with a straw in his mouth during his “ off ”-
hours, but the Adelaide papers now describe him
as a fair-haired, blue-eyed London Journal
vision, and heaps of maidens now nightly dream
of being the Baroness De Ros.
Banker Henry G. Turner, having com
pounded some unusually bad poetry, assumes a
superior position in literature in Melbourne, and
no affair of letters is complete without his little
say so. At the recent meeting re the Stevenson
memorial, this cash-book critic compared the
South Sea work of Louis Becke with that of
Stevenson. Stevenson “ could not be cavilled at,
while Becke described mere carnivals of drink,
and gore, and dissolute life.” Becke describes
South Sea life as he knows it, and as Stevenson
did not know it. Stevenson was a most fascinating
writer, but, so far as the South Seas are concerned,
he was a cockney who, as it were, put his savages
into kilts. Anyhow, literary criticism from the
platform of the Wesleyan Sunday-school superin-
tendent is not worth a cent. Becke is an artist,
and it is not his fault that the missionaries have
not left the islands in such a condition of blessed-
ness and peace that chronicles of South Sea life
might be eagerly sought after by Sabbath-school
librarians (old pattern).

RISING IN THE WORLD.
“ Women are gradually rising in the world, and
will soon displace those that are now at the top of
the tree,” says an axefaced lecturess of the W.O.T.U.
Had she seen some of the village-settlement women
of Victoria out on a moonlight night, she would be
quite sure of it.”
The first intimation received by Chief
Justice Way that he had been called to the Privy
Council was in the shape of a cipher telegram.
Thereby hangs a tale. Dailey, Q.C., long ago,
owned a certain walking-stick, soon after becom-
ing possessed of which he became Privy Coun-
cillor ; later, he gave it to Alfred Stephen, who
subsequently acquired a similar distinction. When
Stephen died, he left the stick by will to Way,
who had received no previous intimation of his
intention. The earliest cable notifying Way’s
appointment came to Adelaide while he was
absent, as a guest of Admiral Bridge, in Hobart.
When the message reached Hobart, Way was in
bed and asleep, and the Admiral took it to his
room and gave it to him, waking him up for that
purpose. Way read it, blinked, scratched his
head, read it again, and then exclaimed, “I’m a
Privy Councillor ; look ! ” The Admiral perused
the telegram, but was bewildered until he had the
virtues of the wonderful stick explained to him.
William Davis, one of the ’9B rebels
whom a contingent of Hibernians propose to
honor in a few months, set up a cottage and
smithy on his arrival in Botany Bay, at the corner
of what is now Grosvenor and Harrington-streets.
He made pikes in the ould sod, but devoted him-
self to the manufacture of more peace-loving imple-
ments in his home. A French priest, having put
in from a surveying vessel, said Mass in Davis’s
cottage. This is supposed to be the first Mass
celebrated in Australia, no priest who was a
British subject being allowed at the time to so
officiate. On the spot on which that cottage stood
St. Patrick’s church now stands, and it is believed
the altar is in the same relative position as when
improvised nearly a century ago. Further, they
are priests of a French order who now own the
church and the land. William Davis lived to be
78, and was buried in the old cemetery in Devon-
shire-street. Davis was never tried before a court
in Ireland. He was simply arrested, carried on
board a convict-ship, and dumped down in Sydney
Cove. To do the old man justice, however, he
never said he didn’t make pikes for the “ bhoys.”

to The Bulletin :
The Right Honorable Sir George Turner has lost much
of the sweet simplicity of speech and manner that be-
longed to plain George Turner, of the wide-awake and
goatee times. He doesn’t know' it, hut he has brought
back with him a distinctly Hinglish flavor, which hangs
about him most unpleasantly. He spreads himself:
speaks patronisingly of his colleagues ; commends their
loyalty, and altogether bears himself with opera-bouffe
regality. Speaking of the Postmaster-General’s mission
to the Postal Conference in the States, the Right Honor-
able told his audience, at the St. Kilda meeting, that “ he
had sent Mr, Ouffv,” &c., &c. Speaking of Mr. Best’s
inspection, as Minister of Lands, of the Mallee country,
he said “ I sent the Minister,” &c. His mates are getting
fidgetty under this patronising attitude of their Right
Hon. boss. Duffy’s Celtic blood (“ and spinal marrow'”)
rebels against this cool assumption of the “one and
indivisible” attitude —by the Right Hon. George. If
there are rows and splits in theXlahinet shortly (Duffy
don’t matter, of course), they will arise, not so much from
diversity of opinion in politics, as resentment against the
“ rank ’’-smelling meanness and methods of their newly-
anointed boss. It is always the w r ay. Give a nigger a
shirt-collar or a plug-hat, and you ruin him. Heap titular
honors on an honest democrat, and, at best, you convert
him into an asinine compromise—neither fit for company
upstairs nor in the kitchen below.

The late John Sheehan, of Maoriland, had
a pretty wit. Meeting “ Hughie ” Mcllhone,
once a well-known political wire-puller in Wel-
lington, he enquired the reason of the latter’s
presence at the capital. “Oh, I’m here just to
see how the world wags,” replied Mcllhone, and,
as he spoke, a palpable earthquake shock oc-
curred. “ There she goes then,” said Sheehan.
Crown Prosecutor Wade is not the only
“ possible ” for the N.S.W. District Court judge-
ship to be vacated by Judge .Forbes. The friends

“ Dipso ” on an Adelaide veteran :
“Passed in his checks,” William Holden, the oldest
newspaper-hack in Australia, aged 89 years 6 months.
The writer knew Mr. Holden 18 years ago, when he
(Mr. H.) was a sprightly youth of 71. Swedenborgian
was he, and as decent a superstitionist as ever falls to
one’s acquaintance. Used to be a raging smoker; but,
suddenly, about 25 years ago, gave up the pipe. That
was on a “ Fourth of July ” day. So he celebrated the
Fourth annually afterwards bv laying in, on the eve of
its coming, a stock of clay pipes and a roll of tobacco.
WE LIVE IN HOPES.
“As a result of inquiries he is making, the
N.S.W. Minister for Agriculture may determine to
establish a Women’s Agricultural College.”
At sparrow-chirp on waking-, he lighted up, and smoked
and smoked and smoked and smoked, pipe after pipe, all
the day, bar meal-times (for which leisure he had that
day small appetite !) until he smoked himself to sleep at
night. Then, no more tobacco for a year. His room at the
S.A. Register (Adelaide) office on Fourth of July was a
levee chamber Holden was a Parliamentary reporter in
the old days of Adelaide, before the Hansa'iid time, and
when the gallery-men used to knock off work just when-
ever they pleased. “ Mr. Speaker: I see the press re-
porters have gone away, and, therefore, I move that the
House do now adjourn,” was a common occurrence
then. Holden was pensioned off (in a manner of
speaking) in a sinecure as editor of “ The Riddler ” page
of the Observer (weekly of Register). He had a childish
delight in puzzles and small wit, and also a curious habit
of proving geometrical problems, by outting paper into
shapes according to scale, and thus demonstrating that
such and such an irregular polygon was equal to such
and such regular figures. When the writer fare-
welled him in ’B2, he had strong notions of becoming a
vegetarian, if he really wasn’t one. A good, clean,
wholesome old fellow, with a head that required a 7t hat
(concerning which his stock wheeze was—“Mine’s a head,
not a pimple ! ”), and yet had only a vast accumulation of
mediocrity in it. Old Holden ” he was to all who knew
him.

Aboriginalities.
The Maori shearers on the Chatham
Islands lately struck for higher pay. Here is their
little song:—
This is the notice to all the sheep stations on the Chat-
ham Islands, Maori and Europeans. We have highed the
price of shearing to £1 a hundred and tucker. It got to
be good, and also to the pressmen Is. lOd. a bale. The
fleece picker 6s. per day, half-day, full-day, or overtime.
The cooks 7s. per day, shepherds 7s. per day. The men
who is shearing for 16s. Bd. per hundred. If the master
of tile place hopes to get the £1 a hundred men he has to
sacks the man who is shearing for 16s. Bd., and then the
£1 a hundred men will shear. If the master of the shed
find fault with one of the shears, and the shears also find
fault with that man the boss has rights to sacks him and
he has to be paid at once. If the shears do not find fault
with that man he will stay and shear. If the boss do not
agree for him to shear the whole gang will leave and the
boss must pay them for the sheeps they have shorn. If
one of these men who has signed this notice has rights to
make this man pay £5. If he does not pay the ±5 the
rest of the party has a right to take his goods or stock till
they get their £5 worth. This law is for this year and the
year coming.
If the stockowners yield there will be another rise
next year ; also the employment of “white-legs”
will be shortly prohibited.
Omega : ‘ * One of the tricks shearers resort-
to to get away to another shed without being
blackballed in the one they leave : In a shed near
Moree lately, a shearer showed the boss a telegram
telling him his mother was dead. ‘ Don’t believe
it,’said the boss. ‘Oh, yes,’put in the shearer’s
little brother ; ‘ she is. She’s been dead two
years, sir.’ The boss discharged that shearer,
and, finding out where he was going, wrote ahead
and had him blackballed.”

E.S.S. : “On D Downs (Q.) during
the late shearing, black gins were employed
_“picking-up.” In that delightful district a man
is nobody if he hasn’t a gin all to himself. “ White
Marys ” are scarce, and the black article is the
substitute. She is won and lost at cards, and her
gallant cavalier will fight stubbornly for the honor
of his lubra. I heard a drover at Nacundra, Wil-
son River, praising a knockabout of a few days’
acquaintance to a local resident. “He’s a hard-
working cove, anyway, an’ a real decent feller.”
“ That pudden-’ead ! ” said the local man, con-
temptuously; “why, he’s got no gin 1 ” That
settled it.

Some bush almanacs.—Old shepherds and
boundary-riders in the far bush use strange de-
vices to keep tally of the days of the week. I saw
one with two jam-tins and seven white pebbles,
and the first thing he would do on upending him-
self in the morning was to transfer a pebble from
one tin to the other. When one tin contained
seven pebbles it was, in his own phraseology, the
“day of the big tuck-out, and plenty shut-eye.”

was shifted one

reached. Most pri-

of all was a board
about the size of an
ordinary plate, hung
over a shepherd’s
bunk, and each
morning, on rising,
the owner scratched
a mark on it with a
piece of charcoal,
and washed it clean
on Sunday evening.
—E.S.S.

* *
“ I had been
under the impres-
sion,” writes a bush-
man to The Bul-
letin, “ that every tribe in Australia wore
some kind of loin - covering till I went to
the Maranoa and spent a fortnight with a
tribe above Merrivale station. All were stark
naked, men, women and children ; and the
gins and plump comely maidens would squat
before me, or join me in any kind of game, with-
out the slightest sense of shame. They were
splendidly-made women, healthy and cheerful;
and the men were fine, muscular fellows. Butthey
were victims of opium, and when they become
opium-sick nothing is held sacred by them that
will buy a smoke. One old fellow offered me the
‘ missus ’ and two daughters for a shilling. When
hunting, the men wear belts round their naked
waists through which the spare boomerangs are
thrust, and pocket-knives are carried by closing
the blade on a tuft of hair on their head.”
* • » *
They were four old whalers—looked like
Druids, who hadn’t had their usual wash. Their
combined ages must have totalled 280, and they
sat in a row, on a form, like mutes. A stranger
was standing at the bar, over his glass, when one
old Druid arose, touched him on the coat, and
said, ‘ ‘ Any chance of a job on the station this
year, boss ? ” Stranger smiled, and paid for a
pint. The old fellow sat down, waited till the
stranger’s back was turned, then poured a share
down each old mate’s throat. Stranger saw him
in the mirror, and said, “ Why don’t you let them
bum for themselves ? ” “ Lord bless you, sir, they
wouldn’t cadge off you, sir—they were gents once
—I never was. They’ll drink your health, sir ”
and they did.
* * * *
‘‘ Had a pretty close call, the other day.
Riding from Lismore to Ballina, was overtaken by
a fearful storm. It became pitch-dark, save for
the lightning, which was remembersome. Horse,
scared and unmanageable, got off the road,
amongst the trees, and an awful flash struck a big
gum-tree just in front of him, splitting the tree to
the roots. As the halves swayed apart the terri-
fied beast plunged straight through between them,
and, just as he landed on the other side, the
halves came together with a snap, gripping three-
fourths of his tail. I had to get off and cut it
to release him. After that, I thought it good
enough to turn back.”—E. J.
* * * *
S. : “A run of luck. In ’B5 two men were
working an old mine on the new rush in Cassilis
district, Gippsland—Peters and Samson. Peters
was a one-armed man. One morning he let his
mate down the shaft, the Blucher, 60ft., and the
latter was overcome by the foul air. Peters could
offer no help, so mounted their pack-horse and
rode helter-skelter for assistance. About a mile-
and-a-half down, the horse ran him into a tree,
broke his leg, and fractured the remaining arm.
Bennet, a splitter, found him lying helpless,
heard his story, and set off in turn to fetch men
from a small rush. Bennet was drowned attempt-
ing to cross the Wentworth River. Peters was
found next day by an Omeo man hunting cattle,
but he only lived long enough to tell his tale again.
Samson was found dead in the shaft. Bennet’s
body was recovered a few days later. A run of
luck ! ”
* * * *
David Carnegie, just returned from an ex-
ploring expedition in the dead, dry, and dreary
N.W. of Westralia, thus describes his way of
getting water: —“We carried out the one plan,
always, and it was this : For hunting purposes,
the natives burn large patches of spinifex ; the
smoke from these fires can be seen for some dis-
tance, and frequently I have counted as many as
five in a day. Choosing a ‘ smoke ’ —if possible, on
A MINING ITEM.
FRIEND (to new-arrival) : “ Would you like to do some luck-shooting? ”
THE SPORTSMAN : “ But where are the bucks? ”
FRIEND: “ Oh, you'll always had them round about silent batteries.” —(Then
Johnnie-come-lately proceeded to shoot Mrs. Murphy's goat.)
our general course —we would steer for it, and
when it died down, as it would in the course of a
few hours, we continued in the same direction
until the entire distance was traversed and we
reached the burnt ground. We would then spread
out, pick up tracks, and, if possible, catch a native.
This we were generally fortunate enough to
manage, though only at the expense of great
patience and much labor. Often as many as four
days would elapse between the time of our sight-
ing the smoke and catching a black. We found
them (the blacks) quite friendly—chiefly owing to
their small numbers, there never being more than
eight in a camp, all told—though inclined to be
‘ cheeky ’ when they had only one of us to deal
with. Some would lead us to water at once, while
others would take a day or two to mate up their
minds.” Good phrase !

white man—baby preferred. His drink was boil-
ing blood out of a skull-cavity, and be was known
to be Bft. high and powerful as an ourang-outang.
Volunteers rolled up to capture the demon, dead
or alive. They bound themselves with awful
oaths not to return until he was laid low or made
prisoner. Affecting were their farewells to their
loved and dear ones, and the local priests and
parsons blessed them, and all the tearful maidens
and slobbering matrons in the township kissed
them good-bye, and saw the cavalcade over the
first ridge. Strategy was their plan of campaign.
They planted a blanket and rations in the scrub,
and ambushed like a half-moon. The ruse suc-
ceeded. The demon came, and 14 stalwart white
men fell on him and bound him. He cried. He
begged for mercy, and asked to be allowed to read
a chapter of the Bible before being murdered. He
was a poor, miserable Bst. 21b. “boy,” sft. lin.
high, a Christianised Kanaka, who had four acres
under maize and sweet potatoes in the heart of
the scrub. Jubilee medals were distributed among
his bold conquerors.

The New Teacher: “You are late, my
boy ; what is the reason ? ” Boy : ‘ ‘ Please, sir,
I’ve been after the cows, and couldn’t come
before.” Teacher : “ I don’t think I can take that
as an excuse. What’s your name ? ” Boy :
“Bull, sir.” Teacher: “H—m, I’ll let you off
this time, but try and not be late in future.”
* * * *
Four Australians on the boat at Dieppe,
returning from a visit to Paris : “ Well, ’ow did ye
like it, George?” was asked of one of them.
“ What did you think of the Louvre, Notre Dame,
the Eiffel Tower, and all the rest of it ? ” “ Oh,
pooty fair,’’said George, dubiously ; then, bright-
ening up—“ I tell ye the best thing I seen in the
’ole show was yistiddy; when that ’orse run away
’n went slap into the corner of a ’ouse ’n broke ’is
neck.” “My word, yes,” chorused the other
three, “that was good.” Travel improves the
mind.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —This is a free eountry,
is it? Well, the other day I happened to be at a
well-known station (in the Hall and Gilbert dis-
trict) when roll was called for shearing. Among
those present was a storekeeper, canvassing for an
order for groceries, but not a man was game to
speak for himself or his mates, although the
difference between station and store prices stood
thus :
Station. Storh.
Black sugar, lb. 2)4 (yellow.)
Tea, 2/6 I/-
Matches, 1/- per doz. 4d.
Pickles, 1/6 per bottle 9d.
Jam, lOd. 4d.
And so on right through the list. This with a
railway-station 20 chains from the shed ! I re-
marked : “If he won’t let the storekeeper supply
you, ask him to lower his price to the storekeeper’s
level.” “We daren’t do it,” was the solemn
reply; “he would sack everyone of us, or make
things so hot that we’d be glad to quit. He’s a
perfect dog to work under —speaks to us like dogs.”
—Bak.
Capt. M‘Kay, Moreton Bay pilot, who
lived for many years in the South Sea Islands,
lately got a letter from Sooah, a Malayta salt-
water chief, commencing—“ Dear captain, I write
these few lines ” and continuing in genuine
schoolboy fashion. Just fancy a picturesque old
cannibal descending to this sort of thing !

Dear Bulletin, — Cheek ! I was making
a drain for a cocky in New England. Last week
while sitting outside my camp at dinner a magpie
dropped on my left shoulder and snatched a piece
of damper out of my hand. — Tim Bobbin.
* • * •
“Scotty the Wrinkler ” on the Papuan
fever question :
If the “ Voice from Papua” is of opinion that acclima-
tised Queenslanders would stand New Guinea better than
fully fed and blooded men from the South, so am not I.
In the Indian Mutiny, the Jacks, Micks, Taffys, and
Sandys could always knock out the native and Eurasian
troops in the hot weather, with perhaps the exception of
the Ghoorkas and Cabulees (hill-men). The Kanakas go
down before the whites (but they are cheaper by the
bundle or gross). I contend that the deaths on the
Palmer were principally among the Cloncurry and Ethe-
ridge pioneers, and the N. Queen-landers generally, be-
cause they were under-fed, over-tea’d, super soda-and-
acided and salt-horsed. Well do I remember a ghost of a
Bananalander at tne Conglomerate rush saying, “ I wonder
how you Southerners would have stood the hardships of
the Cloncurry ? ” This set up my back, because the man
was like two yards of pump-water. “Why, you remnant
of a physiogram,” said I, “ who looks best men, you and
your mates or the skipper and I ? I don’t believe you'll
live another day.” Next morning his tent was closed,
next evening four posts and single rail ; £5 for the digger
out of the 16oz. of gold he had in a shammy. Verdict :
Death from Palmer ; but my rider was, “If the packers
bring apples, plums, currants, preserved potatoes, any-
thing, over and above flour, tea and sugar, buy them and
eat them, even if you spend your last div. in doing so.”
Twenty years after I am still alive, and my battling weight
is list. 71b.

Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will he taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, whether sent by post or handed in,
and in no case will MS. be returned unless stamps
(of any province) accompany.

The sewerage branch of N.S. Wales Public
Works Department is considering a proposal to
provide 30 pumping-stations, with motive-power
electrically conveyed from a central generator.
In this issue appears the portrait of the
well-known Sydney pianiste and composer, Miss
Maud FitzStubbs, whose “ Heather,” “Orlando,
and “Bridal” waltzes have attained a celebrity
not confined to Australasia. During Miss Fitz-
Stubbs’s short visit to London, also, she was
received with a favor which promised for her a
Miss MAUD FITZ-STUBBS.
Photo., Elliott & Fry, Baker-st., Lond.
complete success should she finally decide upon
taking up her residence in the old world. Another
waltz from the lady’s accomplished pen is shortly
to be published by Messrs. Paling & Co., and from
Ist November onwards Miss FitzStubbs will be
nightly heard, in conjunction with other first-class
artists, in a series of 15 popular concerts at Syd-
ney Exhibition Building.
The Singleton (N.S.W.) people have been
quite upset by the vagaries of a cow which has
been milked continuously for seven years, during
which time she has had but one calf; she is now
giving 16 quarts of milk per day. It has been pro-
posed to exhibit her at 6d. per head, the money to
be devoted to the funds of the surrounding
churches.
Sydney Public Librarian Anderson is back
from his jubilee holiday (on full pay, and £2OO ex-
penses) with the astonishing information that the
cataloguing of his libi’ary is comparatively well
up to date. Also, Mr. Anderson is graciously
pleased to approve of the British Museum. “ The
British Museum commanded Mr. Anderson’s ad-
miration on account of its fine body of experts and
its valuable work.”
Hotel cooks’ wages in West Queensland —
whites, 15s. a week; Chinamen, £2 a week !
Alleged that the only thing which will
effectually repel the mosquito is the Norwegian
birch-bark oil, “ which is used for scenting Russia
leather.” So the Muscovite aroma is “made in
Norway ? ” Apropos, oil of lavender is also most
effectual; but when, in Australia, you ask at the
chemist’s for it you get 75 per cent, oil-of-turpen-
tine almost every time. Wanted badly—an
efficient adulteration law.
On the “Banjo’s” authority, travelling
stock do sometimes get a spread on them which
the lawmakers did not contemplate—especially
where the grass is good or where water lies a mile
or two from the route. But, for a while, the
drover-man in the N.S.W. West will have to be
careful, and all through a Water Rights Act, now
just being put into operation. Tanks and dams
handy to stock-routes are being “ licensed ” to the
run-holder —and the traveller who thereafter takes
of that water runs the risk of “ falling in ” badly.
The Australian Disease.
Our Australian climate soon upsets the liver.
The uniform cry on every hand, young and old, is:
“I am bilious to-day.” The softening influences
of a warm climate tax the liver severely, and few
escape occasional s'ymtoms of exhaustion, even
upon a slight effort, going up a hill, a stairway, or
even an incline. Indigestion, heaviness, and full-
ness of the stomach, especially after eating; un-
usual langour during the day, singing in the ears,
blurred eyesight, and loss of appetite, or, still
worse, a fear that kinds of foods others enjoy will
distress. Loss of energy and vitality and desire
to enjoy life’s pleasures, or wish to combat its
trials, result from a torpid liver. Warner’s Safe
Cure never fails in these eases. It. B. Treacy a
well-known and highly esteemed Sydney business
man (52 Pitt-street), writes : “For some consider-
able time I suffered from both liver and kidney dis-
ease. A few months back I began using Warner’s
Safe Cure, and I am pleased to say that I have felt
much better ever since. In fact, I can affirm with-
out hesitation that my health is now better than
it has been for at least twelve months previously.”
Not a miracle —just another cure.

The Colonial Bank of Australasia Limited.
THE NINTH REPORT OP THE DIRECTORS
—OF —
THE COLONIAL BANK of AUSTRALASIA LIMITED.
fo be presented to the Shareholders at the NINTH ORDINARY GENERAL MEETING, to be held at the Bank, 126
Elizabeth-street, at noon on THURSDAY, 28th OCTOBER, 1897.
REPORT.
The Directors beg to submit to the Shareholders their Ninth Report, with a Balance-sheet and Statement of
Profit and Loss for the Half-year ended 30th September, duly audited.
After providing for expenses of management, interest accrued on deposits, rebate on bills current, and tax on
note circulation, the net profit amounted to £3312 7s. Id., as shown in the balance-sheet.
The difficulties referred to in the last report with regard to the adjustment of the amount paid up on the ordinary
shares preparatory to the application to the Court for the reduction of the bank’s capital have not yet been fully over-
come, but the application will be made when the directors are in a position to do so.
The active business of the bank continues to increase, and, as the general outlook in the country districts is satis-
factory, further improvement may be anticipated.
During the half-year Rir Rupert T. H. Clarke, Bart., has been elected to fill the vacancy on the board caused by
the death of his father, the late Sir \V. J. Clarke, Bart.
The Ninth Ordinary General Meeting of shareholders will be held at the head office of the company, 126 Elizabeth-
street, Melbourne, on Thursday, the 28th day of October, 1897, at noon.
By order of the board.
Melbourne, 19th October, 1897. SELBY PAXTON, General. Manager.

POLITICAL
POINTS
N.S.W. delegate J. T. Walker denies the
report of his intended retirement from the Federal
Convention.
Old Robert Stout, the leopard of Maori-
land politics, is now described by the local Tory
news-sheets as a teaching member of a “rising
young democratic party.” !!!!
West Melbourne contempt for newspaper
advice was expressed in a large majority vote for
Dr. Maloney, the one candidate whom both the
Age and Argus had delighted to dishonor.
Brisbane Courier is loud of late in its
admiration of Maoriland legislation. Also, the
Courier talks much of the need of a Liberal-
party in Bananaland politics, and so forth.
Portent ?
Marie Corelli : —“ .... the magnifi-
cent institution of Freetrade, which has resulted
in a vulgar competition of all countries and all
classes to see which can most quickly jostle the
other out of existence.”
Melb. : “ Although the law prescribes no
cabs at elections, it is simply evaded, and they
flew all over tli city in legions this general elec-
tion day. It is illegal to engage them, but they
give their services for nothing, and get a douceur
of £l. A candidate could repudiate the lot, if he
liked.”
Of the “tickets” for the Vic. elections
the most disastrous was that containing the
names of those “selected to receive the support
of the Vic. R.C. Defence League.” Of all its
“min” only 19 were elected, and of these the
only new men are r| 'outcher, Moloney, Stapleton,
and Morrissey
Bryan O’Loghlen, who goes back to Vic.
Assembly, was instrumental years ago in having
defined a knotty point in Imperial Parliamentary
law. Members of the Commons must vacate
their seats if they accept office under the Crown.
O’Loghlen had been elected for Clare (Ireland),
but being appointed to the Vic. Attorney-General-
ship the Imperial Committee of Elections decided
that that was tantamount to accepting an Imperial
office, and declared the Clare seat vacant. Num-
bers of constitutionalists shook their heads ; but
the decision stands.
Acting-Premier Tozer told Q. Par-
liament the other day that Jap. immi-
gration had been practically stopped by
the new passport system. Next mail-boat
brought a number of Japs, without pass-
ports, who landed just the same; and a
later boat dumped down 50 with passports
—as if the passport made a bit of differ-
ence to white Australia ! No fear ! the
Japs, don’t establish a first-class line of
steamers, and give champagne banquets at
which the Hon. Swelled-Head Byrnes
wishes them luck on behalf of Kanaka-
land, without intending to make that line
pay—pay with freight and pay with pas-
sengers. And, as for preventing one
small brown toe of one small brown
Jap. from reaching this country, Premier
Nelson’s diplomatic moonshine would as
soon make ice-water flow out of his favorite
Glenhaggis whisky-bottle.
Four S.A. Ministers are in favor
of the Labor-party’s proposal to absolutely
stop the sale of public lands and adopt
the leasing principle entirely. The others
are hostile or doubtful, so the Govern-
ment has decided to make it an open
Juestion. That good old crusted Tory,
ohn Downer, is bitterly indignant at
this, and says the perpetual lease system
is reverting to one of the features of the
barbaric ages. Certainly it was a feature
in some places in some parts of the bar-
baric ages, but that doesn’t prove that
it should be abandoned. Other features
of the same barbaric ages were food,
clothes, boots, religion and marriage, and
if a thing is necessarily wrong because it
was practised in the dark ages, then
ugly, good-humored John Downer should
be a naked atheist with a concubine.
And the spectacle of a Tory objecting to
anything because it is old and rests on
a fine, solid, well-established precedent
of long ago, is rather ridiculous.
The latest news from Queensland is that
the Federal Enabling Bill is not to be introduced
this session after all. No particular reason is
assigned, except that Queensland has got no soul
or patriotism or sincerity—has got nothing, in
fact, except the wobbles in both its political feet.
Parliament will probably meet again early in the
year, and then, if the Q. Government goes straight
for the first time, there is a chance of getting the
measure through and the delegates elected about
two months after everything is over. But—
“ Nevermind ! have a whisky ? ”
Among the candi-
dates for Vic. Parlia-
ment this election was a
mining-man, who con-
siders that the Shiels
divorce-law stops far
short of publio needs, and
it was an incident of the
battle of ’94 which so con-
vinced him. Having
long ambitioned M.L.A.-
ship, the speculator had
spent a couple of years
in working up a constitu-
ency-paying guineas to
football clubs and regu-
larly attending A.N.A.
meetings, &o. But Pat-
terson’s dissolution came
suddenly, while the ambi-
tious one was at distant
Kalgoorlie. As nomina-
tion day was fixed, a few
friends wired, asking his
intentions. The prospec-
tive candidate replied :
“ Going to the poll; will
return at once. Will
wire deposit to wife.”
This satisfied the friends,
who formed a commit-
tee, spent the absent
man’s money lavishly on
beer and advts., and had
things well forward by
nomination day. Then
it was that the leading
spirits waited upon Mrs.
Candidate, and were
“knocked silly” by the
lady remarking : “ I
won’t let my husband
make such a fool of him-
self. He has too much to
do already without your
electioneering rubbish.”
The chairman scratched
his head. ‘ ‘ But, madam, ”
he urged, “your husband tells us that he has
sent you the £5O for his deposit.” “Yes,” re-
turned the lady, “and it’s been put to a much
better use than it would be wasted over your elec-
tion nonsense.”
Evidently even the Argus had no idea of
the strength of the white-waistcoat vote at Toorak.
In the latter days before the election it filled its
columns with eloquent appeals to the Stomach
party to vote as one large corporation for Gillies,
and not let the Liberal candidate get in by the
splitting of the Tory vote. And the event proved
that the Liberal candidate hadn’t a dog’s hope,
however much the Tory vote was split between
Gillies and Bryant. If the Stomach party had
put up three candidates, the lone, lost Liberal
would almost certainly have been lost just the
same.
HE KNEW HIM.
MRS. BALLYHOOLEY (to the lodger): “Now, Mr.
Sparkins, what do you think my dear Tommy
will he most fitted for when he grows up? ”
MR. S. (who knows the dear child) : “ Fit for anything,
my dear madam.”
An Early Closing Bill is, perhaps, th e most
difficult of all measures to engineer through Parlia-
ment. In Q. Assembly Labor-member McDonnell
wanted to omit wholesale houses from the opera-
tion of his Bill—without which amendment
(Tozer asserted) the Bill could not possibly pass
the Upper House. Whereupon the House rejoic-
ingly rejected the amendment. It was next pro-
posed to omit all the small “ one-man” shops. The
House acquiesced, and then wanted to include bar-
maids and waitresses in the list of “ early-closers. ”
And then confusion smote the legislators. .
BUGOLIG.
MR. COWMAN (from out west, inspecting bird-fancier's window, where a
canary is marked £1 1s.) : “ What, a guinea for a thing like
that! Why, Billy, we sell geese at four bob a pair.”
No reform of N.S.W. Leg. Council should
be accepted by the people unless payment of mem-
bers be made to apply to that body. One-half of
the “side” councillors wear they put on because
of not being hired. And, besides, if they work
their claim for pay should be taken as established.
Hunk of wisdom from squatter Murray,
M.L. A. (Q.) :
It would be better to force a man to live in an electorate
six months after recording his vote rather than six months
before it, so as to make him feel the effect of his action.
Very often he does feel it even now . . . for
straight politics often signify “ sack.”
Trenwith gains—in power, logic and popu-
larity. He had far and away the biggest and
most sympathetic meeting of any candidate, even
reckoning in Turner. At his opening meeting,
the big Richmond Town Hall was packed, stand-
ing-room being unobtainable even on the plat-
form ; and then hundreds went away.
Dear B., —Your correspondent speaks the
truth about employment by Melb. Met. Board of
Works. I can now get a job as soon as anyone
conditionally on my being R.C. This will be
denied, but I hold the proofs. At present lam
not taking any sewerage, whether Roman or
Boynesome. I prefer tamer colors and mates. —
Hiram Abiff.
Why does not some N.S. Wales M.P.
move that the nameg of all non-official persona
who use N.S.W. railways free shall be printed
weekly in the Government Gazette ? Late
events indicate that the Railway Commissioners
are practically powerless against a Ministerial
request, or debit the pass to an account which is
never liquidated.
For several months past the Maoriland
Government has been receiving applications from
people who reckon they can qualify under the
promised Old Age Pension law. About 5600
applicants have already passed muster, and the
total number will probably reach 8000. At 10s.
per week each that will mean a pension-list of
.£208,000 a year.
The other day a Queensland Labor mem-
ber’s pet bill came to grief in his temporary ab-
sence, and a murmur went round the Chamber:
“He must have gone out for a drink.” A very
similar thing happened a few days earlier through
the absence of a large, well-developed Tory, and
then the sympathetic murmur went round : “He
must have run after a girl.”
What is the real value of the Vic. Catholic
vote? In Prahran, Gray carried it in ’94, and
beat the Tory by 306 out of a total of 1868 ; the
outsider at both elections may be neglected. The
week before last, out of 2152 votes, Gray’s majority
over the Tory, who this time carried the R.C.
vote, was 474. It seems to show that, in the
secret balloting - chamber, polities come before
religion. *
Jonas Levien, M.L.A., of the great Mil-
dura Joint-Stock Stink, Limited, had the brassy
effrontery at the late Vic. election to assert that
the Mildura laborers were not paid because the
Government seized on the £3OOO intended for their
wages. In the bad old days the Mildura Co.
owed £BOOO to Govt., but, finding that £3400 was
due for wages, Govt, offered to take £3OOO in set-
tlement provided the Mildura Co. paid its
laborers out of the other £5OOO. And Jonas
Levien’s plea is that the Govt, should have
knoeked-off the whole £BOOO so that the co. could
pay £3400 wages out of it and have the rest for
itself.
The declaration of Opposition - member
Fraser (M.L.) that the purchase of Bushy Park
by the sons of Minister M'Kenzie was a perfectly
square transaction, and that the M ‘Kenzies got
the property simply because they were the highest
tenderers, knocked a lot of the vim out of the
Tory party. Fraser is a Tory with a clean record,
and his exoneration of the hated crowd opposite
counts for a great deal.
In reply to the assertions of Vic. Scrip-
ture League, the electorates have returned 5i
members dead against Bible-reading in school*,
24 nominally in favor (with about eight separate
and irreconcileable “views” as to brand and
method), and 20 who have not declared either way.
Thus, so far as State authority goes, the youth of
Victoria are safe from contamination by Onan,
Lot, David, Mrs. Potiphar and co.
Ed. Bulletin, — Apropos the N.S.W.
Estimates. Civil servants who got the “ wink ”
that they were down for increases are now asking
how far the powers of the Public Service Board
extends. The triumvirate were appointed by the
Reid Govt, to have full control of the working of
the departments, and Reid and party com-
placently tread on their own tail by striking out
the Board’s recommendations.—O.l.C.
Despite all the statements about the ab-
sence of political excitement, 182,000 Victorians
went to the poll at the late election, against 135,000
in 1894. Yet 1894 was the year when Victoria
rose up in a blaze of excitement and fired Patter-
son and Carter into oblivion. The Liberal party
scored 50,000 more votes this time than it did
three years ago, while the avowedly Conservative
vote has diminished since 1894 by nearly 5000.
More Government-by-Contradiction. Pour
out of five Tasmanian Ministers promised the
Emu Bay Railway co. certain concessions. The
odd-man-out is the Attorney-General, who says
the promised concessions are illegal. Yet, if the
House carries a no-confidence motion, out goes the
Attorney-General with his colleagues. Clark
solves the difficulty by resigning on his own.
Otherwise he would have to help the Ministry to
defend the act which he says is illegal.
Foster Rogers, who went out with a loud
crash at the Vic. election, was the gentleman who
wanted a daily Hansard, also to have it posted
gratis every day to every elector in Victoria.
There are about 250,000, and in a session of 112
working days, which is not uncommon, the State
would have had to print and hump gratis to all
the remotest parts of the country 28 million Han-
sards. Poor Rogers never could quite under-
stand the contumely that was heaped on his great
idea.
“A BIT OF A STAGGERER."
(Sketch in Oxford-street, Sydney.)
Agent-General Play ford does not smile on
ex-journalist Govett’s mission to London as
hawker of S.A. State Bank bonds, and there was
a stormy interview when the latter arrived in
London. Playford sensibly points out that there
will be no margin for profit after 12s. 6d. per -6100
is paid for remittance to Adelaide, to say nothing
of the cost of remitting interest to London, and
finally sending back the principal when the loan
is paid off. As Playford has great influence over
Kingston, not unlikely Govett will be recalled.
But why couldn’t Acting-Premier Holder do this
little sum in compound addition himself before
Govett started ?
A well-known Northerner has recently been
at newspaper-war with that accomplished .Tap.,
Mr. Satow. The Caucasian pen-man argued that,
inasmuch as by the treaty of ’5B every British
subject in Japan must, when travelling, be pro-
vided with a. passport and accompanied by a
policeman, therefore the Japs, in Australia have
no right to grumble at restrictions laid on them
by Australian legislatures. Whereupon Satow
rejoins that his countrymen would feel highly
honored if they were trotted round under the
guidance of the police ; that Japan treats the
foreigner thus as a mark of distinction, &c. Evi-
dently the Jap. is no mean casuist.


Maoriland Labor bodies are agitating to
have Ships, while discharging cargo, brought
under the provisions of the Factories Act.
The new W.A. Attorney-General, Yio.
barrister “ Bob ” Pennefather, is best remembered
in Sydney as the defender of Cordini, tried for
murder before Judge Windeyer. Pennefather
“ stood up ” to the Judge in court—partly because
he was a gritty little man anyhow, but also
partly, no doubt, because he was a Victorian
barrister who didn’t appear, and didn’t expect to
appear, every day in a N.S.W. court. Any
barrister game to try and take a bone out of Judge
Windeyer’s mouth while the Judge had it pinned
down with his forepaws and was “ doing a gnaw,”
was bound to make some sort of a mark.

Child-Flogging in “Sunny N.S. Wales.
ATNarandera, N.S.W., last week, R. A. Thomas,
local manager of the A. J.S. Bank, was charged
with ill-treating his nursegirl, a motherless
child 10 years old. Medical evidence showed
that the child had whip or strap scars over
her back and legs. By way of variety, a laundress
testified that the child was put under the shower-
bath for half-an-hour on a very cold morning.
Defendant (who denied undue severi y) engaged
all the lawyers in the town, but the residents sub-
scribed to bring one from Wagga to prosecute.
Some cheerful evidence (from Nakandera Ensign,
Oct. 22,1897): —
Police Court. Thursday, October 21,1897. Before Mr.
G. Martin, P.M., of Wagga. Charge of cruelly illtreating
a child. Reginald Archibald Thomas, bank manager of
A. J.S. Bank, Narandera, was charged on the information
of Senior-constable Bowman with having between the 15th
of May and 12th October, 1897, wilfully illtreated Sarah
Blakely, a girl about 10 years and 9 months, and such ill-
treatment has resulted in bodily suffering to the said
child. Mr. Coleman, of Wagga, appeared for the prose-
cution, and Messrs. VVillans, with Messrs. Robertson and
Hegarty, and Mr. Quirk, for the defence.
Dr. G. Watt deposed: . . . The child was well-
nourished; her back, right arm, buttocks and legs showed
signs of bruises varying in age from four weeks to as many
months ; several bruises on her back and abdomen had
been caused probably by a strap, while many of the others
seemed to have been caused by an instrument with a
rounded edge ; wales on the flesh showed, and the skin in
many places was broken ; there was a clean cut on scalp
of head of old standing ; the right eyelid was bruised by
knock or blow, and skin slightly cut; a mark the size of a
shilling was on left buttock ; two or three skin ruptures
appeared on the abdomen ; etc. . . .
Dr. W. H. O’Neill corroborated.
The child, a bright-looking little girl, was not sworn,
but instructed by the P.M. to tell the truth. She said her
name was Sarah Blakely ; had lived with Mr. Thomas for
over three years -at first, in the bank in Temora ; I used
to mind the baby and do work about the house ; remem-
ber the hospital ball, about four weeks ago, on a Thurs-
day ; about 4 or 6 o’clock I saw Mr. Thomas, when he
came home; I was downstairs, in the toolroom,
and he called me upstairs; Mrs. Thomas was out;
Mr. Thomas was standing in the hall, and he
asked if I had soruhbed the room I had been
ordered to do by Mrs. Thomas ; I told him yes. He took
me to the room and rubbed his hand on the floor, and
asked me if that part was done ; I said yes. He said I was
telling a story, and told me to go and find the whip. I
went and found it and brought it to him ; he then told
me to get undressed, and I did so ; then he beat me with
the greenhide whip. 1 took off all my clothes except my
boots and socks. He beat me in the bedroom. I had been
beaten with the same whip before ; he used the whip for
playing polo. . . . My mother died six years ago, when I was
four years old. Don’t know where father is. Mr. Thomas
took me after mother died and kept me ever since. In
reply to the P.M., the child said while she lived with Mr.
Thomas she was treated fairly well. Once Mr. Thomas
told me to get into the bath ; that was last Monday week,
while he was there in the bathroom. I got in ; the water
was cold ;he had the whip under his arm at the time. I
was in the bath about a quarter of an hour and was very
cold. ...
Margaret Keegan, laundress, deposed that she had been
washing at Mr. Thomas’ house last Monday week ; the
little girl present was there ; I saw Mr. Thomas come in
and call the child, who was in the bathroom. This was
about half-past seven in the morning; he told the child
to go to the bathroom and undress, and I heard him tell
her to turn on the shower. Ten minutes afterwards I saw
Mr. Thomas come from the yard with a greenhide whip
in his hand and go to the bathroom. Heard the child
crying ; she was in the room half-an-hour, and the shower
was turned on her all the time. It was a very cold morn-
ing ; when she came out her body was all blue and all
drawn up with the cold. . . .
Mr. Thomas deposed : I am manager of the local branch
of the A. J.S. Bank. The girl Blakely entered my service
two or three years ago, forget the exact date. A friend
in the Temora district asked me to take her as her father,
who was blind, could do nothing with her. During the
time she went to school with his own children and was
treated exactly as they were. Her general character was
very bad, lying, stealing, and disobedience, in conse-
quence I found it necessary to chastise her with a strap.
I did not strip her . . . never kicked her.
Mrs. Mary Thomas, wife of defendant, deposed : The
girl Blakely has lived with us for about three years ; her
general conduct has been mischievous and untruthful ;
she has been treated the same as our children in all ways,
amusements included. Mr. Thomas has chastised her
when I was unable to do so. He did not punish her
excessively. . . .
The Bench fined defendant £25 and costs.
Judge Gibson recently sent a poor wretch to gaol
for three years— not for stripping a 10-year-old
motherless girl and beating her till the blood came,
but for stealing a ninepenny tin of baking-powder.
But he was not Respectable.
Commemoration of ’gB
On behalf of the ’9B Commemoration Committee, we
hereby invite Irishmen and their descendants of every
shade of Irish political belief, and also ladies of Irish
birth or descent, and all sympathisers to attend a
PUBLIC MEETING
to be held at the GUILD HALL, Castlereagh Street,
(between Park and Bathurst-streets), SYDNEY, at 8
8 o’clock on
Tuesday Next, 2nd November,
for the purpose of taking steps to honor during the
