“Under the best apple tree are found the most
sticks and stones.” We desire to warn the public
against many so-called cycle-agents, who are deliber-
ately circulating false reports against “Bed Birds”
in order to further their own ends. They stop at
nothing to turn the inexperienced cyclist against our
goods, and from the reports which have come under
our notice, we conclude that some of the trade are
making it hard work to pay expenses, and are find-
ing considerable trouble to sell against Red Birds.
Experienced cyclists who know the value of a good
machine, have almost invariably selected the Bed
Bird, which accounts for the large number which can
be seen in all parts of the country.
One of the great features of the new Red Bird is
its bottom bracket. It is a triumph of modern skill
in cycle-building, and is a. feature as far ahead of
bottom brackets on other cycles as the pneumatic
tyre is ahead of the solid. The two cups which form
the bearings are turned from the finest grade of steel
possible to manufacture. They contain ten 5-16 balls.
Each cup is threaded to a steel sleeve or barrel which
adjusts inside the bottom bracket proper, and is held
in place by two bolts.
With the bearings, it is only necessary to oil same
about twice a year, as they are thoroughly dust-proof
and oil-retaining, the balls running in a continuous
oil-bath.
With other cycles it is almost impossible to get a
perfectly true chain-alignment, whereas the chain and
the bearings, with the aid of this most important im-
provement, can be adjusted to 1-1000 part of an inch.
Our lines for the present season will suit all classes
of customers. Terms. Cash.
Red Birds will continue to sell at £26 10s. £25 10s.
Ruby Rims, better than any
other high-class wheel .. .. £2l os. £lB 18s.
Black Birds, thoroughly high-
grade wheel at cheap price of £l6 10s. £l5 os.
All the good points of bicycles are not confined to
one wheel. There are wheels and wheels, so there
are distinguished features in each.
The Red Bird combines more practical, progressive
improvements than any other cycle. Light, swift
and rigid, it is pre-eminently the wheel for racers and
road riders. Its past record is convincing proof of
the strength and easy-running qualities.
The new model is improved beyond all others. If
you have not examined one, it will pay you to look
up the nearest agent.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Good racehorses are undoubtedly scarce in Aus-
tralia for the present—so scarce that, if the Carbine of
1890 were going for this Melbourne Cup with XOst. 71b. on
his five-year-old back, The Bulletin would reckon him a
real certainty, in a turf-way of speaking. With one pos-
sible exception, all the first-class Australian-bred horses
that competed for classic honors in ’96, ’95 and ’94, have
gone wrong or left the country. Therefore, the two or
three best three-year-olds of this season would have had
first claims on Cup consideration but for the handicapper’s
precaution in givingthem burdens heavier than youngsters
are ordinarily able to carry home. Aurum has Bst. 61b.—
half a stone more than Newhaven landed 12 months ago.
Amberite’s penalty brings him up to Bst. 41b. Although one
or other of them the writer prefers Aurum —will probably
kin the Derby, victory in either case cannot be accepted as
a “ straight tip ” for the Cup. On the other hand, should
they get beaten, or even “ split,” in a fast run Derby race
by anything that is lightly handicapped for the
Cup—say Manfred, Clarion, Majestic, or Virtue — then
the unexpected one will seem to have a 5 to 1 chance
to do what Newhaven and Auraria did of yore. At time
of writing, the Derby doesn’t seem likely to yield a
new Cup favorite. Eirids-dale, a promising outsider,
is not entered for the big handicap. Majestic is. This
I fine, massive colt was lately backed at Caulfield by the St.
Alban’s stable, and there may have been a fair excuse for
; his poor running. If the writer were compelled to back a
three-year-old for Melbourne Cup before the Derby is
fought, he would take Majestic on spec., at a long price.
Passing to the older horses, the writer will jump
to the conclusion that Ayrshire, Wait-a-Bit, Parthenopseus
and Positano are less likely to stay the distance than
Coil, Delaware, Toreador, Devon, The Merry Boy, Fucile,
and Trent. Ayrshire swerved, as though beaten, at the
finish of the Caulfield Cup race. Wait-a-Bit gives the
impression that he has trained “ off ”-a-bit. Partheno-
pseus has never been regarded as a real stayer, and when
his late owner was disappointed in his hope of bringing
off the Caulfield Cup coup he let the horse go to auction.
Positano’s appearance suggests that a doubtful leg, or
something else, has prevented him from being wound up
to the pitch absolutely necessary for a Melb. Cup win.
This is a more important point than his horrid temper.
As a possibly first-class horse, the English-bred
Positano, with only 7st. 71b. to carry, would seem to have
the Cup at his mercy if he were a proved two-iniler, but
he isn’t. On paper he should beat Coil and Delaware for
the Melbourne Stakes (1J mile) next Saturday, but, also
on paper, Coil and Delaware should heat him. The
writer reckons that one of the aforesaid three will win the
Stakes, and that victory in Coil’s case would label him
“ very dangerous ” for the Cup. Whereas Positano or
Delaware could win the 1J mile race without earning a
right to the same label. Meantime, the writer, preferring
this pair of unproven stayers to Toreador, Devon, Merry
Boy, Fucile and Trent, on the ground that they have
higher-** class ” credentials, and are more favorably handi-
capped, will take Coil to beat them all and attempt to
show reason for the faith that is in him.
Coil, as a two-year-old, and again last season,
was an unreliable performer, like his rival, Newhaven,
but occasionally he stood out from the common herd as
a racehorse of real merit. He didn’t show any of New-
haven’s brillianoy, nevertheless he was good enough to
run third for the Newmarket Handicap (9st. up), and win
the Australian Cup from a moderate field. This season
Coil has out a respectable figure on five occasions, and in
his latest performance, a ten-lengths victory in the Caul-
field Stakes, he looked very like a triton among minnow’s.
Probably he has improved, as a racer, with age, and is
getting the better of the constitutional weakness that
made him a very in-and-out customer in his younger days*
Abercorn, the sire of Coil, had a tendency to softness
until he touched his fifth year. The weak spot was
transmitted to his offspring, perhaps. Anyway, the
writer will adopt the weak - spot theory, and give
Coil credit for having grown much stronger, internally,
since he carried Bst. 91b. into fourth place for the Sydney
Cup, last April. Seeking a reasonable “ tip,” rather than
a mere guess, for the Melbourne Cup, one can find nothing
as good as Coil. He ought to go near it. Patron, in ’94,
looked much the same thing on paper as Coil looks to-
day, and Patron won his Melbourne Cup pretty easily.
The conclusion of this long sermon is : Coil to win, fol-
lowed by Delaware, Positano, and- one or more of the
three-year-olds.
Symmetry, with fair luck in getting away,
should win the Maribyrnong Plate scramble.
» » * •
On the principle that “nothing succeeds like
success ” The Bulletin prints a metrical tip for the ’97
Melb. Cup from “ H. 0.,” who here tipped Amberite for
Caulfield :
“ Believers in Cup omens (writes R.W. to The
Bulletin) may find an alphabetical promise of Auruin’s
success in the present fashionableness of the letter ‘ A ’
and its general luckiness. The name of the winner of
Caulfield Cup began with A—and was of gold-colored
significance besides. Look at the run on that letter just
now among theatricals and singers—taking the fair sex
alone. We have Ada Ferrar, Ada Willoughby, Ada
Reeve, Ada Humphreys, Ada Crossley, Addie Conyers,
Amy Sherwin, Alma Grey, all of whom have done well
this year. Is it because the unpretentious little name
‘ Ada ’ means good fortune that so many professionals
have adopted it ? ”
* * * •
The Vic. lady who decided that she would
“have nothing tc do with those low racing-men,” who
owned a Caulfield Cup favorite which she had drawn in
Tatt’s Sweep, was perfectly furious when she learned
after the race that the low racing men had elected to
spin for Melbourne Cup, in which their chances of get-
ting a cut in at the sweep money was "better assured.
* * * *
A Melb. civil servant tipped and backed the
seven winners on the card on the first day of Caulfield
meeting. He won £5O on Amberite. A shilling invested
on Carbineer, and played up on the other six winners,
would have brought in £41,000.
» » » *
At race-time, the hotel with a barman who can
talk horse and “has information,” does better business
than the hotel with a beauteous barmaid who tries to
push a sweep along.
* * * •
S.A. cycling officials were not blameless in the
Martin business. They called up Plugger Bill for sus-
picious riding in the mile event. When Bill explained
that he was exhausted from riding a 101-gear instead of
his usual 96, they exonerated him. When the Murkan
came out a quarter-of-an-hour later to ride in the final of
another race, he was peremptorily" ordered off the track.
Martin was very indignant at this showing-up before the
crowd, and he denied the power of the league to call him
off without reason. Apparently the officials, as well as
Martin, made fools of themselves.
Apropos Bill Martin’s 14 days “ without the
option ” at Adelaide police-court and his six months’ dis-
qualification by the league. The Plugger has “ a set ”on
Adelaide officials. On one afternoon, owing to “rumors”
that William meditated a visit, several officials spent some
hours in the league strongroom. “ I don’t know what’s
come over William lately,” said his Yankee trainer. And
it really does look as if the iron-jawed one has got a men-
tal kink somewhere.
Martin’s special victim in Adelaide, Ridley,
was laid up for a week with two black eyes and a battered
nose and ears. Martin’s grievance against Ridley goes
back to September last year, when Ridley was one of the
Judges concerned in disqualifying him. Another official,
one of the referees, also complained that William as-
saulted him. “Did you see him kick me?” said the
assaulted one to a bystander. “If he didn’t I shall be
only too durned glad to do it again ! ’’ rejoined the gentle
William. Offer declined.
* * * *
Maybe Bill Martin has cycled himself on to the
verge of mental aberration. The ordinary road biker
acquires a “face”—the gin drinker gets a liver—the
cigarette smoker a particular affection of the lungs, or
heart, or something. Why not a “ plugger’s brain ” ?
* • * «
Plugger Bill having been “ sent up ” for 14 days’
hard labor, the question arises as to what the aforesaid
hard labor will consist of. Plugger is palpably unfit for
office work, and to set so famous a man breaking stones
would be blasphemous. Now, the treadmill seems to be
just the thing. Good results for the authorities—good
cycling condition for Bill. Talk about home trainers !
Bill will ask for the treadmill, sure.
* * * *
At N.S.W. Wheelmen’s carnival proceedings
began with the Lap Handicaps, which catch on as well as
they deserve. The crowd cannot be expected to get
delirimn-tremens over second and third-class dashes.
The d6but of Platt-Betts was nothing to get upon the
roof and rave over. The chubby young cockney has cer-
tainly a supple movement which was never seen before in
Australia. Phatt-Betts should be sent back to England
\\ ith the next shipload of prime beef, or else be asked to
ride in matches. This country is too tired to watch
record-breakers. The only public interest taken in track-
cycling is in racing. Direct competition between man
and man—that is the human interest of the thing. Other-
wise, you might just as well watch a paddle-wheel
revolve.
The Rev. W. Martin not being there to regulate
matters was a bit of a damper to the carnival; especially
when the final of the one-mile Scratch Race was ordered
to be re-run. Had the strong pluggerwon it instead of
Walne, the League Racing Board might now be all planted
behind its Ma or up the nearest drain-pipe, whilst Bill
tramped round and snorted for gore. All save Megson
grafted out their gall in the first finish ; yet, when ordered
to re-ride, only one man demurred. And all he said was
under his breath, “ Strike me, yous is blanky strong.” In
the re-ride Green got the jump, but was caught by Megson
and tied for first place.
• * • *
There are 400 entries for the coining axemen’s
contest at Melb. Exhibition Grounds 160 from Tasmania,
over 50 from Westralia, and about 180 in all from N.S.W.,
Victoria and Maoriland. The Glenelg steamer brings a
load of logs from Tasmania—the high-school of axeing
education —and “ all the champions ” have undertaken to
chip into the great Carnival chop.
» * • *
Great preparations are being made for the axe-
men’s competition aforesaid. Friday, 20th, is the first
day, but the world’s championship event takes place
on Cup-night by electric light. Tasmania is running
the show and sends along a score of champions in ground
and lofty r chopping, and many topsaw.vers, and about 60
wood-jambers besides. Melb. spielers expect a harvest.
* * * *
The great George Gitfen—let every base varlet
kneel at the mention of the name—refused to play for
S.A. and Australia against Stoddart and co,, it is alleged,
because Major Wardill refused to agree to his demands
for expenses. Further, George is credited with being
much annoyed because S.A. Cricket Association didn’t
include him among its latest list of life-members (which it
certainly could have afforded to do). The writer firmly
believes, however, that if Giffen stands down it will even-
tually prove a grand thing for S.A. cricket, for numerous
reasons known to every local cricketer.
* '* * *
Stoddart and co. are expected to arrive in Mel-
bourne per Adelaide express on Cup-dav—just in time to
get to their hotels, freshen up, and be at Flemington
to see (?) win. If a foreign army were to attack
Australia, they’d time the invasion so as to see Melb. Cup
—and, probably, depart satisfied if they got on to the
winner.
« * * *
Young Trumper is batting in great form—B3 on
Saturday week, run out, and last Saturday 123, caught by
a brilliant catch off a fine stroke. The fine play Saturday
after Saturday by such Sydney colts as Trumper, Pye,
Poidevin, Hopkins, Austin, Noonan, and Farquhar, will
make the picking of the N.S.W. representative team par-
ticularly difficult.
* * * *
George Giffen’s little brother Walter made 127
in a club-match in Adelaide, Oct. 16, by really fine
cricket, and will probably be in the S.A. team for the
match against Stoddart and co., commencing this Thurs-
day (28th)
* * * *
Not unlikely S.A. will shake up Stoddart and
co., who will be in poor form, with but two days’pre-
liminary practice. Jones is in great bowling form, and
Darling, Lyons, arid Hill have already all made good
scores in club cricket.
* * * *
The opening ceremony of the League of Vic.
Wheelmen’s splendid rooms was a miserable travesty on
the alleged democracy and brotherhood of cycling, which
the cycling press is so loud in trumpeting. The shivoo
was attended only by a handful of league members, ad-
mitted by invitation ticket. Outside a score of the most
prominent Australian racing men gloomily passed the
time on the footpath, while Acting-Gov. Madden and a
score of the league elect were participating in the cham-
pagne within.
One of the drawbacks of the league’s premises
is the presence of several religious cranks—relics of the
days when the Y.M.C.A. owned the building, who stand
in the street and make personal remarks about the souls
of the passers-by. This evangelical gang was on the spot
when Gov. Madden arrived, and one of the comihon or
garden league members, mad at not being invited, worked
off a joke on the unsuspecting C.J. Madden having
arrived, the joker stepped up to him with such a confi-
dent air that the visitor at once put him down as an
official, and when he was solemnly led up to a monkey-
bearded gentleman with a hymn-book and assured “ This
is Mr. Salter, the vice-president of the league, Sir John,”
he grasped the evangelist warmly by the hand and said,
“ Pleased indeed to meet you, Mr. Salter.” It took the
real secretary about ten minutes to sort things out, dur-
ing which t'mi the chuckling avenger escaped. Mean-
while the gorgeously-tailored Salter was glaring on the
doorstep.
* * * *
Even the presence of Dr. W. J. Barry, the
giant English athlete and amateur champion of the world
at “ heavy weight athletics,” failed to draw more than
1000 payers to the Vic. Amateur Show, at St. Kilda, last
Saturday. The rain was no doubt partially responsible,
but it is the wretched management of past amateur
meetings that has sickened the public. The events this
time were, as a rule, fairly handicapped and contested,
and instead of the crowd having to wait until a dreary
three-mile walk was fought out, two courses were
arranged, so that the walkers would be battling on the
inner while cycle sprints were taking place on the outside
track.
Dr. Barry had a soft thing in the hammer-
throwing contest, in which he flung a 161 b. knapper 119 ft.
9in., apparently without effort, the next best man scoring
only 86ft. The previous Vic. record was made by W. J.
Doolan and stood at 93ft. Bin. In putting the weight
Barry tossed 161 b. of cold iron 36ft. 4Jin., D. J. Bevan fol-
lowing with 33ft. 9Jin. A trifle over 6ft. 4in. high and
weighing 17st., Barry was the feature of the afternoon,
and received a real ovation. He is an Irishman (of course),
and scored first at Queen’s College, Cork, where he was a
fellow student of Collins-st. surgeon O’Hara, of stoushing
celebrity.
Bevan, the runner-up in the weight-throwing, is
a tip-top all-round athlete, and, though he unfortunately
excels at no one branch of sport, would probably take down
any other Australian amateur in a “points” contest em-
bracing all departments. On Saturday he won (more by
good luck than good management) 120yds. hurdles cham-
pionship and (on its merits) the long jump of 21ft. lin., a
Vic. record. On lepping this distance, Bevan’s pictur-
esque “ old man,” the Melb. divine, stepped up to his red-
headed progeny and kissed him vigorously, to the great
borak of an irreverent gathering.
In the cycling events, Park proved himself the
boss amateur of the lot, and the real owner of the
amateur championship—a fine record for a man who
never crossed a bike until eight months ago. A lawyer
by occupation, Park has, in his spare time, succeeded in
acquiring the fine, easy, swinging gait of which cham-
pions are made, and he is probably as speedy a cyclist as
the fallen amateur, Berry. Defeating Reynolds, of M.L.,
by a clear length in the mile championship, Park started
for, and won, the half-mile handicap from scratch, after
which he came out for the five-mile championship. A
splendid race home took place between him and Rey-
nolds, the two leaving the field standing, and the Maori -
lander only squeezed in front by a tyre.
* * * *
The nine professionals of Stoddart’s team, while
in Melbourne, will stay at the White Hart Hotel, opposite
Parliament House, 10 or 15 minutes through gardens from
the M.C.C. ground. The pro’s of last English team put
up at the same ancient hostelry. The “gentlemen’ will
probably camp at Menzies’, but people are wondering if
Prince Ranhitsswiftly will be invited to isolate himself at
Government House.
» * * *
The first decent wrestling-match that has hap-
pened in Melbourne for years was witnessed by a big
crowd at the old Victoria Hall, the other Saturday night,
when Jack Graham, the veteran champion middle-weight,
and Lucifer, the young and elegant athlete, who looks
like a muscular Greek god fresh from a .Turkish bath,
hurled each other about the premises for the champion-
ship and a stake of £5O a side. The result was one of the
most interesting exhibitions of muscular strength, manual
and pedal dexterity, and scientific scrambling that the
present writer has ever seen. Graham is certainly the
better wrestler, as the term is conventionally understood,
but he came into the arena with a corporation like that of
Ephraim Zox, and with 40-odd years on his shaven head,
whereas the other was as firm and white and clean-limbed
as a marble Athenian, and is probably under 30. Lucifer
is a contortionist, and is the American person who gave a
strong-man turn with the Firm’s last panto. Lucifer’s
methods quile disconcerted the veteran wrestler, who
imagined he had him on toast several times, when the
former would suddenly split himself down the middle,
and, straddling over the stage with the two halves of him
sticking out in different directions, defy the animated
steam-crane on top to up-end him ; whereupon Graham
would take a heart-broken survey of the human problem,
and regretfully abandon the struggle. It was like a hip-
popotamus wrestling with an anaconda. Lucifer secured
the first fall with a neat hamrnerlock, arid Graham got the
second. The third was unattainable, apparently, and the
referee announced a draw after two hours’ hefting and
heaving ; whereupon Lucifer challenged the champion to
a pully-hatrl to a finish, and Graham was understood to
accept the invitation. If the tug comes off its ould be
a popular spectacle, as Lucifer's comic relief is as shriek-
ingly funny as it is scientifically effective.
A FANCY.
“ O, tell me, tell me what to back,’*
The festive widow cried.
Her weeds are now a rusty black,
And she is wicked-eyed ;
Her sweet intent to be embraced
’Twas not for me to foil,
In serpent style, around her waist,
I said : “I’m fond of Coil.’’
“ I cannot tell you what will win
Nor what will get a place ;
The * Lamb ’ that bookies love to skin
May carry off the race ;
Thousrh Positano prove a * pot,”
Perchance he’ll overboil,
And Aurum, with the weight he s got,
Can hardly beat the blessed lot—
I’d rather go for Coil.
“ Big Delaware seems w'ondrous fit,
I’m well aware of that;
And Taylor’s sweet on Wait-a-Bit,
The bay from Ballarat.
Cook’s Merry Boy good work has done,
And thriven on the toil;
But, since you bid me name the one
That I expect to take the bun,
I'll put a name to Coil.
“True, Ayrshire’s fast —but does he stay?
Fucile’s a Carbine horse ;
Dark Trent is built the proper way
To last a trying course ;
And Titan’s brother, Toreador,
Makes light of heavy soil;
Whilst Key—but, as I said before,
If I’m to have a lucky * drore,’
The winner must be Coil.
“ Young Manfred oft has brought to grief
His backers (I’ve been there) ;
Yet he might win—the craven thief—
With many lengths to spare.
If sturdy Devon's not too old
He may secure the spoil;
Of True Blue tales are sometimes told,
But, all the same, my bit of gold
Will take its chance on Coil.
“ Turf-tippirig is a foolish thing
That mostly breeds remorse,
Yet fancies in the vernal spring
Will turn to thoughts of ’orse.
When Ikey Mo, next Tuesdaj’ morn,
Sets up his loud turmoil,
I’ll back the son, by Copra borne
To brave old father Abercorn —
My fancy turns to Coil.”
Melb.
E.F.
All the year he makes pretence
At Australian defence,
Though of fighting anyone he’s got no notion,
And the secret of his pride,
In the squadron on the tide,
Is the thought that Britain rules the mighty ocean.
The initials, you can guess,
That he loves are H.M.S.,
And his love of lace to landsmen’s very filling;
And he prates of “Britain’s sons”
(They’re the men who clean the guns,
And who turn out now and then to do some drilling).
He has officers and men,
And he sees ’em now and then,
If they’re not upon some very distant island ;
But he knows that loads of grease
Keep the guns in climes of peace
And the “ safety ” of the great Australian dry land !
But arrives the time at length
When the squadron in its strength
Comes together from all kinds of curious places,
And Port Phillip smiles to see,
At its annual horsey spree,
Britain’s bulwarks betting madly at the races.
For the tar that they call Jack,
Though he may not know a hack
From an elephant, has saved a bit of money,
And h«- flings it night and day
When he lands in Hobson's Bay—
Jack ashore’s a youth that’s very, very funny I
Then, when past and gone the Cup,
To the rope that’s all rolled up
Britain’s bulwark once again is quickly hurried,
Or, he sits on it and dreams
As the squadron slowly steams
To a specklet when the Carnival is buried.
There’s the tip for 1,2, 3 ;
Though you don’t yourself agree,
Learn it off and then recite it to your neighbour; ’
There’s Fleet Admiral’s pride and joy,
And Jack Tar, The Merry Boy,
And the Coil of rope that’s emblem of his labour.
H.C.

The Young Lady who would go
to a Dance.
“I went to such a delightful dance last night,’’
said a young lady, by name Miss Elsie Kelly, of
42 Palmer-street, Darlinghurst, thereby disclosing
the fact that life still contained pleasures and com-
forts for her. “But,” continued she, “less than a
month ago, however, I never expected to be able
to dance again, as I was suffering most virulently
from a fearful attack of anaemia that threatened
to cut my existence short. You see, I’m in my
teens, and am consequently at an age that is criti-
cal to all Australian girls. My trouble began
from indigestion in the most cruel form. With-
out apparent reason, I found myself forced to live
on small pieces of toast, water biscuits, bovril,
and such light food, and even the digestion of
these occasioned me the greatest pain ; so bad was
I that I was even unable to take vegetables. Ex-
cessive palpitation of the heart also distressed me
acutely, and this became so exhausting that even
if I merely went up the stairs (a short flight) my
heart would beat with such terrific force
against my side that I could hear it plainly, and
I was always compelled to sink into a chair for
several minutes to recover my breath, and till
the excited heart-beating quietened somewhat. I
was even unable to walk up the hill on the east-
ern portion of the Domain, near Woolloomooloo,
without support. My kneecaps swelled to an
extraordinary size, and sharp, shooting pains
would dart persistently down my legs if I essayed
to walk; the agony was unendurable as if I’d been
struck with red-hot iron rods. Erequently I was
compelled to put my legs off the ground onto a
chair so as to ease the awful pain in my knees. If
I did go for a walk, when I returned, after suffer-
ing excruciating agony all the way, I would be
quite unable to sleep—an unprecedented occurrence
with me. At times, also, I was attacked by
headaches, my face was always pale, dark rings
came like stains under my eyes, which lost their
lustre. I was totally unable to ride my bicycle. I
had always a nauseating taste in my mouth, and
my gums turned to a peculiar white colour. My
breathing was forced and difficult, while I always
felt so ill, so tired, and so thoroughly 4 done-up ’
in every way that I could neither go out nor re-
main at home with composure. These fearful
sufferings continued for three weeks, and my
mother, in a frenzy of fright, consulted a well-
known doctor. ‘ It’s anaemia,’ he said, * and that
is a terrible complaint. Her blood is poverty-
stricken, and likely enough this is the first stage
of consumption. ’ My mother suggested a trial of
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People, as she
had heard a most satisfactory account of them ;
but the medico merely shrugged his shoulders,
looked imperturbable, and remarked, depreciat-
ingly, ‘ Try them if you like ! ’
“ 4 She did like, and that, probably,’ said the
young lady, with a flash of spirit, 4 is the reason I
am here to-day in perfect health to tell you all
about it. I took the pills according to directions,
and shortly afterwards a change for the better
resulted, whilst a little later on I found myself
wholly and totally cured. Pains, puffiness, heart-
palpitation, indigestion, headaches, sleeplessness,
the nauseous taste in my mouth, the restlessness
and the constant feeling of despondency, were all
swept from my system. The blushes came back
to my cheeks, the sparkle to my eyes, the spright-
liness to my step, the buoyant feeling to my heart,
the dark circles disappeared from under my eyes,
and my gums regained their natural color of red.
In short, I was a new person, better and stronger
even than before I had been ill. This is the first
time I have ever had sickness in my life, and I
only hope that if I should ever fall ill again I will
have this complete, wonderful and sovereign
remedy with me.”
Mrs. Kelly corroborated every word that her
daughter had said, making many kindly references
to Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People, which
she assured us she would recommend to all who
came within her reach. Mr. Kelly, who has a
host of friends, is well and favorably known, es-
pecially in maritime circles, as the head stevedore
of the North German Lloyd Steamship Company,
Limited.
Miss Kelly’s words clearly show that her cure is
permanent. These pills are not like other medi-
cine, and their effects are permanent. They act
directly on the blood, and thus it is that they are
so famous for the cure of anaemia and rheumatism,
weak heart, scrofula, consumption, chronic ery-
sipelas, and to restore pale and sallow complexions
to the glow of health. They are also a splendid
nerve and spinal tonic, and thus have cured many
cases of paralysis, locomotor ataxia, neuralgia, St.
Vitus’ dance, and nervous headache. They are
sold by chemists, and by Dr. Williams’ Medicine
Company, Queen's Place, Sydney, at 2s. 9d. a box
or six for lfis. 3d., but are genuine only with full
name, Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People.
See that your chemist does not substitute some-
thing just as good when making your purchases.
The just as good kind cost him less money, and he
is looking out for his pocket, not your health.*

High rents do not stop a Chinaman from
securing a piece of land that suits him. Tobacco-
growers pay =£l and 30s. per acre rent for land the
freehold of which probably did not cost the lessors
as much, and at Botany (Sydney) .£lO and even
£l5 per acre is paid as ground-rent for choice
gardening land. During a drought, about three
years ago, some Double Bay Chinese gardeners
proved, in connection with a claim for compensa-
tion, that they were making 7s. per week out of
one plot 40ft. square.
All old convict-settlements seem to be
specially cursed. Up Scone-way, the flies are
playing havoc with the sheep, and good old
Wingen Abbott works his cyclostyle in the same
unhappy district. Perhaps the Shearers’ Union
is in some way responsible for the plague—both
plagues, in fact. Port Arthur reeks with melan-
cholia ; Macquarie Harbor owns a dangerous bar ;
Hobart has Dobson ; Norfolk Island is ruled from
Sydney ; and Sydney has Brunker. And so on,
and so on. How the shadows of the system
cling !
The life of a M.L. gum-digger is a fair
training for Sheol. 111-fed, ill-housed, charged
six times to n-prices for necessaries by the store-
keepers, digging hard all day, scraping hard at
night (when there’s any gum to scrape), and
having to be pretty lucky to clear expenses by 2s.'
or 3s. per week. Yet, amongst the followers of
the woeful industry, are doctors, lawyers, ex-
Imperial officers, and the flotsam and jetsam of
society in every variety—including, according to a
Bulletin correspondent, at least one clergyman
and no less than three baronets.
D. Healey’s new Star game does great
credit to the Sydney inventor’s industry and
capacity. The star is a kind of five-headed
domino, and the addition of three heads gives j
the game an enormous variety of new and interest-1
ing combinations, of which the mathematical
validity is guaranteed absolutely. The new game
seems calculated to drive more people along the
flowery problem-path that leadeth to insanity
than the fifteen puzzle or pigs-in-clover—which is
saying seven volumes for it.
A Forward Step. —One commission
charge to large and small consignors is taken in
the wool-selling business by Pitt, Son, andßadgery
Ltd. The characteristic equity of this is self-evi-
dent, and will be appreciated.*
“The tools of the Australian bushman/
writes H.F. to The Bulletin, “ receive little
outside attention, and no maker specially caters
for him. The axes, invariably American, while
no doubt suited to the soft timber of that country,
are seldom of the right temper in the blade, and
always too long in the handle. You never see the
knob left on a bushman’s axe ; also he breaks out
every alternate tooth in the cross-cut saw, and
finds it cut better, and draws out his wedges
thinner and longer, so that they enter more easily.
There is no doubt that hardwood needs special
tools, and there seems a good opening for a local
firm who will study the trade; for, as matters
stand, buying new tools is a lottery, and a tool
that will stand is valued at treble its store price.’

ABRIDGED PROSPECTUS
Of the
BOOTH’S UNITED GOLD MINES COMPANY NO
LIABILITY,
Coolac, Near Gundagai, N.S.W.
CAPITAL, £60,000, in 60,000 shares of £1 each.
80,000 shares are offered to the public at 2s. 6d. per share
on application, and 2s. 6d. on allotment.
30,000 shares, fully paid-up, are to be held by the vendor.
The whole proceeds of the sale of 30,000 shares, without
any deduction whatever, will be placed to the credit of
the company for the purchase of battery and plant, and
for working expenses of the mine, as the vendor under-
takes to pay all brokerage, and preliminary expenses of
flotation and formation of the company, but as a consider-
able sum of money has been spent in securing and open-
ing up the properties, he is to receive, in addition to the
30,000 fully paid-up shares abovementioned, the first gross
returns from the crushings until the value of the same
amount to £3OOO.

This company is being formed by Mr. Alexander John
Miller to acquire from him, on the terms stated, certain
mining properties about four miles from Coolac railway
station, which is distant about 16 miles from Gundagai,
New South .Wales. These consist of a claim known as
“ Booth’s Reward,” which is an area of 35 acres recently
acquired by the vendor from the former proprietors (Mr.
Miller’s contract in relation to this acquisition may be
seen at the office of the Solicitors), and also an adjoining
claim of about 40 acres known as “ Mount Morgan,” ap-
plied for by Mr. Miller himself, as well as a further area of
20 acres, which, if obtained, will give the company
an area of about 90 acres, extending over country
which, according to the reports of Messrs. W. J. Wright,
A. H. Merrin, M.C.E., and R. H. Hamilton, obtained by
Mr. Miller, and which may be seen at the company’s
office, has been proved to be highly auriferous, with every
facility for being profitably worked if sufficient capital be
expended for providing the battery and other necessary
plant.
On the Booth’s Reward two shafts have been sunk, No.
Ito a depth of 140 ft., disclosing a large ore bed, from
which 254 tons have been taken, yielding an average of
Boz. per ton. Three hundred tons more of mixed ore and
mullock have been raised, and lie on the surface, giving
an average assay value of loz. 9dwt. 16gr. per ton. No. 2
is down to 105 ft., and is connected at this depth with No.
1 shaft. At the mouth of this shaft there are about 700
tons, showing 15dwt. sgr. per ton. On the Mt. Morgan
property a large amount of prospecting work has been
done by sinking numerous small shafts, and cutting
trenches and costeens, from nearly all of which good dish
prospects can be obtained. It is estimated that there are
on this property fully one and a quarter million tons of
crushing stuff, which could he put, through the battery at
a very small cost for a return of not less than fldwt. per
ton.
Steps are being taken in Adelaide to re-
establish the medical school on a decent basis.
Both the profession and Premier Kingston have
come to their senses, and are joining in the
aiithem~-“ Don’t shoot, Colonel, I’ll come down.”
The struggle over the validity of ex-
Treasurer Ward’s re-election to M.L. Parliament
is becoming an interesting complication. The
M.L. Act says that “ the seat of any member
shall become vacant ... if he is a bankrupt,”
&c. Ward is a bankrupt, and his seat became
vacant. Then he was re-elected, and as he still is
a bankrupt it certainly seems that his seat again
becomes vacant. Until he obtains his certificate,
m fact, it appears that every seat he gets becomes
vacant again as soon as he gets it, and re-electing
him is only pouring Ward through a sieve to no
purpose. Ward’s friends try to read the Act that
he only vacates his seat when he becomes a bank-
rupt, and that having, become, one and resigned
the matter ends there, but the letter of the
law is against them. Meanwhile the struggle to
keep the discredited Joseph in Parliament is
doing the Seddon Government no end of harm.
A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
Apply Sulpholine Lotion. It drives away

Premier Nelson, of Queensland, advances
it as his tired opinion that the selection of the
Federal capital must not be left to the Federal
Parliament, but must be done by the Convention.
In that case so much the less hope of having any
Federal Parliament. The chance of getting the
Federal capital in “our” province is one of the
big prizes in the Federal gamble, and the average
person doesn’t take tickets in a sweep if the
big prize is allotted in advance and the other fellow
has got it. Nelson also wants the financial
question settled by the Convention, and the sur-
plus allotted among the provinces. The probable
result would be that the only provinces which
would want to go in at all would be the one which
secured the capital and the one which reckoned it
had got ahead of the others in the rush for the
surplus.

Herald : „„„
RETURN OF THE MOUNTED RIFLEMEN.
THEIR DOINGS IN ENGLAND.
The Mounted Riflemen return to-day. . . . They
have witnessed tho power of that wonderful sentiment
which the late Henry Parkes felicitously termed the
“ crimson thread of kinship.” . . . The very horses
which they will ride cannot fail to remind us that they
are similar to those which were sold by the members of
the contingent in England at an average over £4O a head.
Never mind about the “crimson thread;” let us
give praise that the horses were sold.
Melb. : “Duffy, touching on his slight at-
tempts to practise at the Bar, reminds us how
evanescent is fame in that walk. Fellows towered
so by head and shoulders over all that he is per-
force remembered while Madden, Purves, Holroyd,
Williams, and so’ on are in the land of the living.
Banco made all Fellows’ celebrity—the tip-top line,
of course ; but at nisi prius he was nowhere beside
Ireland, Aspinall, Michie, Dawson, Wood, Mart-
ley, and even others. Michie is only a survivor,
but Dennistoun Wood is a sturdy relic of brilliancy
in all but the first rank. The blarney of Billing
may preserve his memory somewhat as a fly in
amber, while verdict-getters like Sewell and Chap-
man have sunk in oblivion. Looking for some-
thing else, I have just turned up Michie’s speech
of 40 years ago in George Milner Stephen v. the
Argus, covering a page. What fire and power !
What pace! Duffy, Parliamentary colleague of
both, says Michie equalled Disraeli.”
Ed. Bulletin,— lll old Parramatta ceme-
tery last month I found a dilapidated, square,
sky-facing tomb with a most interesting inscrip-
tion, which I subjoin. Time and weather are
gradually rendering the writing illegible, but it
would not cost much, if the work is done at once,
to give the memorial another half-century’s in-
telligent existence. —-C. R Wilton, Adelaide,
Oct. 10.—
SACRED
to the memory of
AUGUSTUS THEODORE HENRY ALT
who died Jan. 9, 1815, aged 84 years
late Surveyor-General of New South Wales at the first
settling of this colony, which situation he held till
superannuated.
He served as Lieut, in the Guards in George the Second’s
Reign.
Was aide-de-camp to Prince Ferdinand at the battle of
Minden and Capt. in the Royal Manchester Volunteers at
the siege of Gibralter under General Elliott where he
distinguished himself in a most gallant manner.
He died universally regretted by all his friends, who lost
in the Baron a most Complcat Gentleman and a man who
never tolc an Untruth to the injury of another man.
This Monument is erected by his nephew
MATHEW BOWLES ALT
Lieut, in his Majesty's Royal Navy as a Tribute of
Gratitude to His Respected Uncle.
A Boon to Sufferers. —A well-known stock-
dealer and storekeeper announces that, if written
to confidentially, he will reply and state the plan
he pursued by which he was restored to health
and vigor after years of suffering from Nervous
and Physical Weakness, Loss of Vigor, etc. He
writes : “I have no scheme to extort money from
anyone whomsoever. I was victimised by quacks
and empirics until I lost faith in mankind; but,
thank heaven, I am now well, vigorous, and
strong, and anxious to make this certain means of
cure known to all. Having nothing to sell, I
want no money.” Address —J. KILIAN, Store-
keeper, Daveyston, S.A. #

A CASE IN WHICH SEVEN
SPECIALISTS FAILED.
NINE MONTHS’ AGONY IN THE MEL-
BOURNE HOSPITAL.
OR. WILLIAMS’ PINK PILLS FOR PALE
PEOPLE TO THE RESCUE.
(From the Melbourne Advocate.)
Dave Kirk, the well-known and highly-popular
hero of the cycle track, was, on the Btn October of
last year, training in the Scotch College Grounds,
Melbourne, when, all of a sudden, and without
any warning, the tire of the leader broke, and
becoming at once entangled in the wheel, brought
the rider to the ground with terrific force. So
great was the impact that not a single limb or
member of the unfortunate cyclist’s frame escaped
serious injury. In all haste the victim was re-
moved to the Melbourne hospital, more dead than
alive, and quite unconscious from shock and
hemorrhage. A hasty examination of the
patient by the medical staff of that institution
showed them that the case was a most serious one,
and the grave looks of the surgeons in attendance
plainly indicated that they entertained but the
slightest hopes of the unfortunate young man’s
Doctor
recovery. AH that the resources of that fully-
plenished hospital could command were brought
into play for the relief of the patient, but the
gravest fears were felt as to his ultimate safety.
The external injuries were not, after all, the most
serious—the symptoms of serious internal injury
to the nervous system were all too patent to the
practised and expert eye of the medical faculty.
80 serious, indeed, was the case of Dave Kirk
considered, that an eminent surgeon who called to
see the patient declared it as his opinion that the
patient would, in all probability, never rise from
his sick bed. There poor Dave lay, betwixt life
and death, for about seven weary and exceedingly
trying months of pain and suffering, unable to
stir hand or foot, unable to move from side to side
—a victim of hemorrhage, spinal disease, pleurisy,
dysentery, complete paralysis of the body, and
last, but by no means least, locomotor ataxy, a
fearful complaint, which utterly prostrates the
human frame and destroys the power of the will;
so much so that the common functions of nature
are performed by a power absolutely extraneous to
the will. An idea of the dreadful injuries sus-
tained by the patient may be gathered from the
following details gleaned from Kirk by our repre-
sentative. His left heel had to be removed, and
32 stitches were inserted in that part of his
foot, 18 being placed in his right leg. The knee-
cap was smashed, and had to be lifted. Alto-
gether seven surgeons had to do with the sufferer,
Pale
who was reduced to a very weak and almost ooma-
tose state after nine operations had been performed
on him. Mortification set in in parts of the lower
limbs, and artificial means had to be employed to
draw off the urine. The system was washed in-
ternally four times every day with water and car-
bolic acid, the stomach being cleaned out by means
of a tube. Injections were also largely employed.
This was a most singular case, and one which
must have afforded ample play for the physician’s
skill and experiment, yet it remained for Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills to effect a radical and com-
plete cure.
THE BRIGHT SIDE OF THE PICTURE.
AN ASTOUNDING CHANGE.
After seven months of bed-ridden existence in the
Melbourne General Hospital, Dave Kirk left that
institution with faint hopes of ever being able to
get about without the aid of crutches, with all
hopes vanished as to his ability to again enter the
cycle arena and carry off once more those brilliant
laurels which were at various big wheel gatherings
wont to be his, for he was Victoria’s largest scratch
crack.
In May last Kirk’s friends, being impressed by
the numerous authentic and spontaneous testi-
monials from persons of both sexes, of every age
and condition of life, and in all parts of the world,
recommended a course of the now world-famed Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People as a last re-
source. Marvellous, but true, as may be learnt
from the patient’s own lips, no sooner had he begun
to take these wonderfully curative pills than
Kirk experienced palpable relief. To let him de-.
scribe himself the returning powers to his limbs
and nervous system generally : —“ Gradually the
sense of numbness began to disappear, and the
extremities acquired by degrees their wonted
healthy glow and warmth. While under treat-
ment in the hospital the sense of feeling entirely
disappeared, so much so that when the surgeons
would put a needle into any part of my body I
was absolutely insensible to the fact. To be sure
of the absence of the sense of touch, the doctors
would conceal my vision, and then question me as
to the part they were piercing ; but my answers
clearly proved to them that I was absolutely bereft
of all sensibility !
“ After a month’s trial of Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills I was able to throw aside my crutches. But
one of the most powerful effects of the pills was
the restoration of functional health. Bowel
troubles had been a terrible trouble to me ever
since the day I met with the accident. Regu-
larity in this direction was perfectly set up, and I
am now, after four months taking of these pills, in
the enjoyment of perfect health.” It wanted no
assurance of this, as those who had seen the sub-
ject of this narrative are not slow to express their
Burprise at the picture of health and almost com-
pletely resuscitated power of body and mind pre-
sented in the person of David Kirk.
But perhaps the most demonstrative proof of
the absolute numbness and absence of all feeling
in the patient while under treatment in the hos-
pital was the fact that, although he received a
shock from a powerful galvanic battery —a shock
so strong that the operator declared it to be half a
volt more than he had given to anyone in the in-
stitution for 40 years—Dave Kirk was utterly un-
able to feel its effect!
A PRACTICAL ILLUSTRATION OF
RESTORED VITALITY.
A few days ago Dave Kirk, who, by the way, is
to be found at his hairdresser’s and tobacconist’s
establishment, 471 Elizabeth-street north, caught
sight of a certain individual to whom, it seems,
he had let out one of his bikes on hire and
who is said to have been possessed of so bad a
memory that he forgot to return the machine to its
owner! As showing the restored strength and
vigor of the erstwhile bed-ridden man, Kirk made
a dash, and a hand-to-hand struggle ensued. His
opponent was a powerfully-built fellow, and a much
heavier weight than Dave. Blood flowed freely
on both sides, but Dave proved one too many for
his man, and with a dexterous effort managed, un-
assisted to get him into a cab, and took him uncere-
moniously off to lay a charge against him. That
was, perhaps, the mostpracticalevidenceof restored
vitality that could well be given on the part of
the cycle champion. Naturally enough, the thou-
sand and one chums of the once-thought dead, but
now very much alive, cyclist are loud in their
praises of the world-famed Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills, and whenever Dave goes out he is beseiged
with congratulations on his regained health. In
common gratitude, the athlete never fails to give
the whole credit of his recovery to that wonderful
remedy discovered by Dr. Williams. Moreover,
Williams
Kirk is able to trench a large garden at home, and
walk two miles daily to his business place.
EXTRACT FROM THE MELBOURNE
“ARGUS.”
“ Everyone has heard of Kirk, the crack racer.
About a month ago he met with an accident
whilst training, and has been in the Melbourne
Hospital ever since. The medical authorities say
that he will never ride again, as his spine has
been so badly hurt that he will likely be paralysed
for life.”
EXTRACT FROM THE “HERALD.”
“ Dave Kirk, residing at the Coffee Palace,
Victoria-street, North Melbourne, sustained
severe injuries through falling from his bicycle
while training on the track at the Scotch College
ground. The tyre of his bicycle burst, and Kirk
was thrown violently to the ground. He was re-
moved in a cab to the Melbourne Hospital.”
The latter extract appeared in the Herald on
the afternoon of the accident, October 9, 1896.
The former appeared in the Argus about a month
later, after the physicians and authorities had
placed him under treatment, and discovered that
his injuries had resulted in paralysis, spinal disease,
and locomotor ataxia, and that he was entirely
beyond their aid. His case was accordingly, by
the physicians who examined him, pronounced in-
People.
curable, and they told poor Kirk that he would
never rise from his bed again.
Pale and Sallow Faces.
DIZZINESS, SHORTNESS OE BREATH.
HEART PALPITATION, Etc.
This world is full of suffering and unhappiness.
In every walk of life may be seen young girls,
pale, sallow, and nerveless, the victims of troubles
peculiar to their sex, from which they suffer
m silence, and with the mistaken notion that there
is no help for them. Their lives are made miser-
able by headaches, dizziness, palpitation of the
heart, shortness of breath on the slightest exer-
tion, an indisposition for either work or pleasure,
and frequently a feeling that life is but a dreary
burden scarcely worth preserving. To all such we
say take hope ; there is a cure for your trouble,
and it is within your power to be bright-eyed,
rosy-cheeked, and happv. Dr Williams’ PINK
PILLS for PALE PEOPLE will restore your
lost color, correct irregularities, and bring baok
health and happiness. In proof of this assertion
read the following testimony from those who have
suffered and found renewed health through this
marvellous medicine: —
Miss Alma Millar,of Upper Southampton, ISI. 13.,
says : “ I scarcely know when my illness began, as
my mother was unable to work, and most of the
duties of a large household devolved upon me, so
that I felt that I must keep up, but I kept getting
worse and worse. My appetite failed, my com-
plexion became sallow, and my eyes sunken. I
was troubled with dizziness, shortness of breath,
and palpitation of the heart, until I would almost
suffocate. I was also troubled with a terrible pain
in the side. I could not go upstairs without rest-
ing, and was so afflicted with headache that my
life became almost unbearable. At last I was
forced to give up and keep my bed. My friends
feared I was going into consumption, but recom-
mended one remedy after another, which, how-
ever, did not help me. Finally I was induced to
try Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and in less than
three weeks I was able to leave my bed, and after
using the pills a few weeks longer, I feel that I
am as well as ever I was. My appetite has re-
turned as well, and my strength and general
health are in every way restored. I feel that in
bringing this subject before the public I am only
doing justice to suffering humanity, and hope that
all afflicted as I was will give Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills a fair trial. ”
Sciatica—A Complete Recovery.
Mr. Ernest Buckhold now resides at 226 George-
street, Sydney. He is 51 years of age. Mr.
Buckhold has not always lived ashore; for 32
years he followed “a life on the ocean wave.” He
has had many trying experiences, and to face the
dread terrors of the deep on more than one occa-
sion. The story of his miraculous escape from
death is one full of interest, particularly to sufferers
from sciatic rheumatism. Here is his statement:
—“ I arrived in Sydney in May, 1896, from Japan,
suffering severely from pains in the right leg. The
trouble increased daily to an acute stage. I as-
cribe the malady to exposure and wet feet. The
pains were first in the hip, and occasionally shot
to the calf and the foot. I had no peace or rest
night or day. I could not sit down to a meal, but
had to lean heavily against the table. The pain
took away all desire for food, and I could not
Pink
sleep. I had to leave the ship, and take to bed at
Ashfield. I got worse, and was bed-ridden, and
in great agony for three months. My hip grew
out, and the joint bone in the back protruded. I
endeavored to get about the house with the
assistance of two sticks. But it was a great
struggle to move, and caused excruciating pain.
I could not bear to put the foot to the floor. Two
months afterwards, in September, I had to take
to bed again, and for 70 nights morphia was in-
jected. I remained in bed till the 31st December
last. In January of this year, the E. and A. S.S.
Company granted me a passage to Japan.—This
was done with the hope that the change would
effect a cure. Five days after leaving Sydney the
full fury of the malady returned. In fact, it was
worse than ever. I was unable to go to the table
for food, and would rather go without a meal than
walk a short distance. The perspiration would pour
out of me with the pain, which doubled me up. The
slightest movement meant indescribable torture.
Yet, there was no cessation from suffering while
I kept quiet. When I arrived in Japan, I thought
to take a position at Kobe, but I found that the
incessant pain incapacitated me for any work. I
was compelled to return by the Australian, and
arrived in Sydney on the 13th May last, a physical
wreck and a bag of bones, and with undiminished
pain. Two days later, I purchased some Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People. This was a
critical moment, and I despaired of recovery. I
Precious.
was like a vessel suddenly taken aback ; every-
thing seemed against me. I followed the direc-
tions for taking Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and
after using three boxes I felt much improved. I
continued with the fourth, with happy results. I
had four pills left from tjpe sixth box when I had
become perfectly strong, healthy, and robust. I
can now walk any distance without fatigue, and
suffer no pain whatever. My appetite is keen.
The recovery was gradual at first, and then rapid
towards absolute cure. I began the pills on the
15th May, and put the unswallowed four aside on
the 14th June. I have taken none since, for mv
health has been thoroughly re-established. I
arranged with Captain Legg, of the s.s. Thornley,
to go as chief steward as far as Adelaide, to as-
certain how I would get along on a sea voyage.
Well, the work was hard and the weather bad,
and my feet and clothing were often wet, but I
fared first-class and suffered no ill-effects. I re-
turned to Sydney on the sth July by the A.U.S.N.
Company’s steamer Waroonga in perfect health
and strength. I now feel fit for any work.”
“ Yes, I passed through twelve months of un-
speakable suffering, which I am sure would have
been averted had I known of the extraordinary
efficacy of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People. However, I trust my experience may
bring hope to the hearts of others suffering from
sciatica. ”
An Enervated System Revived.
(From the Cart/i'ON Gazette, Melbourne.)
Mr. A. Bayers, of 57 Adam-street, South Yarra,
is, perhaps, one of the best known mining men of
Victoria. It would be a difficult thing, in fact, to
6T
find any mining
centre where this
gentleman has not,
with more or less
success, assisted in
the development of
Victoria’s golden
treasures. At Bal-
larat, Sandhurst,
Maldon, etc., his
name and person are
familiar. While
awaiting fresh op-
portunities, and at
the same time re-
cuperating there
is nothing so wear-
ing on the system
as a life on ’Change
—he has been so successful in the later respect
that one of our representatives, visiting him on
business, could not help remarking it.
“I feel better now than I have for many years
past. The last two months my whole system has
been retimed—wound up to concert pitch, as it
were,” was the reply.
“ You suffered severely ? ”
“Yes, but not so much physically as from an
entire absence of tone in the system. I could
transact my business fairly well, but the continual
excitement of over three decades of mining life —
prospecting, promoting, speculating, and Exchange
life—had told on me of late years. The Bystem
had run down ; that was about it.”
“ Why not have visited a medical man and
secured a tonic ? ”
“ I did ; several doctors agreeing that it was a
tonic I required. But they failed to supply that
much-needed mental and, also, physical bracer.
They supplied plenty of them, both in quantity
and variety, but with no appreciable benefit —the
least business-effort bringing about a recurrence of
the all-overish feeling.”
“ What do you attribute the change to?”
“ Well, a barrister-friend of mine, a great suf-
ferer from paralysis, mentioned to me the immense
benefit he had experienced from a course of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People, and, some
two months since, I invested in a couple of
boxes.”
“ How did they pan out ? ”
“ Much better than some of my mining specs.—
like pure gold. A few boxes strung me up pro-
perly, my appetite improved, and work (before a
heavy task) became a pleasure.”
“ Taking them still ? ”
“ No. A few boxes of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
did for me what pounds’ sterling worth of doctors
stuff failed to accomplish.”
“ You are perfectly satisfied of the merits of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for purifying the blood and
as a nerve tonic ? ”
“ Certainly. For retoning a system run down
by overwork or mental anxiety, they cannot be
beaten, and I lose no opportunity of so informing
my fjiends.”

Spirit
Business, Bobbery, etc.
A strict observer of Jewish religious cere-
monies, feasts and fasts is a money-lender who returned
to Australia in time to take advantage of the Day of
Atonement—and, coincidentally, to cater for the many
co-religionists who depend on him to finance them during
the racing-boom. His strength amongst the borrowing-
division of the sporting community is largely due to his
having both backers and layers under his thumb. Backer
A has lost, say, £lOO to Bookie B on Saturday, and cannot
face Monday’s settlements. Both are clients of the
financier’s. He lends Backer A the £lOO for the week for,
say, £lOO. The £lOO lent to A is repaid to the lender by
Bookie B the same day in settlement of perhaps £5O,
with which the money-lender had started the bookie’s
“ bag ” two days before. The next Wednesday perhaps
the Bookie B gets hit, and has to borrow from the
financier at high interest enough to pay the Backer A,
who in turn repays the usurer’s loan to him (A) of the
previous Monday, and so it goes on. A good game !
Mein Vrendt, make your schildrens shtudy de compound
inderest table !
* * * *
On the lower parts of the stone-walls of a re-
constructed Southern bank are sculptured a number of
open-mouthed lions’ heads. About 30ft. above each of
these hungry-looking carvings are human heads with
most mournful expressions. The sculptors, who did the
work 20 years ago, had rare prophetic instinct, and
apparently intended the animals’ heads to depict the
bank’s directors of to-day, but present facts justify a
little more misery in the expression of the faces of the
shareholders evidently represented above.

very rich—why, a search-warrant would be got and a
police constable would execute it. That means gossip
and all sorts of unpleasantness. As it is, the provision is,
if necessary, enforced without any fuss, and nobody’s the
wiser. So you see we adopt this measure just out of
respect for the men’s own feelings."
* * • •
Bendigo Miners’ Association carried a motion
that no action be taken with regard to the Lucknow
strike. Thereupon an agent of the co., who was seeking
labor in the district, apparently reckoning that he had
fallen among pals, sent in a request that he might be
permitted to address a meeting of members which had
been called for a Saturday evening. The committee
invited the agent to talk to itself, but barred the general
meeting against him And apparently he pleaded so
well that at the general meeting the members of the com-
mittee got the old motion rescinded, and, further, carried
a motion authorising a ballot as to whether £lOO should
be sent to the strikers ! Likewise, the Broken Hill miners
have relented, and promise help.
* * * *
Gold-stealing is said to be rampant at Kal-
goorlie, on suspicion of which a well-known mining
manager has just dismissed men from a local bat-
tery. The plates running from two battery-boxes were
almost barren, while the others were covered with
heavy amalgam, and the whole battery was crushing
from the same mine—Horseshoe. A mighty hard thing
to stop gold-stealing in mines where there is much to
steal. The Bulletin’s remedy might be tried : Let the
onus rest with all miners to account for any gold found in
their possession, and not with managers to prove that it
came from any particular mine. Miners working for
wages should be content to rub along without gold in any
form but sovereigns.
* * * *
Last year Lansell, by far the biggest holder in
the Great Southern mine, Bendigo, offered to bond it to
Mactear, the South African cyanide and mining expert,
who, however, was not on at the price. The mine had
struck the new run of gold, but was not opened enough
to see the extent. It has since paid in divs. about double
the money then asked for the mine.
* * * *
In Melb. police force are men who lend money
in small sums at heavy interest by the month to their
comrades. Apart from the extra money numbers of the
foorce earn as tailors, cobblers, &c., not a few' draw rents
from property, equalling, if not exceeding, the amount of
their pay ; and two, at least, are said to make small books
among their comrades on the leading Vic. racing events.
More than one Melbourne police official has substantial
interests in ponv-racing stables.
• * * *
A Maoriland brewer, who had settled in Mel-
bourne, told a Bulletin man that since he had come to
the smellful city he had learnt to brew a beer 30 per cent,
cheaper than ever he had believed it possible. “ But,
then,” he added, “ they’ve got a population in Melbourne
who never knew good beer.”
* * * »
Many people in Melbourne know who the
potentate is who won’t cut his loss on tobacco, but he
goes one better than hanging on to the hopeless 11-years-
stored weed. He has on hand over 40 gross of common
spectacles, costing 10s. 6d. a dozen, which he brought to
Melbourne when he landed here in 1849 ! They are now
worth 18s. 6d. a gross. He imported printing plants in
’6B which cost him £12,000 at the time, but are now
worth perhaps £6OO. Rather than sell at a penny loss, he
is actually trying to sell both stocks at cost in London,
where the plant would scarcely fetch the price of old
iron, and the specs, would not realise the value of the
metal rims. Clearly a bad spec. !
* * * *
The man who doesn’t take or give tips is finding
it phenomenally hard to do business in N.S.W. contract-
ing circles since certain English firms spread tentacles
around. Some high officials, controlling big contracts,
could tell queer stories of the way they have been
“ approached and some won’t, for they have met the
approacher half-way. There is an odour about one or
two recent transactions “strong as Death and deep as
Hell.”
* • * *
An amusing thing lately took place before Kal-
goorlie (YV.A.) Licensing Bench. There were many appli-
cations for publicans’ and provisional licenses—all opposed
by a solicitor on behalf of the L.V. Association. It was
pointed out by solicitors for applicants that the actual
names of those opposing should be stated. The Bench
concurred, and the names of most of the leading local
publicans, with the addition of “ and others,” were given.
Now, several holders of wine-licenses had joined the pub-
licans in forming this association, framed (in part) to keep
up a monopoly, and one of these wine-men was now apply-
ing for a publican’s license. He confidingly imagined
that, since he belonged to the guild, his application
would, anyhow, not be opposed, especially as several of
the members had signed the advt. affirming him to be “ a
person of good repute,” &c. When the solicitor read out
the names of his opponents, however, there appeared
amongst them several who had signed his advt., and he
found himself in the novel position of having uncon-
sciously joined his friends in feeing a lawyer to oppose
his own application ! The kind “ friends,” of course, had
not expected that their names would have to be dis-
closed in court; and the fat in the fire has been frizzling
ever since.
* * * *
Howe, one of the Con ventioners, and chairman
of B.H. Junction directors, was much disgusted when,
after travelling up to Newcastle, he was met at the Cockle
Creek (Ashcroft sulphide process) works with the intima-
tion that visitors weren’t allowed over the works except
on production of an order from the London board 1
This lias lately been the experience of several people.
Certainly Howe is a Junction man j and the Camden
syndicate, which has a big say in that mine, has also a
big say in a rival works.
There must be money in the glass-trade when it
pays men to go round the mullock-heaps and corporation
tips, turn them over with pick and shovel, and cart away
broken glass, as they are now doing round Melb.
* * * *
Something is said in newspapers every day re
the operations of the Bull exploration cos. who have
mines and representatives in Australia ; now, the Ex-
ploration Co. of Australia is in process of incubation. It
is to have a very large operating capital, and its own
examiners, consisting of several of the best-known Aus-
tralian mining men. The founders have been prominently
connected with the formation of similar organizations else-
where, and the fixed determination seems to be to keep
good mines and the money made out of them in the
country, and to exercise much more than ordinary judg-
ment and discrimination in selection. It has adopted the
name of the boss mining body of the world—the Explora-
tion Co.—which has missed many a good mine but never,
or hardly ever, got hold of a bad one, and can reckon its
profits by millions. That co. has operated successfully in
Australia.
• * • •
A rumor which keeps rising periodically from
nowhere in particular concerns a possible gold rush in
China. Probably it will come by-and-bye. China is
notoriously a rich mineral country, and a big gold-find is
more than possible as the while man eats his way further
and further into the interior. As likely as not it will be a
very big find—and then there will be a rush which will
trample Joss underfoot, and bang Confucius endways,
and knock spots off Buddha. The one thing which would
shake up the great mummified empire and blow the dust
of fifty centuries off it would be a gold scurry like that of
early California.
*- • * *
The rush of stony-brokers, who wish to avoid
the terrors of the new (Vic.) Insolvency Act (which comes
into force Ist Jan.) has not yet set in. A little more pub-
licity as to the stringent provisions of the Act would cause
an unprecedented rush to the Insolvency Court before
the end of this year. Gentry with doubtful settlements
on their wives are hereby specially urged to hurry up.
“ Now is the time of grace. Now is the day of salvation.”
Never to occur again.
• * * *
Coffee-growing, according to a Ceylon expert,
should simply boom in Northern N.S.W. The few trees
he met with have been producing more than five times
the average Ceylon yield, at which rate he estimates a
return (with 1200 trees) of £2OO per acre.
* * * *
Johannesburg butchers are erecting cold-storage
chambers, and contracting to supply dealers with as
many thousand carcases of imported mutton as they can
take' at an all-round rate of 5Jd. per lb.
* * * *
A bad item for Queensland planters. In Java a
disease has appeared that attacks the roots of the cane.
The roots rot and the canes die. Cause unknown ;no
known remedy ; the planters are helpless. At all events,
this will relieve the monotony of the tick question.
* * * *
A great cattle-artist’s game in Queensland is to
take out a miner’s right, and, on the strength of that,
graze cattle on large areas of proclaimed goldfield, thus
evading the payment of rent. A determined effort is,
therefore, to be made in the Assembly, under the new
Land Bill, to have goldfields areas placed under the juris-
diction of divisional boards. But how will this clash with
mining legislation ?
Bunny, the miner’s friend! Farmers and
squatters in Cassilis district, Gippsland, are terrified to
find the rabbit dropping in and making himself at home
at last. Hitherto the greater part of Gippsland has been
immune. Prospectors, however, hail bunny as a good
friend, and, considering the monotony of ’possum as a
steady diet, this is not to be wondered at. Besides,
bunny’s burrow proffers hints and indications that often
save the experienced prospector much slogging.
An English exchange avers that Premier
Turner started to learn bike-riding at a time when
he had a severe cold, and that every time he
sneezed he fell off. Which explains why the
bikists’ ranks don’t include a certain Right Honor-
able.
The Anglican Bishop of Christchurch
(M.L.) is credited by an English paper with
having made a sewing machine for his wife, and
with being clock-mender-in-ordinary to the settlers
in his diocese.

no* to do it ” again !
The climate of Western Queensland is “a
caution ”at times. The other day at Longreach
(Centralia) the thermometer ran up gaily to 105°
in the shade. Then a hot wind-storm came along
and skinned the iron off some of the principal
buildings. And, shortly after, the night-tempera-
ture dropped down to 45° at a jump. And all this
occurred at the fag-end of Sept.

substituted for Peel and Twiss and Herries, would
