The Orthodox.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To my—unduly biassed—view,
Some folks I know
Seem somewhat quaint, no doubt ’tis true
To them I’m so.
They shine in every gracious deed
(If not in looks),
And every evening they read
Improving books.
lo mix the Bible they are prone
With all their views ;
And spend much cash to make them known
To the Hindoos.
Much sympathy they always find
For folks o’ersea ;
It grieves them much to see how blind
Is the Chinee.
The story-books they read evoke
No thoughts of guile;
And if they ever make a joke
They do not smile.
In all their ways they take much care
As if they had
A notion that—like eggs—they were
Turned quickly bad.
They gravely preach that Kingdom Come
Most men must miss ;
They think the hard-won bliss of some
The sum of bliss.
The soul set free from out its dust
They seek to guide ;
It seems as though they cannot trust
The Other Side.
. And yet they’re happier far than I,
For as they go
They trouble not'of Whence or Why,
But know they know.
If right or wrong, they spend their day
Sans care or doubt;
And, if they’re wrong, it’s likely they
Won’t find it out.
Unpatriotic, they despise
Our sun-swept strand,
And seek, beyond our star-strewn skies,
A Better Land.
And that is why our folks, they grieve,
Are lost to grace :
Australians cannot well believe
In such a place.
They tell me sadly that I may
Not meet them there;
But then, such is my reckless way,
I do not care.
Sydney.
ZEF.
A Lady at Shingle Hut.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Miss Ribbone had just arrived.
She was the mistress of the local school, and had
come to board with, us a month. The respective
parents of the score of youngsters attending the
school had arranged to accommodate her, month
about, and it was our turn. And didn’t mother
just load us up how we were to behave—particu-
larly Joe.
* * * «
Dad lumbered in the usual log for the five,
and we all helped him throw it on—all except the
schoolmistress. She would have injured her long,
miserable, putty-looking fingers ! ' Then we sat
down to supper—that old familiar repast, hot meat
and pumpkin.
Somehow we didn’t feel quite at home ; but
Dad got on well. He talked away learnedly to
Miss Ribbone about everything. Told her, with-
out swearing once, how, when at school in the old
country, lie fought the school master and leathered
him well. A pure lie, but an old favorite of Dad’s,
and one that never failed to make Joe laugh. He
laughed now. And such a laugh ! —a loud, mirth-
less, merciless noise. No one else joined in
though Miss Ribbone smiled a little. When Joe
recovered he held out his plate.
“ More pumpkin, Dad.”
If wnat, sir ’! ” Dad was prompting him in
manners.
“If?” and Joe laughed again. “Who said
‘ if ’ ?—I never. ”
Just then Miss Ribbone sprang to her feet,
knocking over the box she had been sitting on,
and stood for a time as though she had seen a
ghost. We stared at her. “Oh,” she murmured
at last, “it was the dog! It gave me such a
fright ! ”
Mother sympathised with her and seated her
again, and Dad fixed his eye on Joe.
“Didn’t I tell you, he said, “to keep that use
less damned mongrel of a dog outside the house
altogether—eh ’—didn’t I ? Go this moment and
tie the brute up, you vagabond ! ”
“ I did tie him.up, but be chewed the green-
hide.’’
“Be off with you, you ” (Dad coughed sud-
denly and scattered fragments of meat and
munched pumpkin about the table) “ at once, and
do as I tell you, you ”
“That’ll do, father—that’ll do,” mother said,
gently, and Joe took Stump out to the barn
and kicked him, and hit him against the corn-
sheller, and threatened to put him through it if
he didn’t stop squealing.
He was a small dog—a dog that was always on
the watch—for meat; a shrewd, intelligent beast
that never barked at anyone until he got inside
and well under the bed. Anyway, he had taken a
fancy to Miss Ribbone’s stocking, which had
fallen down while he was lying under the table,
and commenced to worry it. Then he discovered
she had a calf, and started to eat that. She didn’t
tell us though—she told Mrs. McPherson, who im-
parted the secret to mother. I suppose Stump
didn’t understand stockings, because neither
mother nor Sal ever wore any, except to a picnic
or somebody’s funeral; and that was very seldom.
The Creek wasn’t much of a place for sport.
* * * *
“I hope as you’ll be comfortable, my dear.”
mother observed as she showed the young lady
the back-room where she was to sleep. ‘ “ It ain’t
s’ nice as we should like to have it f’ y’; we hadn’t
enough spare bags to line it all with, but the
cracks is pretty well stuffed up with husks an’
one thing an’ ’nother, and I don’t think you'll
hnd any wind kin get in. Here’s a bear-skin f’
your feet, an IVe nailed a bag up so no one kin
see min the morning. S’ now, 1 think you’ll be
pretty snug.
The schoolmistress bast a distressed look at the
wa ( .ving bag-door and said,
“ Th-h-ank you—very much.”
\V hat a voice ! I’ve heard kittens that hadn’t
their eyes open make a fiercer noise ; but then, I
suppose, a voice like the last wheeze of “the
dying poet is a sign of brains and breeding in a
young lady.
* * » *
Mother must have put all the. blessed
blankets in the house on the school-teacher’s bed.
1 don’t know what she had on her own, but we
only had the old bag-quilt and 'a stack of old
skirts, and other remnants of the family wardrobe,
on ours. In the middle of the night, the whole
confounded pile of them rolled off, and we nearly
froze. I).i what we boys would—tie ourselves in
knots and coil into each other like ropes—we
couldn t get warm. We sat Tip in the bed in turns,
anil glared into the darkness towards the school-
mistress’s room, which wasn’t more than three
yards away; then we would lie back again
and shiver. We were having a time. But, at last,
we heard a noise from the young lady’s room.
We listened all we knew. Miss Ribbone was up
and dressing. We could hear her teeth chattering
and her knees knocking together. But, then, we
heard her sneak back to bed again and feit disap-
pointed and colder than ever, for we had hoped
she was getting up early, and wouldn’t want the
bed any longer that night. Then we, too, crawled
out and dressed and tried it that way.
* * * *
In answer to mother at breakfast, next
morning, Miss Ribbone said she had “slept very
well indeed.”
We didn’t say anything.
She wasn t much of an eater. School-teachers
am t, as a rule. They pick, and paw, and fiddle
round a meal in a way that gives a healthy-
appetited person the'jim-jams. She didn’t touch
the fried pumpkin. And the way she sat there at
the table in her watch-chain and ribbons made
poor old Dave, who sat opposite her in a ragged
shirt without a shirt-button, feel quite miserable
and awkward.
For a whole week she didn’t take anything but
bread and tea though there was always plenty
good pumpkin, and all that. Mother used to
speak to Dad about it, and wonder if she ate the
litule pumpkin-tarts she put up for her lunch.
Dad couldn’t understand anyone not eating pump-
kin, and said that he'd tackle grass before he’d
starve.
“And did every’see such a object?” mother
went on. “ The hands an’ arms on her! Dear
me ! why, I do believe if our Sal was to give her
one squeeze she’d kill her. Oh, but the finery and
clothes ! Y’ never see the like ! Just look at her ! ”
And Dad, the great oaf, with Joe at his heels
followed her into the young lady’s bedroom. Man ’l
—will you ever understand Eve? Will she for
ever fool you and make of you even a greater
jackass than God Almighty originally framed you ?
.But I forgive Dad ; he needed variety.
Look at that !” —pointing to a couple of
dresses hanging on a nail—“she wears them on
week-days, no less; and here (raising the lid
of a trunk and exposing a pile of clean and
neatly - folded clothing that might have been
anything, and drawing the articles forth one
by one). Look at them ! There’s that —and that
—and this—and ”
“ 1 say, what’s this, mother? ” interrupted Joe,
holding up something he had discovered.
“ And that—an’ ”■
“ Mother ! ”
“ And this
“Heigh, mother?”
“Don’t; bother me, boy, it’s her tooth-brush,”
and mother pitched the clothes back into the trunk
and glared round. Meanwhile Joe was hard at
his teeth with the brush.
"’Oh, here,” and she dived at the bed and
drew a night-gown from beneath the pillow, un-
folded it, and held it up by the neck for inspec-
tion.
Dad, with his huge, ungainly, hairy paws
behind him, stood mute, like the great pitiful
elephant he was, and looked at the tucks and the
rest stupidly. “ Where before did y’ever see
such tucks and frills and lace on a night shirt?
Why, you’d think ’twere for goin’ to picnics in,
stead o’ goin to bed with. Hero too, here’s a
pair of brand-new stays, besides the ones she’s
on her back. Clothes ! —she's nothin’ else but
clothes. ”
Then they came out, and .foe began to spit and
said he thought there must have been something
on that brush.
Miss Ribbone didn’t stay the full month—she
loft at the end of the socond week ; and mother
often used to wonder afterwards why the creature,
never came to see us.
STEELE RUDD.
It is a stock-story down at Fiji how John
Hennings and Silas Page, two Levuka magnates,
once made a journey in an open boat to Suva—CO
miles at least. They were both in the provision
trade, and each had relied on the other to bring
the provender for the trip. When tiffin-time
came each gravely produced a bottle of pickles—
that s all.
It is told of a Melbourne suburban girl,
unwed and encumbered with a baby (quite a little
Ol } e > by the way) that she interviewed a lawyer
with a view to obtaining maintenance. “And
who is the father ? ” she was asked, and the simple
creature answered: “Well, I’m not sure, but
we’ll take the case to North Melbourne—Dr.
Lloyd’ll know.” Every woman in quest of 7s. 6d.
a week likes to appear before Dr. Lloyd.

which has been made in Germany must be numbered the

ing beer-bibber who chooses to forsake his fatherland, and
claim our too catholic hospitality.

learned toady, Max Muller, is writing in Cos-
mopolis about “ Royalties” he has known.
